Ruling on Expressions of Lineage Denial in Qadhf: Anger vs Contentment
Differentiates between saying 'you are not your father's son' in anger versus contentment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between absolute and conditional qadhf rulings for expressions denying lineage and apply the anger vs contentment distinction.

Core Idea
Denying lineage absolutely implies qadhf of the mother, regardless of anger; specifying a father requires anger for hadd.

In this lesson, we examine a nuanced area of qadhf: verbal expressions that deny a person's lineage. The Hanafi school carefully distinguishes between absolute denials (like 'you are not from your father') and specific denials (like 'you are not the son of [named father]'). The key variable is the context of anger, which signals an intent to insult rather than a gentle reproach. This distinction, rooted in al-Hidayah, shapes whether the hadd punishment for qadhf is applied.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of lineage denial
The passage presents two scenarios: absolute denial without specifying a father, and denial with a specific named father. Understand the difference.
Absolute denial targets lineage itself; specific denial targets relationship to a named individual.
2
Determine the ruling for absolute denial
According to the first issue in al-Hidayah, absolute denial is qadhf regardless of anger or contentment. Why? Because it necessarily implies the mother committed zina, as lineage is only denied from an adulterer.
The mother must be free and Muslim for hadd to apply.
3
Determine the ruling for specific denial
According to the second issue, denial with a specific father is qadhf only if said in anger; otherwise it is considered a reproach (mu'ataba) and not subject to hadd.
In contentment, the phrase is understood as a constructive criticism of the person's character, not a literal accusation.
4
Analyze the attempted harmonization
Some later scholars tried to unify the two issues by making anger a condition for absolute denial as well. The text criticizes this harmonization, citing al-Kafi and a report from Ibn Mas'ud that the absolute view is stronger.
The harmonization is considered weak because it contradicts the plain meaning of the first issue.
5
State the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that absolute denial remains qadhf unconditionally, as supported by authoritative sources like al-Hidayah and al-Kafi.
This is the view of the Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فلو قال لغيره لست لأبيك أو لست بابن فلان في غضب حد وفي غيره لا) أي وإن قال له ذلك في حالة الرضا فلا حد لأنه عند الغضب يراد به حقيقته سباً له وفي غيره يراد به المعاتبة بنفي مشابهته له في أسباب المروءة. ثم اعلم أنه قد وقع في الهداية مسألتان : الأولى قال : ومن نفى نسب غيره وقال لست لأبيك فإنه يحد، وهذا إذا كانت أمة مسلمة حرة لأنه في الحقيقة قذف لأمه لأن النسب إنما ينفى عن الزاني لا عن غيره. الثانية قال لغيره في غضب لست بابن فلان لأبيه الذي يدعى له يحد ولو قال في غير غضب لا يحد وعلله بما ذكرناه، فظاهره أنهما مسألتان مختلفتان صورة وحكماً لأن في المسألة الأولى قد نفاه أبيه من غير تعرض للأب الذي يدعى إليه، وحكمها وجوب عن الحد مطلقاً . سوءا كان في غضب أو رضا، لأنه لم يفصل. وفي المسألة الثانية قد نفاه عن أبيه المعين الذي يدعى إليه وحكمها التفصيل، وقد حمل بعضهم المسألة الأولى على التفصيل في الثانية وهو أنه إن كان في حالة الغضب حد لا في غيره. وجزم به في غاية البيان ولم يتعقبه في فتح القدير وهو لما صرح به في الكافي للحاكم الشهيد بقوله : وإن قال لرجل يا ولد الزنا أو يابن الزنا أو لست لأبيك وأمه حرة مسلمة فعليه الحد، بلغنا عن عبد الله بن مسعود رضي الله عنه أنه بعيد
His statement: (If he said to another 'you are not from your father' or 'you are not the son of so-and-so' in anger, he is given hadd; otherwise, no) — that is, if he said it in a state of contentment, there is no hadd, because in anger it is meant as a real insult, while in other cases it is meant as a reproach by denying resemblance in reasons of manliness. Then know that in al-Hidaya there are two issues: The first: He said: Whoever denies the lineage of another and says 'you are not from your father', he is subject to hadd, and this is if the mother is a free Muslim, because in reality it is qadhf of his mother, since lineage is only denied from an adulterer, not from anyone else. The second: He said to another in anger 'you are not the son of so-and-so' referring to his claimed father — he is subject to hadd; if he said it not in anger, he is not subject to hadd, and he explained it with what we mentioned. So it appears that they are two different issues in form and ruling, because in the first issue he denied him from his father without specifying the claimed father, and its ruling is absolute obligation of hadd whether in anger or contentment, because he did not differentiate. In the second issue he denied him from a specific claimed father, and its ruling is detailed. Some scholars have carried the first issue over to the detailed ruling of the second, i.e., if in anger, hadd; otherwise, no. This is affirmed in Ghayat al-Bayan and not refuted in Fath al-Qadir, because al-Hakim al-Shahid stated in al-Kafi: If he said to a man 'O child of zina' or 'O son of zina' or 'you are not from your father' and his mother is a free Muslim, then he is subject to hadd. It has reached us from Abdullah ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him) that it is far-fetched.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on when verbal expressions implying qadhf (like denying lineage) incur hadd. The key distinction is intent: if said in anger, it is taken as a literal insult and constitutes qadhf; if said in contentment, it is seen as a reproach and not qadhf. The author then compares two issues from Al-Hidayah: (1) saying 'you are not from your father' without specifying the claimed father — this is absolutely qadhf because it implies the mother committed zina, regardless of anger. (2) Saying 'you are not the son of so-and-so' with a specific father — only in anger is it qadhf. Some scholars harmonize the first with the second, making both dependent on anger. The text criticizes this harmonization, citing al-Kafi and a narration from Ibn Mas'ud that such expressions are clearly qadhf and subject to hadd without qualification.

Key Terms
نفي النسب (nafy al-nasab): Denying a person's lineage, implying his mother committed zina.
المعاتبة (al-mu'ataba): Gentle reproach or rebuke, not meant as a literal accusation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if someone says to another 'you are not from your father' without specifying the father, in a state of contentment?
A) He is subject to hadd because it is an absolute qadhf of the mother
B) He is not subject to hadd because contentment negates the intent to insult
C) He is subject to ta'zir only
D) The ruling depends on whether the mother is a Muslim or not

Key Takeaway
Absolute denial of lineage is qadhf of the mother and incurs hadd regardless of anger; specific denial requires anger to be considered qadhf.

The Ruling on Saying 'You Are Not Your Father's Son' – Anger vs. Contentment in Qadhf
Stating the ruling and its qualification based on the speaker's state

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the phrase 'lesta li-abeek' and distinguish between cases said in anger and contentment, and apply the condition of intent to determine if hadd is due.

Core Idea
Saying 'not your father's son' is qadhf only if said in anger; context proves intent.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi distinction regarding the phrase 'lesta li-abeek' (you are not your father's son). While such words might seem to accuse the mother of zina, the Hanafi school considers the speaker's state—anger or contentment—to be decisive. In anger, the literal slander is intended, incurring the hadd of qadhf; in contentment, it is understood as a rebuke for lacking manly qualities. This nuanced ruling highlights the importance of context in interpreting verbal offenses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is saying 'lesta li-abeek' to another. The ruling depends on whether it is said in anger or contentment. If in anger, hadd is wajib; if in contentment, no hadd.
2
State the Hanafi position
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that hadd is wajib only if said in anger; otherwise it is not qadhf.
3
Locate the dalil
The passage cites practice from 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud and the reasoning that context indicates intent. The phrase in anger is intended as slander; in contentment it is a rebuke.
The dalil is a qawl sahabi (statement of a companion).
4
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some narrations from Abu Yusuf hold that hadd is due regardless of context, as the words literally deny paternity implying zina.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The majority (Abu Hanifa and later authorities like al-Kafi, al-Hidayah) adopt the anger distinction. The commentator in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq confirms this as the relied-upon position.
The thamarah al-khilaf is that in practice, hadd is applied only when the statement is proven to be said in anger.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلو قال لغيره لست لأبيك أو لست بابن فلان في غضب حد وفي غيره لا كنفيه عن جده الذي قضى على الرجل بالرجم قال : القياس أن يحد قاذفها لأن القاضي إنما قضى عليه لا عليها لكني أستحسن أن لا أحد قاذفها ثم قال : وكما يزول الإحصان بالزنا من كل وجه يزول بالزنا من وجه، فكل وطء حرم لعدم ملك المتعة من وجه فهو زنا من كل وجه وذلك كوطء الأجنبية، وكل وطء حرم مع قيام ملك المتعة من كل وجه لعارض كوطء المرأة في حالة الحيض لا يزول به الإحصان. وإذا وطئ أمته المجوسية لا يزول إحصانه لقيام ملك المتعة من كل وجه ولو اشترى أمة وطئها أبوه أو وطئ هو أمها ووطئها فقذفه إنسان فلا حد على القاذف بالإجماع، وكذا لو اشترى أخته من الرضاعة ووطئها سقط إحصانه لأن الحرمة هنا ثابتة على سبيل التأبيد بخلاف ما تقدم. ولو اشترى أمة لمس أمها أو بنتها بشهوة أو نظر إلى فرج أمها أو بنتها بشهوة أو نظر أبوه أو ابنه إلى فرجها بشهوة ووطئها قال أبو حنيفة : لا يزول إحصانه ويحد قاذفه :وقالا : يزول إحصانه ولا يحد قاذفه وكذلك على الاختلاف إذا تزوج امرأة بهذه الصفة ووطئها ا هـ . وجعل في الخانية من وطئ بنكاح فاسد كمن وطئ الجارية المشتركة في عدم وجوب الحد على القاذف. والحاصل أن من زنى أو وطئ بشبهة أو بنكاح فاسد في عمره أو وطئ من هي محرمة عليه على التأبيد سقط إحصانه ومالا فلا؛ كذا في شرح الطحاوي. قوله : (فلو قال لغيره لست لأبيك أو لست بابن فلان في غضب حد وفي غيره لا) أي وإن قال له ذلك في حالة الرضا فلا حد لأنه عند الغضب يراد به حقيقته سباً له وفي غيره يراد به المعاتبة بنفي مشابهته له في أسباب المروءة. ثم اعلم أنه قد وقع في الهداية مسألتان : الأولى قال : ومن نفى نسب غيره وقال لست لأبيك فإنه يحد، وهذا إذا كانت أمة مسلمة حرة لأنه في الحقيقة قذف لأمه لأن النسب إنما ينفى عن الزاني لا عن غيره. الثانية قال لغيره في غضب لست بابن فلان لأبيه الذي يدعى له يحد ولو قال في غير غضب لا يحد وعلله بما ذكرناه، فظاهره أنهما مسألتان مختلفتان صورة وحكماً لأن في المسألة الأولى قد نفاه أبيه من غير تعرض للأب الذي يدعى إليه، وحكمها وجوب عن الحد مطلقاً . سوءا كان في غضب أو رضا، لأنه لم يفصل. وفي المسألة الثانية قد نفاه عن أبيه المعين الذي يدعى إليه وحكمها التفصيل، وقد حمل بعضهم المسألة الأولى على التفصيل في الثانية وهو أنه إن كان في حالة الغضب حد لا في غيره. وجزم به في غاية البيان ولم يتعقبه في فتح القدير وهو لما صرح به في الكافي للحاكم الشهيد بقوله : وإن قال لرجل يا ولد الزنا أو يابن الزنا أو لست لأبيك وأمه حرة مسلمة فعليه الحد، بلغنا عن عبد الله بن مسعود رضي الله عنه أنه بعيد
If he said to another, 'You are not your father's son' or 'You are not the son of so-and-so' in anger, then there is a hadd; otherwise not. [...] He said: 'If he said to a man, 'O child of adultery' or 'O son of adultery' or 'You are not your father's son' and his mother is a free Muslim woman, then he is subject to the hadd.' It was conveyed to us from 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud, may Allah be pleased with him, that he was distant.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn ruling regarding the phrase 'you are not your father's son' (lesta li-abeek). The author (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) states that if such a statement is made in anger, the hadd for qadhf is imposed; if not in anger, there is no hadd. The commentator (al-Bahr al-Ra'iq) elaborates on this, explaining that in anger the literal meaning of slander is intended, while in contentment it is taken as a rebuke for lacking manly qualities. He then discusses a difference of opinion within the Hanafi school: Abu Yusuf (according to some reports) considered the statement a qadhf in anger but not in contentment, while many later scholars adopt the qualification by anger as the relied-upon position. The author also mentions an incident from 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud and a ruling from al-Kafi that equates 'you are not your father's son' with explicit slander like 'O child of adultery,' provided the mother is free and Muslim. The commentary notes that the hadd is due to the implicit accusation of the mother's zina.

Key Terms
قذف (qadhf): False accusation of zina (adultery or fornication) against a muhsan person, incurring hadd punishment.
حد (hadd): Fixed corporal punishment prescribed by shari'ah for specific crimes like qadhf (80 lashes).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does saying 'You are not your father's son' (lesta li-abeek) incur the hadd of qadhf according to the relied-upon Hanafi position?
A) Always, regardless of context.
B) Only if the mother is a free Muslim woman.
C) Only if said in anger.
D) Only if said in contentment.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the phrase 'lesta li-abeek' is not automatic qadhf; the speaker's state of anger is the decisive factor.

When Insults Become Qadhf: Hanafi Rules on Slanderous Phrases
Listing phrases and their qadhf status with reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between insults that incur the hadd of qadhf and those that do not, applying the Hanafi conditions of muhsanah and explicit accusation.

Core Idea
Only direct or implicit accusations of zina against a muhsanah incur hadd; mere lineage insults do not.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq enumerates various insults and determines whether they constitute the crime of qadhf (false accusation of unchastity). The Hanafi school strictly limits hadd to explicit or implicit accusations of zina against a muhsanah. General insults like 'Nabati' (non-Arab) or 'son of rain-water' do not qualify, while denying a man from his known father in anger does. Let us walk through each phrase and its ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling for generic insults
Locate the phrases 'Nabati' and 'Ibn Ma' al-Sama' and note that they do not incur hadd. Why? Because they do not accuse the mother of zina.
These are mere insults, not qadhf.
2
Examine denial from grandfather
The text states that denying a man from his grandfather is not qadhf, as it is truthful (the grandfather is not necessarily the father) and does not imply zina of the mother.
Truthful denial cannot be qadhf.
3
Analyze denial from father with anger condition
The phrase 'lesta li-abeek' (you are not your father's son) is qadhf only if said in anger. If said in jest or as a query, it is not. This is the mufta bihi position, though Abu Yusuf differed.
Context of anger is key.
4
Check the condition of muhsanah
For hadd to apply, the mother must be muhsanah (free, Muslim, chaste). The commentator emphasizes this, noting that apparent conditions in Al-Hidayah (freedom and Islam) do not exclude other requirements.
The victim must be muhsanah.
5
Apply the ruling to similar phrases
The author extends the same detailed ruling to saying 'You are the son of so-and-so' for someone other than his known father. The same anger distinction applies.
Consistency in application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (كنفيه عن جده وقوله لعربي يا نبطي أو يابن ماء السماء ونسبته إلى عمه أو خاله أو رابه ولو قال يا قال : لا حد إلا في قذف محصنة أو نفي رجل عن أبيه اهـ. لأنه سوی بین الألفاظ الثلاثة . وقد صرح في فتح القدير بأنه إذا قال يا ولد الزنا أو يابن الزنا لا يتأتى فيه تفصيل بل يحد ألبتة .اهـ. فكذلك إذا قال لست لأبيك لأنهم صرحوا أنه بمعنى أمك زانية أو زنت ولا يراد به المعاتبة حالة الرضا لأنه لم يعين أباً مخصوصاً حتى ينفى أن يكون على إطلاقه. ثم رأيت التصريح بذلك في فتاوى قاضيخان قال لرجل لست لأبيك عن أبي يوسف أنه قذف كان ذلك في غضب أو رضا، ولو قال ليس هذا أباك لأبيه المعروف فإن كان هذا في حالة الرضا أو على وجه الاستفهام لا يكون قذفاً، وإن كان في غضب أو على وجه التعيير كان قذفاً اهـ. وما في فتح القدير من أن التقدير حالة الرضا لست لأبيك المشهور مجازاً عن نفي المشابهة في محاسن الأخلاق فبعيد كما لا يخفى، وقد علم مما ذكرناه أنه لا بد من تقييد المختصر بأن تكون أمه محصنة لأنه قذف لها وما في الهداية من التقييد بحرية أمه وإسلامها لا ينفي اشتراط بقية شروط الإحصان ولذا اعترضه الشارحون. وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لو قال إنك ابن فلان لغير أبيه فالحكم كذلك من التفصيل . وقيد بالنفي عن أبيه فقط لأنه لو نفاه عن أبيه وأمه فلا حد في الأحوال كلها للكذب في الثاني ولأن فيه نفي الزنا لأن نفي الولادة نفي للوطء وللصدق في الأول لأن النسب ليس لأمه. ولم يتعرض المصنف لطلب الولد لأن الأم إن كانت حية فالطلب ،لها ،وإن كانت ميتة فالطلب لكل من يقع القدح في نسبه المخاطب وغيره سواء. وفي القنية : سمع أناس من أناس كثيرة أن فلاناً ولد فلان والفلان يجحد فلهم أن يشهدوا مطلقاً أن هذا ولده بمجرد السماع وإن لم يعلموا حقيقته، ولو قال واحد لهذا الولد ولد الزنا لا يحد اهـ . قوله : (كنفيه عن جده وقوله لعربي يا نبطي أو يابن ماء السماء ونسبته إلى حاله وعمه ورابه) أي لا يجب الحد في هذه المسائل. أما الأول وهو ما إذا نفاه عن جده فلأنه صادق في ليس قذفاً .
His statement: 'Like his denial of lineage from his grandfather, and his saying to an Arab, 'O Nabati' or 'O son of rain-water,' and his attributing him to his paternal uncle or maternal uncle or his brother-in-law...' He said: 'No hadd except in the case of qadhf of a muhsanah or denial of a man from his father.' [...] He pointed out that if one says 'You are the son of so-and-so' for other than his father, the ruling is similarly detailed. [...]
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage enumerates various phrases and whether they constitute qadhf. The commentator explains that denying lineage from a grandfather or calling an Arab a 'Nabati' (i.e., non-Arab) or 'son of rain-water' (a metaphorical insult) does not incur hadd because these are not explicit accusations of zina. Only direct denial of paternity from one's known father (in anger) or explicit accusations like 'O child of adultery' qualify. He also discusses the condition that the mother must be muhsanah (free, Muslim, chaste) for the hadd to apply. There is a distinction between 'you are not your father's son' (general, ambiguous) and 'this is not your father' (specific, can be a question). Additionally, he covers the ruling when the denier mentions both mother and father: if he denies from both, no hadd because the falsehood cancels out. If the mother is alive, she can demand qadhf; if dead, the child or others affected can demand.

Key Terms
نبطي (Nabati): A term used to insult an Arab by calling him non-Arab; considered an insult but not qadhf.
ابن ماء السماء (Ibn Ma' al-Sama'): Literally 'son of rain-water'; a metaphorical insult meaning illegitimate child, but not considered qadhf because it is not an explicit accusation of zina.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which of the following statements incurs the hadd of qadhf?
A) You are a Nabati (non-Arab).
B) You are the son of rain-water.
C) You are not your father's son (said in anger).
D) Your lineage is not from your grandfather.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, only explicit or implicit accusations of zina against a muhsanah incur hadd; mere lineage insults do not.

Ruling on Attributing Someone to His Grandfather in Slander Cases
Ruling with reasoning: metaphorical usage averts hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that attributing someone to his grandfather does not incur the hadd punishment for slander (qadhf) and explain why metaphorical usage creates doubt that averts the penalty.

Core Idea
Attributing someone to his grandfather is metaphorical, so no hadd for slander.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in the law of qadhf: what if a person attributes another to his grandfather rather than his father? The Hanafi school rules that no hadd punishment is incurred because the term 'father' can be used metaphorically for 'grandfather'. This doubt (shubha) averts the fixed punishment. The passage also clarifies related expressions from al-Zahiriyyah to distinguish between slanderous and non-slanderous statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the default ruling
The act is attributing a person to his grandfather (e.g., 'you are the son of your grandfather') instead of his father. Normally, denying someone's lineage to his father would be slander. What is the default ruling here?
2
Recognize the exception due to metaphorical usage
The text states that if the attribution is to the grandfather, no hadd is imposed because 'father' can be used metaphorically for 'grandfather'. This possibility of metaphorical interpretation creates a doubt (shubha) that prevents the application of the fixed punishment.
Hanafi usul: doubt averts hadd.
3
Examine the examples from al-Zahiriyyah
The passage quotes various phrases: 'You are not from the offspring of so-and-so' is slander; 'You are not from the birth of so-and-so' is not slander; 'You have no father' etc. are slander of the mother; 'You are not of legitimate birth' is slander. Understand each distinction.
Key distinction: denying birth (wiladah) is not slander, but denying lineage or paternity is.
4
Apply the principle: shubha averts hadd
The underlying principle is that hadd is averted when there is any plausible interpretation that could make the statement true. Here, 'grandfather' may be called 'father' metaphorically, so the statement is not a clear false accusation of adultery.
This is consistent with Quran 2:229 and hadith 'Avert the hadd by doubts'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله، وأشار إلى أنه لو نسبه إلى جده لا يحد أيضاً لأنه قد ينسب إليه مجازاً. وفي الظهيرية : إذا قال لست من ولد فلان فهذا قذف ولو قال لست من ولادة فلان فهذا ليس بقذف وإذا قال لغيره لست لأب لست لأبيك لم يلدك أبوك فهذا كله قذف لأمه، وكذا إذا قال لست للرشدة ا هـ
His statement: And he indicated that if he attributed him to his grandfather, he is not subject to hadd likewise, because he may be attributed to him metaphorically. And in al-Zahiriyyah: If he says 'You are not from the offspring of so-and-so,' this is slander. And if he says 'You are not from the birth of so-and-so,' this is not slander. And if he says to another 'You have no father,' 'You have no father,' 'Your father did not give you birth,' all of this is slander of his mother. Likewise if he says 'You are not of legitimate birth.' End quote.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with reasoning. The author comments on the matn's indication that if a person attributes another to his grandfather (e.g., 'you are the son of so-and-so' meaning the grandfather), no hadd punishment for slander is imposed because the term 'father' can be used metaphorically for grandfather. Then he quotes al-Zahiriyyah with examples: saying 'you are not from the offspring of so-and-so' is slander of the mother, while 'you are not from the birth of so-and-so' is not slander because it denies birth but not lineage. Saying 'you have no father' or 'your father did not give you birth' clearly implies the mother's adultery, so it is slandering the mother. Similarly, denying legitimate birth is slander.

Key Terms
حد (hadd): The fixed corporal punishment prescribed by Shariah for certain crimes, here for false accusation of adultery (qadhf).
قذف (qadhf): Slander; accusing a married person of adultery or denying his lineage without legal proof.
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical usage of a word, as when 'father' is used for 'grandfather'.
ولد (walad): Offspring; in the phrase 'lasta min waladi fulan' it means 'you are not from his offspring', implying denial of lineage.
ولادة (wiladah): Birth; in the phrase 'lasta min wiladati fulan' it means 'you are not from his birth', which is not slander because it does not deny lineage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) 'You are not from the offspring of so-and-so.'
B) 'You are not from the birth of so-and-so.'
C) 'You have no father.'
D) 'Your father did not give you birth.'

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules that attributing someone to his grandfather does not incur hadd because the term 'father' can be used metaphorically for 'grandfather', creating doubt that averts the punishment.

When an Insult Is Not Slander: The Case of 'Nabati'
Explains that calling an Arab 'Nabati' is not slander because it is metaphorical for character

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between an insult and slander (qadhf) by applying the Hanafi criterion that qadhf requires an explicit imputation of adultery.

Core Idea
An insult is not slander unless it accuses of adultery.

The Hanafi school strictly limits the fixed punishment of slander (hadd al-qadhf) to cases where a person explicitly accuses another of adultery. Insults based on ethnicity, character, or appearance, even if offensive, do not trigger the hadd penalty, though they may be subject to discretionary punishment (ta'zir). This lesson examines a classical example from the Hanafi tradition: calling an Arab person 'Nabati' (a non-Arab).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the apparent ruling
What is the act? Calling an Arab 'Nabati'. What is the ruling in the text? No hadd (fixed punishment) applies.
2
Understand the rationale (talil)
Why is there no hadd? Because the term is used metaphorically for character, not to deny lineage or accuse of adultery. The text says it is intended as a simile in character (تشبيه في الأخلاق).
This is the key principle: qadhf requires direct imputation of zina.
3
Examine the linguistic context
The word 'Nabati' originally refers to non-Arab inhabitants of Iraq. It is used as an insult implying lack of eloquence. The passage cites lexical sources to confirm this usage.
Knowing the literal meaning helps to see it is not an accusation of adultery.
4
Apply the criterion to other similar statements
The text mentions another example: saying 'You are not of the Arabs.' This is also not qadhf. Generalize: any statement that does not explicitly accuse of zina is not qadhf, even if insulting.
This step helps the student generalize the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما عدمه فيما إذا قال العربي يا نبطي فلأنه يراد به التشبيه في الأخلاق لما قلنا، وفسره الفقيه أبو الليث برجل من غير الفصاحة، وكذا إذا قال لست عدم العرب. وفي المغرب : النبط جيل من الناس بسواد العراق الواحد نبطي. وعن ثعلب عن ابن الأعرابي : رجل نباطي ولا تقل نبطي .اهـ.
As for its absence in the case when an Arab says 'O Nabati,' because it is intended as a simile in character, as we said. And al-Faqih Abu al-Layth interpreted it as a man lacking eloquence. Likewise if he says 'You are not of the Arabs.' And in al-Mughrib: 'Nabat' are a people from the Sawad of Iraq; one [singular] is 'Nabati.' And from Tha'lab from Ibn al-A'rabi: 'Nabati' (with the long vowel), and do not say 'Nabti.' End quote.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is a ruling explaining why calling an Arab 'Nabati' does not incur hadd. The term 'Nabati' originally refers to non-Arab inhabitants of Iraq but is used metaphorically to describe someone with rough or unrefined character, not to accuse of illegitimate birth. Similarly, saying 'you are not of the Arabs' is not slander but an insult. The author cites lexical sources to confirm the linguistic usage.

Key Terms
نبطي (nabati): A non-Arab person from the Sawad of Iraq, often used derogatorily to imply lack of eloquence or refinement.
قذف (qadhf): The crime of falsely accusing a muhsan person of adultery or illegitimate birth, incurring a hadd punishment of 80 lashes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Calling someone 'Nabati' is not slander (qadhf) because it does not directly accuse of adultery; only ta'zir may apply.

Denying Tribal Affiliation: Not Slander but Possible Ta'zir
States that denying tribal affiliation is not slander but may incur ta'zir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on denying someone's tribal affiliation and explain why it does not constitute slander (qadhf).

Core Idea
Denying tribal membership is not slander; it carries no hadd but may incur discretionary ta'zir.

Many people assume that insulting a person's lineage is equivalent to slander (qadhf), which carries a fixed hadd punishment. However, Hanafi fiqh distinguishes between accusing someone of adultery or illegitimate birth and simply denying their tribal identity. This lesson examines a key ruling from the chapter on hudud, clarifying when a statement triggers a fixed penalty versus a discretionary punishment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act
The act under consideration is saying to someone 'You are not from the tribe of so-and-so.' Note that this does not explicitly accuse the person of adultery or illegitimate birth.
2
Assess Qadhf Conditions
Slander (qadhf) in Hanafi law requires an explicit or implicit accusation of fornication (zina) or illegitimate lineage. Denying tribal membership alone does not meet this standard.
Implicit accusations are those that necessarily imply zina, e.g., calling someone a 'bastard'.
3
Apply the Ruling of No Hadd
Because the condition for qadhf is not met, the speaker does not incur the fixed hadd punishment (80 lashes). The passage explicitly states 'fala hadd' (no hadd).
This is based on the principle that hudud are interpreted restrictively.
4
Consider Ta'zir Possibility
Although no hadd, the statement may still be harmful or insulting. The judge has discretion to impose ta'zir – a discretionary punishment such as lashing, imprisonment, or reprimand.
Ta'zir is applied when public order or personal dignity is affected.
5
Note the Hashimi Exception
The same ruling applies even if the target is a Hashimi (descendant of the Prophet). The text says 'wa khadha li-hashimi' – likewise for a Hashimi. No hadd, but ta'zir.
This clarifies that lineage status does not change the legal classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لو قال لست من بني فلان فلا حد، وكذا إذا قال لهاشمي لست بهاشمي لكنه يعزر كما في المبسوط .
And the author indicated that if he said 'You are not from the tribe of so-and-so,' then there is no hadd. Likewise if he said to a Hashimi 'You are not a Hashimi,' but he is given a discretionary punishment (ta'zir) as stated in al-Mabsut.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is a ruling that denying someone's tribal affiliation (e.g., 'you are not from the tribe of Banu so-and-so') does not constitute slander (qadhf) because it does not accuse of adultery. However, if the statement is insulting, the judge may impose a discretionary punishment (ta'zir). This is the Hanafi position as recorded in al-Mabsut.

Key Terms
تعزير (ta'zir): A discretionary punishment for offenses that do not have a fixed hadd penalty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Hadd of 80 lashes
B) No punishment
C) Ta'zir
D) Death penalty

Key Takeaway
Denying tribal affiliation is not qadhf and carries no fixed hadd, but may be subject to discretionary ta'zir if it causes harm.

The Ruling on Calling Someone 'Son of Water of the Sky'
Explains that calling someone 'son of water of the sky' is not slander due to its metaphorical meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on saying 'son of water of the sky' and explain why it is not slander.

Core Idea
A nickname that is clearly metaphorical praise cannot be slander.

In the chapter on hadd al-qadhf (slander), the jurists discuss what expressions constitute an accusation of adultery. One example is calling a man 'son of water of the sky.' This passage explains that such a phrase is not slander because it is understood as a metaphor for generosity and purity, not as an imputation of illegitimate birth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the expression and the legal question
What is the phrase? What is the potential ruling? Is it slander (qadhf) or not?
The phrase is 'yā ibna mā'i as-samā' (O son of water of the sky).
2
Analyse the linguistic meaning
Determine whether the phrase is literal or metaphorical. 'Water of the sky' means rain, a symbol of generosity. Thus the phrase is a metaphor.
Metaphors are not intended as factual statements about lineage.
3
Apply the rule of qadhf
Qadhf requires an explicit or implicit accusation of unlawful intercourse. A metaphor of praise does not meet this threshold.
The ruling hinges on the understood meaning, not the literal words.
4
Conclude the ruling
Since the phrase is clearly praise, it is not slander. No hadd penalty applies.
This is the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إذا قال لرجل يابن ماء السماء فلأنه يراد به التشبيه في الجود والسماحة والصفاء لأن ابن ماء السماء لقب به لصفائه وسخائه. وفي غاية البيان : ماء السماء هو عامر أبو مزيقيا وسمي به القحط أقام ماله مقام المطر وكان غياثاً لقومه مثل ماء السماء للأرض، وكانت أم المنذر بن امرئ القيس أيضاً ماء السماء لجمالها وحسنها. وإنما سمي عمر وولده مزيقيا لأنه كان يمزق كل يوم حلتين يلبسهما ويكره أن يعود فيهما ويكره أن يلبسهما غيره اهـ.
And if he says to a man 'O son of water of the sky,' because it is intended as a simile in generosity, magnanimity, and purity, since 'son of water of the sky' is a nickname given for his purity and generosity. And in Ghayat al-Bayan: 'Water of the sky' is 'Amir Abu Muzyqiya, and he was called by that due to drought; he made his wealth take the place of rain and was a succor to his people like water of the sky to the earth. And the mother of al-Mundhir ibn Imri' al-Qays was also called 'Water of the sky' for her beauty and goodliness. And 'Amr and his children were named Muzyqiya because he used to tear apart two garments every day to wear them and disliked wearing them again or giving them to others. End quote.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with linguistic explanation. The phrase 'son of water of the sky' (ibn ma' al-sama') is a nickname for a generous person, likening him to rain. It is not slander because it is intended as praise or metaphorical description, not an accusation of adultery. The author provides historical background from Ghayat al-Bayan to illustrate the meaning.

Key Terms
ماء السماء (ma' al-sama'): A nickname for a generous person, literally 'water of the sky' (rain), used metaphorically for generosity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Metaphorical 'Father' and the Aversion of Hadd in Slander
Ruling on slander when attributing to uncle, maternal uncle, or stepfather

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that attributing a person to their uncle, maternal uncle, or stepfather does not constitute slander, and explain the Quranic evidence for the metaphorical use of 'father' that creates doubt (shubha) and averts the hadd punishment.

Core Idea
Calling someone by the name of their uncle is not slander because 'father' is used metaphorically for uncles, creating doubt that averts hadd.

The law of slander (qadhf) is strict: accusing someone of illegitimate birth without four witnesses merits the hadd punishment. However, not every statement that could imply such an accusation triggers this penalty. This passage explains that when a person is called by the name of his paternal uncle, maternal uncle, or stepfather, it does not constitute slander. Why? Because in Arabic, these relatives are figuratively called 'father', and a metaphorical usage creates doubt (shubha) that blocks the hadd. The proof comes from the Quran itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
The act is attributing someone to a male relative who is not the biological father: paternal uncle, maternal uncle, or stepfather. The ruling is that this does not constitute slander (qadhf) and thus no hadd punishment applies.
The act is considered mubah (permissible) because no prohibition is attached.
2
Locate the Legal Basis: The Concept of Metaphor (Majaz)
The author reasons that each of these relatives is called a 'father' figuratively in Arabic. Therefore, such an attribution is not a clear assertion of illegitimacy; it could be interpreted as using a common metaphor. This ambiguity creates shubha (doubt), and in hudud, doubts avert the punishment.
This is a key principle in Hanafi fiqh: hudud are suspended by shubha.
3
Examine the Quranic Proof (Dalil)
The author cites Quran 2:133: 'And the God of your fathers, Abraham, Ishmael, and Isaac.' Ishmael was an uncle to Jacob (Israel), yet he is called a 'father' in the verse. This shows that the term 'father' is used metaphorically for paternal uncles in Arabic. Therefore, calling someone by the name of their uncle is not necessarily an accusation of illegitimate birth.
The proof is from the Quran, with qat'i thubut but zanni dalala (the metaphorical meaning is inferred, not explicit).
4
Apply the Ruling to the Specific Relatives
The author explicitly mentions paternal uncle, maternal uncle, and stepfather. For each, the same principle applies: they are customarily called 'father' in a broad sense, so the attribution does not amount to clear slander.
Stepfather is included because he is often called 'father' in a social sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إذا نسبه إلى عمه أو خاله أو زوج أمه فلأن كل واحد من هؤلاء يسمى أبا؛ أما الأول فلقوله تعالى وإله آبائك إبراهيم وإسماعيل وإسحق ﴾ [البقرة : ۱۳۳] فإسماعيل كان عماً له أي ليعقوب عدمه يراد المجازي.
And if he attributes him to his paternal uncle, maternal uncle, or stepfather, because each of these is called a 'father' – as for the first, due to His statement 'And the God of your fathers, Abraham, Ishmael, and Isaac' [2:133]; Ishmael was an uncle to him, i.e., to Jacob; its intended meaning is metaphorical.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with a Quranic proof. The author states that if someone attributes a person to his paternal uncle, maternal uncle, or stepfather, it does not constitute slander because each of these is figuratively called 'father' in Arabic. The Quranic verse 'the God of your fathers Ibrahim, Ishmael, and Ishaq' shows that Ishmael, the uncle of Ya'qub (Jacob), is called a 'father' in a metaphorical sense. Thus, such attribution is not a clear accusation of illegitimate birth.

Key Terms
أب (ab): Literally 'father', but used metaphorically for any male guardian or ancestor.
عم (amm): Paternal uncle.
خال (khal): Maternal uncle.
زوج أم (zawj umm): Stepfather (husband of mother).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

The Grandfather Case: When Denial of Lineage Does Not Incur Hadd
A detailed analysis of whether calling someone 'not the son of his grandfather' incurs hadd for slander, with a refined distinction from the father case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that calling someone 'not the son of his grandfather' does not incur hadd for slander, and distinguish it from the case of denying paternity to one's father.

Core Idea
Multiple possible meanings create doubt that averts hadd – the father case is clear slander, the grandfather case is not.

This passage delves into a subtle but important distinction in Hanafi hudud law. When someone insults another by denying his relationship to his grandfather, the phrase carries multiple meanings – literal, metaphorical, and legal ambiguities. Unlike a direct denial of paternity to one's father (which is clearly slander), the grandfather case introduces doubt (shubha) that prevents the application of the hadd penalty. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq refines this analysis by weighing conflicting indicators (qara'in).

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase and its possible meanings
The phrase 'You are not the son of so-and-so' when said about a grandfather has at least three meanings: metaphorical (not resembling him), literal (not created from his semen), and another literal (denying that he is a higher father, which itself has two sub-meanings). Understanding these is key to the analysis.
2
Contrast the father case with the grandfather case
In the father case, the same phrase has only one clear literal meaning: denial of paternity equals slander. In the grandfather case, multiple meanings exist, creating doubt about the intended meaning.
3
Analyze the conflicting indicators (qara'in)
The state of anger suggests an insulting literal meaning, but the fact that the speaker is a Muslim and the prohibition of slander suggest a metaphorical meaning. These indicators cancel out, leaving the literal meaning but with doubt.
4
Apply the shubha principle
In Hanafi law, any doubt in the evidence or in the act averts the hadd punishment. Here, doubt arises from the multiple possible meanings and the conflicting indicators, so no hadd is imposed.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi response to objections
Some claimed consensus that all such phrases incur hadd, but the author distinguishes: the consensus only applies to clear cases; here doubt prevents it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله لست بابن فلان لجده له معنى مجازي هو نفي مشابهته لجده ومعنيان حقيقي وهو نفي كونه مخلوقاً من مائة، وآخر هو نفي كونه أباً أعلى له وهو يصدق بصورتين : نفي كون أبيه خلق من مائة بل زنت جدته به أو جاءت به بشبهة وهذه المعاني يصح إرادة كل منها، وقد حكم بتعيين الغضب أحدها بعينه في الأول وهو كونه ليس مائه زنا الأم به إذ لا معنى لأن يخبره في السباب بأن أمه جاءت به بغير زنا بل بشبهة فيجب من كونها من مائة مع زنا الأم به، ومع مع أن يحكم أيضاً بتعيين الغضب في المعنى الثاني الذي هو نفي نسب أبيه عنه وقذف جدته به فإنه لا معنى لإخباره في حالة الغضب بأنك لم تخلق من ماء جدك وهو مع سماجته أبعد في الإرادة من يراد نفي أبوته لأبيه لأن هذا كقولنا السماء فوق الأرض ولا مخلص إلا بأن يكون فيها إجماع على نفي الحد بلا تفصيل كما أن في تلك إجماعاً على ثبوته بالتفصيل .اهـ. قلت: قد يجاب بالفرق وهو إرادة القذف في نفيه عن جده بالعدول عن الحقيقة إلى المجاز للقرينة وذلك شبهة يندرئ بها الحد لأن الأصل في الكلام الحقيقة وحال المسلم شاهدة بأنه أراد الحقيقة وأتى في حال الشتم بكلام يحتمل القذف فصارت حالته قرينة معارضة لقرينة إرادة الشتم بخلاف نفيه عن أبيه فإنه قذف حقيقة، وحالة الغضب قرينة أيضاً مساعدة للمعنى الحقيقي، وكون القذف محرماً قرينة على إرادة المعنى المجازي وهو كونه ليس مثل أبيه في الأخلاق فقد تعارضت القرينتان وهما حالة الغضب وحالة المسلم فتساقطتا وبقي المعنى الحقيقي سالماً عن المعارض وهو نفي كونه مخلوقاً من مائه
And his saying: 'You are not the son of so-and-so' regarding his grandfather has a metaphorical meaning, which is denying that he resembles his grandfather, and two literal meanings: one is denying that he was created from his water (semen), and another is denying that he is a higher father to him, and that applies to two scenarios: denying that his father was created from his water, but rather his grandmother committed adultery with him or came by him through a legal doubt. Each of these meanings is possible to intend. And it is judged that anger specifies one of them in particular in the first, namely that he is not his water, and the mother committed adultery with him, since there is no meaning to inform him in insult that his mother came with him without adultery but through a doubt. So it is necessary that the mother committed adultery with him. And also it is judged that anger specifies in the second meaning, which is denying his father's lineage from him and slandering his grandmother with him, because there is no meaning to inform him in anger that you were not created from your grandfather's water – which, despite its ugliness, is more remote from intention than denying his fatherhood to his father, because this is like our saying 'the sky is above the earth'. There is no escape except that there is consensus on denying hadd without detail, just as in that there is consensus on establishing it with detail. End quote. I say: It can be answered by the distinction, which is the intention of slander in his denial of his grandfather by turning away from the literal to the metaphorical due to the indicator, and that is a doubt that averts hadd because the default in speech is literal, and the state of a Muslim testifies that he intended the literal. And he came in the state of insult with words that carry the possibility of slander, so his state became a literal indicator opposing the indicator of intending insult. Unlike his denial of his father, which is slander literally, and the state of anger is also an indicator assisting the literal meaning. And the fact that slander is prohibited is an indicator of intending the metaphorical meaning, which is that he is not like his father in character. So the two indicators are conflicting – the state of anger and the state of the Muslim – and they cancel each other out, leaving the literal meaning safe from opposition, which is denying that he was created from his water.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is a detailed legal analysis (ta'miq) of a specific phrase when said to someone regarding his grandfather. The author unpacks multiple meanings: a metaphorical one (denying resemblance) and two literal ones (denying biological descent from grandfather, or denying that grandfather is an ancestor). In anger, the litigant likely intended a literal insult, but the presence of doubt (shubha) due to the possibility of metaphorical meaning and the conflicting indicators of anger and Muslim character leads to cancelling both indicators. Ultimately, the literal meaning remains, but the doubt averts the hadd. The author then adds his own response (qultu) distinguishing the case of denying fatherhood (where slander is clear) from denying grandfatherhood (where the doubt is stronger).

Key Terms
شبهة (shubha): A doubt or ambiguity that prevents the application of hadd punishment.
قرينة (qarina): An indicator or contextual evidence that helps determine the intended meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school consider the phrase 'You are not the son of your grandfather' as not incurring hadd, while the same phrase about one's father does incur hadd?
A) Because the grandfather is not a direct relative.
B) Because the phrase has multiple possible meanings when said about a grandfather, creating doubt.
C) Because the hadd is only for accusing the living.
D) Because grandfathers are not protected by qadhf laws.

Key Takeaway
When a phrase can be interpreted in multiple ways, including non-slanderous ones, the doubt (shubha) prevents the application of hadd for slander.

Slandering a Deceased Person: Who Can Demand Hadd?
Explains that slandering a deceased woman gives her relatives the right to demand hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which relatives can demand hadd for slandering a deceased person and explain the Hanafi reasoning based on disgrace.

Core Idea
The right to demand hadd for slandering a deceased person extends to all ascendants and descendants touched by the shame.

Slandering a deceased person (qadhf) still carries the hadd punishment in Islamic law. But who has the right to demand that punishment? The Hanafi school takes a broad view: anyone whose lineage is dishonored by the slander can demand hadd. This lesson unpacks the detailed ruling from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, including the scope of eligible relatives and a key intra-Hanafi dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is qadhf (slandering adultery) of a deceased person who was a muhsan. The ruling is that hadd (80 lashes) is wajib upon the slanderer. But the right to demand hadd depends on the claimant's relationship to the deceased.
The passage deals with the claimant, not the definition of qadhf.
2
List the eligible claimants
The text states that the father, son, and grandson (from the deceased woman) can demand hadd. It then generalizes: all ascendants (usul) and descendants (furu) who are affected by the disgrace can demand.
This includes grandparents, great-grandparents, children, grandchildren, etc., through both paternal and maternal lines.
3
Understand the reasoning (ta'lil)
The disgrace (al-ār) attaches to the relatives due to the partitive relationship (al-juz'iyyah). Slandering someone's mother implies dishonor to the entire lineage. Therefore, the slander is effectively directed at them as well.
This is the key Hanafi principle: the shame spreads to ascendants and descendants.
4
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani reportedly opposed extending the right to grandchildren through daughters (e.g., daughter's son) and to grandfathers beyond the paternal grandfather. But the relied-upon position (al-madhhab al-awwal) includes them, as the disgrace attaches.
The author sides with the broader view, citing that the disgrace is the operative cause.
5
Apply the rule to relatives not explicitly mentioned
The text clarifies that the grandmother (paternal) can demand (despite some opposite reports). Also, if slandering a deceased man, his mother can demand. The term 'walad' includes the daughter's son.
The ruling is inclusive: any ascendant or descendant who feels the dishonor can demand.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو قال يابن الزانية وأمه ميتة فطلب الوالد أو الولد أو ولده حد) لأنه قذف محصنة بعد موتها فلكل من يقع القدح في نسبه بقذفه له المطالبة وهم الأصول والفروع لأن العار يلتحق بهم لمكان الجزئية فيكون القذف متناولاً لهم معنى قيد بموتها لأنها لو كانت غائبة لم يكن لهم المطالبة لجواز أن تصدق القاذف إذا حضرت والتقييد بقذف الأم اتفاقي لأنه لو قذف رجلاً وهو ميت فلأصله أو فرعه المطالبة ، ولذا ذكر في شرح الطحاوي: ولو قذف وجب الحد على القاذف وللوالدين والمولودين أن يخاصموا سواء كان الولد أو الوالد أو لم يكن. والتقييد بالوالد اتفاقي أيضاً إذ الأم كذلك لما قدمناه من قوله «وللوالدين». فعلى هذا لو قذف ميتاً بالزنا وله أم فلها المطالبة لأنه يلحقها العار بذلك . وصرح الزيلعي بأن للأصول المطالبة وهو يقتضي أن للجد المطالبة وقد صرح في غاية البيان معزياً إلى شرح الجامع الصغير للفقيه أبي الليث بأن المراد الأب والجد وإن علا ويخالفه ما في فتاوى قاضيخان من أن الجد أب الأب لا يطالب به ولا أم الأم ولا الأخ ولا العم ولا العمة ولا مولاه؛ كذا في فتح القدير وهو سهو من القلم في النسخة التي نقل منها. والموجود في الفتاوى أن الجد أب الأم ليس له المطالبة وليس فيما ذكر الجد أبو الأب فالحق أن له المطالبة. وأفاد بالتعبير بـ«أو» أن للفرع المطالبة مع وجود أصله وأن لولد الولد المطالبة مع وجود الولد، وأنه إذا صدق القاذف بعضهم فللبعض الآخر المطالبة ، ولذا ذكر في الخانية أن رجلاً لو قذف ميتاً وله ابنان فصدقه أحدهما فللآخر أن يحده .اهـ. وكذا إذا عفا بعضهم فللآخر المطالبة ، وأطلق في الولد فشمل ولد البنت فله المطالبة بقذف جده، وروي عن محمد خلافه والمذهب الأول لأن الشين يلحقه
His statement: (And if he says 'O son of the prostitute' while his mother is dead, and the father, the son, or his son demands hadd) because it is slander of a married woman after her death. So everyone whose lineage is defamed by that slander has the right to demand hadd, and they are the ascendants and descendants because the shame attaches to them due to the partitive relationship, so the slander includes them by implication. The restriction [to the mother] is by coincidence because if he slandered a man while he is dead, his ascendant or descendant can demand hadd. Hence it is mentioned in Sharh al-Tahawi: 'If he slanders, hadd becomes obligatory on the slanderer, and the parents and children may litigate, whether the child [is present] or the parent or not.' The restriction to the parent is also coincidental, as the mother is also included, as we presented from the phrase 'and for the parents.' Accordingly, if he slanders a dead person with adultery and he has a mother, she can demand hadd because the shame attaches to her. Al-Zayla'i explicitly stated that ascendants have the right to demand, which implies that the grandfather has the right. And it is stated in Ghayat al-Bayan, attributed to Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir of Abu al-Layth, that what is meant is the father and grandfather, however high. This is contradicted by what is in Fatawa Qadikhan: the paternal grandfather does not demand hadd, nor the maternal grandmother, nor the brother, nor the paternal uncle, nor the maternal aunt, nor the freed slave. As such in Fath al-Qadir, and it is a pen slip in the copy from which it was taken. What is found in the Fatawa is that the maternal grandfather cannot demand hadd, and what is mentioned does not include the paternal grandfather, so the correct view is that he can demand. And by using the word 'or', he indicated that the descendant can demand even if the ascendant exists, and that the grandson can demand even if the son exists, and that if some of them believe the slanderer, the others can still demand hadd. Hence mentioned in al-Khaniyah: If a man slandered a dead person and he has two sons, and one of them confirms the slanderer, the other can still demand hadd. Likewise if some pardon, the other can demand. And the word 'son' is general, so it includes the daughter's son, so he can demand hadd for slander of his grandfather. It is narrated from Muhammad a contrary view, but the first school is the one [relied upon] because the disgrace attaches to him.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on slandering a deceased person. The author explains the matn's example: if someone says 'son of a prostitute' about a dead woman, her father, son, or grandson can demand the hadd punishment. The reasoning is that slandering a deceased married person is still qadhf, and the shame attaches to their ascendants and descendants. The text clarifies that this is not limited to the mother; it applies to any deceased person. The grandmother (paternal) can also demand, though there is a dispute: some sources deny her right, but the author sides with the view that she can. The relative pronouns in the matn are inclusive: one relative's pardon does not nullify the other's right. The term 'walad' includes grandchildren through daughters (i.e., the daughter's son can demand for slander of his maternal grandfather). There is a narrations from Imam Muhammad opposing this, but the relied-upon position (al-madhhab al-awwal) is that the disgrace attaches, so they have the right.

Key Terms
أصول (usul): Ascendants; parents, grandparents, etc. In the passage, includes both paternal and maternal ascendants except certain exceptions (maternal grandfather).
فروع (furu'): Descendants; children, grandchildren, etc. In the passage, includes children of both sons and daughters.
القذف (al-qadhf): Slander; falsely accusing someone of adultery or illegitimacy. The act triggers hadd.
العار (al-ār): Disgrace, shame. The legal cause for allowing relatives to demand hadd: the shame attaches to the lineage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a man slanders a deceased married woman, who can demand hadd?
A) Only her father
B) Only her son
C) Her father, son, or grandson
D) Only the state

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school grants the right to demand hadd for slandering a deceased person to all ascendants and descendants touched by the disgrace, not just immediate relatives.

Clarification of the Phrase 'You Are Not from the Tribe of So-and-So'
Clarifies the scope of the phrase 'bani fulan' in the context of qadhf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the legal significance of the phrase 'lastu min bani fulan' and identify whether it constitutes qadhf.

Core Idea
Saying 'You are not from the tribe of so-and-so' does not constitute slander because it refers to tribe, not lineage.

This short passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a subtle but important point in Hanafi fiqh regarding the law of qadhf (false accusation of illicit relations). The phrase 'You are not from the tribe of so-and-so' might appear to deny someone's lineage, but the text specifies that it refers to the tribe (qabilah), not to the individual's personal lineage. Therefore, it does not incur the hadd punishment for qadhf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act being ruled upon
What is the action? It is the utterance of the phrase 'You are not from the tribe of so-and-so'.
This is a verbal statement.
2
Determine the legal issue
Is this statement considered qadhf (slander) that warrants the hadd punishment? The answer depends on its interpretation.
Qadhf requires a false accusation of adultery or illegitimate birth.
3
Apply the clarification from the text
The text says 'ya'ni al-qabilah' — meaning the tribe. So the phrase refers to tribal affiliation, not biological lineage.
Thus, it is not an accusation of illegitimate birth.
4
Derive the ruling
Since the phrase is about tribal membership, it does not meet the definition of qadhf. Therefore, it is not subject to the hadd punishment.
This is a specific ruling from Al-Khaniyah, as referenced.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لو قال لست من بني (فلان) يعني القبيلة كما صرح به في الخانية قوله: (ولا الأم ولا الأخ) كذا
If he says 'You are not from the tribe of so-and-so,' meaning the tribe, as stated in al-Khaniyah. His statement: 'And not the mother nor the brother' etc.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This short passage is a clarifying note. The phrase 'lastu min bani fulan' (you are not from the tribe of so-and-so) refers specifically to the tribe (qabilah), not to an individual person. This distinction is important for the ruling that it is not slander. The reference to 'and not the mother nor the brother' appears to be a citation from another source, perhaps stating that these relatives also cannot claim hadd in certain contexts.

Key Terms
قبيلة (qabilah): Tribe; a larger social unit than family.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The immediate family
B) The tribe
C) The clan
D) The individual

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'lastu min bani fulan' refers to the tribe, not individual lineage, and thus does not constitute qadhf.

Demanding Hadd for Slandering a Deceased Muhsan
Explaining the ruling that descendants and ancestors can demand hadd for slander of a deceased muhsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on who can demand hadd for slander of a deceased muhsan and identify the conditions required for the hadd to be due.

Core Idea
A descendant or ancestor of a deceased muhsan can demand hadd for slander, even if they are a kafir or slave.

In this passage, the commentator of Kanz al-Daqa'iq expands on the ruling that when a deceased muhsan person is slandered, not only the direct descendants but also ancestors and more distant descendants have the right to demand the hadd penalty. He addresses a potential objection from al-Khaniyya and clarifies the condition that the deceased must be muhsan. The commentary also highlights an intra-Hanafi disagreement with Zufar, who restricts this right to free Muslim relatives. By the end, you will understand the scope of this right and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Main Ruling
Extract the core ruling from the author's explicit words: a grandchild (child's child) can demand hadd for slander of his grandfather. Note that this applies to all ancestors and descendants, regardless of how high or low.
The passage uses the example of a grandchild, but by extension it applies to all ascendants and descendants.
2
Understand the Condition of the Deceased
The commentator explains that for the hadd to be due, the deceased slandered person must be muhsan (free, adult, Muslim, chaste). This condition is implicit in the matn but is made explicit here.
If the deceased is not muhsan, the slander does not incur hadd, and no one can demand it.
3
Resolve the Apparent Contradiction from al-Khaniyya
The passage addresses a case from al-Khaniyya where saying 'Your grandfather is a fornicator' does not incur hadd. The resolution is that the slanderer might be referring to an unspecified grandfather, possibly a non-Muslim, so hadd is not automatic. He must specify a Muslim grandfather.
This shows the importance of specificity in slander accusations.
4
Analyze the Zufar Dispute
Zufar holds that a kafir or slave relative cannot demand hadd, arguing they lack eligibility. The majority Hanafi position (including Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn) disagrees, because the dishonor (shayn) affects the relative regardless of faith or free status.
The 'illa (effective cause) is the attribution of zina to a muhsan, which brings dishonor upon the relative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد أفاد صريح كلام المصنف أن لولد الولد المطالبة بقذف جده ولم يخالف في ذلك إلا زفر، ولا يخالفه ما في الخانية من أنه لو قال له جدك زان لا حد عليه لما علله في الظهيرية من أنه لا يدري أي جد هو. وأوضحه في فتح القدير بأن في أجداده من هو كافر فلا يكون قاذفاً ما لم يعين مسلماً بخلاف قوله أنت ابن ابن الزانية لأنه قاذف لجده الأدنى فإن كان أو كانت محصنة حد اهـ. وقد استفيد مما قدمه أنه لا بد أن يكون المقذوف ميتاً محصناً فلذا لم يقيد به هنا وأطلق في الطالب فشمل ما إذا كان غير محصن، فلو كان أصل المحصن الميت أو فرعه كافراً أو عبداً فله أن يطالب بالحد خلافاً لزفر لأنه من أهل الاستحقاق إذ الكفر أو الرق لا ينافيه وقد عيره بنسبة محصن إلى الزنا ما إذا قذفه ليس بمحصن فلا يلحقه العار، فلو قال المصنف ولو قذف ميتاً محصناً فلأصله وإن علا أو فرعه وإن سفل مطلقاً المطالبة لكان أولى.
The explicit meaning of the author's words indicates that the child's child can demand [hadd] for the slander of his grandfather, and none disagreed except Zufar. And what is in al-Khaniyya – that if someone says 'Your grandfather is a fornicator' there is no hadd – does not contradict him, because al-Zahiriyya explains it by saying he does not know which grandfather he means. And al-Fath al-Qadir clarified that among his grandfathers there may be a disbeliever, so he is not a slanderer unless he specifies a Muslim, unlike his saying 'You are the son of the son of the fornicatress' because he is slandering his immediate grandfather; if he or she was muhsan, then [hadd] is due. It has been understood from what has been presented that it is necessary for the slandered deceased to be muhsan; therefore it was not restricted here, and [the rule] was stated absolutely regarding the demander, so it includes when [the slandered] is not muhsan. So if the deceased muhsan's ancestor or descendant is a disbeliever or a slave, he can demand the hadd, contrary to Zufar, because he is among those eligible; disbelief or slavery does not contradict that, as he has been dishonored by the attribution of a muhsan to zina. But if the one slandered is not muhsan, no dishonor attaches to him. So if the author had said: 'and if he slanders a deceased muhsan, then his ancestor, even if high, or his descendant, even if low, may absolutely demand [hadd]', it would have been more appropriate.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn regarding slandering a deceased person. It explains that a grandchild can demand hadd for slander of his grandfather, and this is agreed upon except by Zufar. The author then addresses an apparent contradiction from al-Khaniyya, where if someone says 'Your grandfather is a fornicator' there is no hadd. The commentators resolve this by noting that the slanderer may be referring to any grandfather, including non-Muslims, so he must specify a Muslim for the hadd to apply. This is contrasted with saying 'You are the son of the son of the fornicatress', where the immediate grandfather is clearly identified. The passage then derives the condition that the deceased slandered must be muhsan (chaste, free, Muslim) for hadd to be due. However, the right to demand hadd extends to all ancestors and descendants, even if they are non-Muslims or slaves, because they are still eligible to feel the dishonor (shayn) caused by the slander. This is contrary to Zufar's view, who likely considered that a kafir or slave is not harmed by such slander. The Hanafi position is that faith and freedom are not prerequisites for this right; the key is the attribution of zina to a muhsan, which brings dishonor upon the relative. Hence, the commentator suggests that the author should have explicitly required the deceased to be muhsan for the rule to apply clearly.

Key Terms
مُحْصَن (muhsan): A person who is free, adult, Muslim, and chaste (not previously convicted of zina). The slandered person must be muhsan for hadd to be due.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the slanderer may be referring to a non-Muslim grandfather.
B) Because the grandfather is not muhsan.
C) Because only the father can demand hadd, not a grandchild.
D) Because the slanderer is a non-Muslim.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school grants the right to demand hadd for slander of a deceased muhsan to all ancestors and descendants, regardless of their faith or free status, based on the dishonor caused to them.

Child and Slave Cannot Demand Hadd Against Father or Master
Explaining an exception in hadd qadhf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a child and slave cannot demand hadd for qadhf against their father or master, explain the qiyas to qisas, and distinguish it from tazir.

Core Idea
A father is not punished due to his child; analogously, no hadd for slander against him.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a specific exception in the law of qadhf: a child cannot demand hadd against his father for slandering the child's mother, nor a slave against his master. The reasoning is based on a well-known principle in qisas. We will unpack this ruling, its analogical basis, and its implications, including the distinction between slander (qadhf) and mere insult (shatm).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Rule and its Scope
The text states that a child and a slave do not demand hadd against their father or master for slandering the mother. Note that this applies to all descendants (even low) and ancestors (even high), male or female.
The rule is not limited to biological sons; it includes all descendants.
2
Locate the Qiyas Proof
The author draws an analogy with qisas: a father is not executed for killing his son, nor a master for killing his slave. The common illa is that a parent/master is not punished on account of the child/slave.
The proof is qiyas jaliy, as the illa is clearly shared.
3
Analyse the Exception for Non-Related Claimants
If the slandered woman has a son from a different father, that son can demand hadd. Similarly, if she has a father who is not a slave of the slanderer, he can demand. This shows the impediment is specific to the relationship between slanderer and claimant.
The right to demand is per-person, unlike qisas which is indivisible.
4
Distinguish between Hadd and Tazir
The rule applies only to qadhf (false accusation of zina). If it is simply an insult (shatm), tazir applies, and according to al-Quniyya, a parent may be subjected to tazir for insulting a child. The author of al-Bahr al-Raiq expresses doubt about this.
The doubt arises from the principle that a father is not punished on account of his child at all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولا يطلب ولد وعبد أباه وسيده بقذف أمه لأن المولى لا يعاقب بسبب عبده، وكذا الأب بسبب ابنه ولهذا لا يقاد الوالد بولده، ولا السيد بعبده المراد بالولد الفرع وإن سفل، وبالأب الأصل وإن علا ذكراً كان أو أنثى. قالوا وليس للولد المطالبة بالحد إذا كان القاذف أباه أو جده وإن علا وأمه وجدته وإن علت؛ كذا في غاية البيان. وأشار إلى أنهما لا يطالبان بقذفهما بالأولى .. وقيد بولد القاذف لأنه لو كان للمقذوفة الميتة ابنان أحدهما من غير القاذف فله أن يطالب بالحد لعدم المانع في حقه. وكذا لو كان لها أب ونحوه فله المطالبة حيث لم يكن مملوكاً للقاذف فسقوط حق بعضهم لا يوجب سقوط حق الباقين بخلاف القصاص. والفرق بينهما أن القصاص حق العبد يستحقونه بالميراث ولهذا يثبت لجميع الورثة بقدر إرثهم، فإذا سقط حق بعضهم وهو لا يقبل التجزي سقط حق الباقين ضرورة، وأما حد القذف فحق الله تعالى وإنما للعبد حق الخصومة إذا لحقه به شين فيثبت لكل واحد منهم على الكمال فسقوط حق بعضهم في الخصومة لا يسقط حق الباقين ولهذا كان للأبعد منهم حق مع وجود الأقرب وقيد بالقذف لأنه لو شتمه والده فإنه يعزر . قال في القنية : ولو قال لآخر يا حرام زاده لا يجب عليه حد القذف قال : وقد كتبت أنه لو قال ذلك الوالد لولده يجب عليه التعزير .اهـ. وفي نفسي منه شيء لتصريحهم بأن الوالد لا يعاقب بسبب ولده فإذا كان القذف لا يوجب عليه شيئاً فالشتم أولى
His statement: (And a child and a slave do not demand their father and master for slandering their mother) because the master is not punished on account of his slave, and likewise the father is not punished on account of his son. For this reason, a father is not executed for his son, nor a master for his slave. By 'child' is meant the descendant, even if low; by 'father' is meant the ancestor, even if high, male or female. They said: And the child has no right to demand the hadd if the slanderer is his father or grandfather, even if high, or his mother or grandmother, even if high; similarly in Ghayat al-Bayan. And he indicated that they do not demand [hadd] for slander of themselves a fortiori. And it is restricted to the child of the slanderer because if the deceased slandered woman had two sons, one of whom is not from the slanderer, then he may demand the hadd because there is no impediment in his right. Similarly, if she had a father or similar, he may demand as long as he is not a slave of the slanderer. So the fall of the right of some does not necessitate the fall of the right of the others, unlike qisas. The difference between them is that qisas is a right of the servant that they inherit; thus it is established for all heirs according to their inheritance share. So if the right of some falls and it is indivisible, the right of the others falls necessarily. As for hadd qadhf, it is a right of Allah; the servant only has the right of litigation (khusuma) when dishonor attaches to him. So it is established for each of them completely; the fall of the right of some in litigation does not nullify the right of the others. For this reason, the more distant has a right even in the presence of the nearer. And it is restricted to slander because if his father insults him, he is punished with tazir. Al-Quniyya said: 'If someone says to another, 'O forbidden one, increase!' there is no hadd of qadhf upon him.' He said: 'And I have written that if a parent says that to his child, tazir is obligatory.' End. But I have something in myself about this, because of their explicit statement that a father is not punished on account of his child. So if slander does not obligate anything upon him, then insult is even more so.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining that a child cannot demand hadd for slander against his father, nor a slave against his master, when the slander is of the child's mother. The reason given is that a master is not punished because of his slave, and a father is not punished because of his son—this is an established principle analogous to qisas where a father is not executed for killing his son. The rule extends to all ancestors (including grandparents) and all descendants (including grandchildren). Interestingly, if the slandered mother has sons from different fathers, the son not related to the slanderer can still demand hadd. Similarly, if she has a father who is not the slanderer's slave, he can demand. This shows that the right to demand hadd belongs to each eligible person independently, unlike qisas which is one unified right. The passage also clarifies that the rule applies only to slander (qadhf); if it is merely an insult (shatm), then tazir (discretionary punishment) applies, and the father may be punished with tazir according to al-Quniyya. The author of al-Bahr al-Raiq expresses some doubt (shay' fi nafsi) about this, because if a father is not punishable for his child in anything, then even tazir should not apply. This indicates a point of scholarly debate.

Key Terms
حَقُّ الخُصُومَة (haqq al-khusuma): The right of litigation or to initiate a legal case. In hadd qadhf, the victim or their representative has the right to bring the case; it is not purely a right of Allah.
تَعْزِير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment left to the judge's discretion, as opposed to the fixed hadd penalty. For insults (not qadhf), tazir is applied.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position, why can a child not demand hadd against his father for slandering his mother?
A) Because the mother is not considered a victim of qadhf.
B) Because a father is not punished on account of his child.
C) Because the child must first seek permission from the father.
D) Because hadd is only enforceable by the state.

Key Takeaway
A child or slave cannot demand hadd for qadhf against their father or master, based on the principle that a parent is not punished due to his offspring, analogous to qisas.
