Hadd Qadhf: Allah's Right vs. Servant's Right
Explaining that the hadd penalty is nullified by the death of the slandered person, but not by the slanderer's retraction or the victim's pardon

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that hadd qadhf is not inherited or pardonable, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument based on the nature of the right involved.

Core Idea
Hadd qadhf is primarily Allah's right, so it cannot be inherited or pardoned by the victim.

The hadd penalty for false accusation of zina (qadhf) protects both the victim's honor and societal morality. But when these two aspects conflict, which takes precedence? This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq unpacks the theoretical foundation behind the Hanafi view that the penalty is primarily a right of Allah, and how that shapes key rulings on inheritance, pardon, and overlapping penalties. You will see how a single principle generates multiple furu' and distinguishes the Hanafi position from that of Imam al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Exception
The matn states that the hadd is invalidated by the death of the slandered person, but not by the slanderer's retraction or the victim's pardon. Note the annulment upon death—this is the starting point for the discussion.
The death of the victim cuts off the right to enforce the hadd entirely, even if part of it was already carried out.
2
Analyze the Dual Nature of Hadd Qadhf
The commentary explains that the penalty has two aspects: it removes dishonor from the victim (a servant's right) and it deters corruption in society (a right of Allah). Understand how these two elements coexist and can conflict.
The 'right of the servant' here is the victim's claim to have his honor vindicated; the 'right of Allah' is the public interest in punishment.
3
Contrast the Hanafi and Shafi'i Positions
When the two aspects conflict, the Hanafis give preponderance to the right of Allah; Imam al-Shafi'i gives preponderance to the right of the servant. Identify the reasoning behind each position.
The Hanafi reasoning is that the servant's right is taken care of by the ruler (guardian), so it becomes a right of Allah. The Shafi'i reasoning emphasizes the victim's need.
4
Trace the Practical Consequences (Furu')
From the chosen principle flow several branches: (1) non-inheritance of the hadd upon death, (2) no pardon by the victim after establishment, (3) no compensation, (4) overlapping of penalties (tadakhul). For each, note the Hanafi ruling and the Shafi'i counterpart.
Even scholars like Sadr al-Islam, who consider the servant's right dominant, still upheld the Hanafi furu', showing the strength of the school's position.
5
Examine the Agreed and Disputed Rulings
The passage also lists many rulings on which the schools agree, such as the requirement of a lawsuit, the judge's authority to act on his knowledge, and the prioritization of hadd qadhf over other hudud. These stem from the servant's right aspect.
This demonstrates that even in a disputed area, there is common ground. The disagreement is limited to specific branches.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ويبطل بموت المقذوف) أي بطل الحد لأنه لا يورث عندنا ولا خلاف في أنه فيه حق الشرع وحق العبد فإنه شرع لدفع العار عن المقذوف وهو الذي ينتفع به على الخصوص، فمن هذا الوجه حق العبد، ثم إنه شرع زاجراً حداً والمقصد من شرع الزواجر إخلاء العالم عن الفساد وهذا آية حق الشرع وبكل ذلك تشهد الأحكام، فإذا تعارضت الجهتان فالشافعي مال إلى تغليب حق العبد تقديماً لحق العبد باعتبار حاجته وغنا الشرع ونحن صرنا إلى تغليب حق الشرع لأن ما للعبد من الحق يتولاه مولاه فيصير حق العبد مدعياً به ولا كذلك عكسه لأنه لا ولاية للعبد في استيفاء حق الشرع إلا نيابة، وهذا هو الأصل المشهور الذي تتفرع عليه الفروع المختلف فيها منها : الإرث إذ الإرث يجري في حقوق العباد لا في حقوق الشرع ومنها العفو فإنه لا يصح العفو عن المقذوف عندنا ويصح عنده، ومنها أنه لا يجوز الاعتياض عنه ويجري فيه التداخل، وعنده لا يجري، وعن أبي يوسف في العفو مثل قول الشافعي، ومن أصحابنا من قال إن الغالب حق العبد وخرج الإحكام والأول أظهر كذا في الهداية واعلم أنهم اتفقوا على أنه يشترط الدعوى في إقامته ولم تبطل الشهادة بالتقادم ويجب على المستأمن ويقيمه القاضي بعلمه إذا علمه في أيام قضائه، وكذا لو قذفه بحضرة القاضي حده وإن علمه القاضي قبل أن يستقضي ثم ولي القضاء ليس له أن يقيمه حتى يشهد به عنده ويقدم استيفاؤه على حد الزنا والسرقة إذا اجتمعا، ولا يصح الرجوع عنه بعد الإقرار به وهذا كله باعتبار حق العبد، واتفقوا على أن الإمام يستوفيه دون المقذوف بخلاف القصاص ولا ينقلب مالاً عند سقوطه، ولا يستحلف عليه القاذف ويتنصف بالرق كالعقوبات الواجبة حقاً الله تعالى، ولا يباح القذف بإباحته ولا يحلف القاذف ولا يؤخذ منه كقيل إلى أن يثبت وهذا كله باعتبار حق الله تعالى ووقع الاختلاف في الفروع المذكورة أولاً. ثم اعلم أن صدر الإسلام وإن صحح أن الغالب حق العبد لم يخالف في الفروع من عدم الإرث وصحة العفو إلى آخره، وإنما أجاب عنها كما التبيين. وأطلق بطلانه بموت المقذوف فشمل الكل والبعض حتى لو ضرب القاذف بعض الحد فمات المقذوف لا يقام ما بقي وقيد بكونه قذفه حياً إذ لو قذفه ميتاً فلأصله وفرعه المطالبة بطريق الأصالة لا بطريق الميراث .
His statement: (And it is invalidated by the death of the slandered person) i.e. the hadd is annulled because it is not inherited according to us. There is no dispute that it involves the right of the Shari'a and the right of the servant. It was legislated to repel dishonor from the slandered person, who specifically benefits from it; from this aspect it is the right of the servant. Then it was legislated as a deterrent penalty, and the purpose of legislating deterrents is to rid the world of corruption; this is an indication of the right of the Shari'a. All rulings attest to this. When the two aspects conflict, al-Shafi'i inclined to give preponderance to the servant's right, giving precedence to the servant's right due to his need and the Shari'a's self-sufficiency, while we have given preponderance to the right of the Shari'a. Because what the servant has of right is taken care of by his guardian, so the servant's right becomes a matter of litigation; but not the opposite, because the servant has no authority to exact the right of the Shari'a except by delegation. This is the well-known principle from which various disputed branches follow. Among them: inheritance, because inheritance applies to rights of servants, not rights of the Shari'a; [2] pardon, because pardon is not valid from the slandered person according to us, but it is valid according to [al-Shafi'i]; [3] that it is not permissible to take compensation for it, and overlapping occurs, whereas according to him it does not. And from Abu Yusuf regarding pardon, [he has a view] like that of al-Shafi'i. Some of our companions said that the dominant aspect is the servant's right, and they derived rulings from that, but the first is more apparent, as stated in al-Hidayah. Know that they all agreed that a lawsuit is required for its establishment, testimony is not invalidated by passage of time, it is obligatory upon a musta'min, and the judge implements it based on his own knowledge if he knows of it during his tenure. Similarly, if someone slanders in the presence of the judge, he imposes the hadd. If the judge knew of it before he was appointed as judge, then he assumed the judiciary, he is not to impose it until testimony is given before him. Its execution is given priority over the hadd for zina and theft if they are combined. Retraction after confession is not valid. All of this is from the perspective of the servant's right. They all agreed that the ruler implements it without the slandered person, unlike qisas. It does not become property when it falls. The slanderer is not made to take an oath regarding it. It is divided by slavery like punishments that are obligatory as a right of Allah. Slander is not permitted by permission [of the slandered]. The slanderer is not made to take an oath, nor is anything taken from him as 'qil' until it is proven. All of this is from the perspective of the right of Allah. And difference of opinion occurred in the branches mentioned first. Then know that Sadr al-Islam, even though he corrects that the dominant aspect is the servant's right, did not differ in the branches regarding non-inheritance and validity of pardon and so on; he only responded to them as elucidated. And he stated its annulment by death of the slandered person absolutely, so it includes all and some, so that even if some part of the hadd has been inflicted and then the slandered person dies, the remainder is not carried out. And it is restricted to the case that he slandered him while alive, because if he slandered him dead, then his ancestor and descendant may demand [hadd] by way of original right, not by way of inheritance.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the matn statement that the hadd for qadhf is annulled by the death of the slandered person, but not by the slanderer's retraction or the victim's pardon. The commentator uses this to launch a thorough analysis of the nature of hadd qadhf as a composite of the right of Allah (haqq Allah) and the right of the servant (haqq al-'abd). The slanderer is punished both to protect society (right of Allah) and to remove dishonor from the victim (right of the servant). The conflict between these two aspects leads to differences between the Hanafi school and al-Shafi'i. The Hanafis (except Abu Yusuf on some points) give preponderance to the right of Allah. This explains several furu': (1) The hadd is not inherited upon the victim's death, because inheritance pertains to rights of servants, not of Allah. (2) The victim cannot pardon the hadd after it is established because the right of Allah is involved. (3) No compensation can be accepted in lieu of the hadd. (4) If multiple hadd penalties accumulate (e.g., slander and zina), they overlap according to Hanafis. Al-Shafi'i, by contrast, gives precedence to the servant's right and thus allows pardon and considers inheritance. The text also lists many agreed-upon rulings that flow from considering the servant's right (e.g., requirement of lawsuit, testimony, judge's initiative) and rulings from Allah's right (e.g., judge implements, no oath, division by slavery). The commentator then notes that even those who consider the servant's right dominant (like Sadr al-Islam) do not deviate from the Hanafi furu' like non-inheritance. Finally, it clarifies that the death annulment applies even if only part of the hadd has been administered; also, the rule applies only when the slander was made while the victim was alive, because slander of a dead person gives the original right to relatives directly, not via inheritance.

Key Terms
حق الله (haqq Allah): A right that belongs to Allah, meaning the public interest in upholding the limits and punishments of the Shari'a. Such rights cannot be waived by individuals and are enforced by the ruler.
حق العبد (haqq al-'abd): A right that belongs to an individual servant (human being), such as the right to seek vindication for a personal wrong. This right can be waived or inherited.
التَّدَاخُل (al-tadakhul): Overlapping of penalties: when multiple hadd offenses are committed, they may merge so that only one penalty is executed. In the context of qadhf, it means that if the slanderer also commits another hadd crime (like zina), the hadd for qadhf takes priority and the others are dropped or subsumed.
قذف (qadhf): The act of falsely accusing a chaste person of zina or denying his lineage, which incurs a hadd penalty of 80 lashes for a free person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is the hadd for qadhf not inherited upon the death of the slandered person?
A) Because inheritance applies only to rights of servants, not to rights of Allah.
B) Because the slanderer is usually the heir.
C) Because the victim can pardon it before death.
D) Because the hadd is a personal penalty that dies with the victim.

Key Takeaway
Hadd qadhf is primarily a right of Allah, so it cannot be inherited, pardoned, or compensated, and it allows overlapping with other hudud; this is the relied-upon Hanafi position derived from the purpose of deterrence.

Qadhf by Ambiguous Word: The Case of 'Zanata fi al-Jabal'
Intra-Hanafi dispute on qadhf by ambiguous word

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad on qadhf using the ambiguous word 'zanata' and apply the condition of anger to determine the ruling.

Core Idea
An ambiguous word becomes qadhf if the context of anger makes the insult clear.

This lesson explores an intra-Hanafi dispute about a phrase that can mean either 'climbed the mountain' or 'committed zina in the mountain.' The key question: Does saying 'zanata fi al-jabal' to someone constitute false accusation of zina (qadhf) warranting the hadd punishment? The answer depends on linguistic ambiguity, contextual clues, and the speaker's intention. We will examine the arguments of the founding imams and the relied-upon position for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed phrase and the two positions
The phrase is 'zanata fi al-jabal' (زنأت في الجبل). Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that if said in anger, it constitutes qadhf and hadd is obligatory. Imam Muhammad holds that no hadd applies because the hamzated form literally means climbing, and mentioning the mountain confirms that.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf – all three agree on the general ruling of qadhf, but differ on this particular ambiguous expression.
2
Understand the linguistic ambiguity of 'zanata' with hamzah
The verb 'zanata' (with hamzah) can mean both 'to commit zina' and 'to climb' in Arabic. Some Arabs use the hamzated form for zina, while others use it for climbing. The word is not decisive on its own.
If the word were with ya (zana) instead of hamzah, it would clearly mean zina and hadd would apply by consensus.
3
Analyze the contextual clue: anger and insult
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf argue that the context of anger and verbal abuse (sabb) indicates that the speaker intended the insult meaning (zina), because climbing a mountain is not an insult. The situation overrides the literal climbing meaning.
If the statement is made in a calm state (rida), then even Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf agree that no hadd applies, as there is no insulting context.
4
Note the consensus on clear cases
If the speaker simply says 'zanata' (without 'fi al-jabal'), hadd applies by agreement because the climbing meaning is absent. Similarly, if he intended zina (not climbing), hadd applies by agreement. The dispute only exists when the phrase is complete and the stated intention is climbing.
The preposition 'fi' is key: if 'ala' were used ('zanata ala al-jabal'), it would clearly mean climbing and no hadd.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The later commentators (sharihun) such as the authors of Fath al-Qadir and Ghayat al-Bayan prefer the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: hadd is obligatory if the statement is made in anger. They restrict the absolute wording of the matn to the angry state.
This is the operative position for fatwa today.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ولو قال زنأت في الجبل وعنى الصعود حد) ا هذا عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف وقال محمد : لا يحد لأن المهموز منه للصعود حقيقة قالت امرأة من العرب : وارق إلى الخيرات زنأ في الجبل وذكر الجبل يقرره مراداً ولهما أنه يستعمل في الفاحشة مهموزاً أيضاً لأن من العرب من يهمز الملين كما يلين المهموز وحالة الغضب والسباب تعين الفاحشة مراداً بمنزلة ما إذا قال يا زاني أو قال زنات، وذكر الجبل إنما يعين الصعود مراداً إذا كان مقروناً بكلمة «على» إذ هو المستعمل فيه قيد بـ«في لأنه لو قال زنأت على الجبل قيل لا يحد، وقيل يحد للمعنى الذي ذكرناه وفي غاية البيان والمذهب عندي إذا كان هذا الكلام خرج على وجه والسباب يجب الحد لدلالة الحال على ذلك إذ لا يكون صعود الجبل سباً وإلا فلا للاحتمال والحد لا يجب بالاحتمال .هـ. وفي فتح القدير: والأوجه وجوب الحد حيث كان في الغضب. وقيد بقوله «زنأت بالهمز إذ لو كان بالياء وجب الحد اتفاقاً. وقيد بالجار والمجرور إذ لو اقتصر على قوله «زنات» يحد اتفاقاً كما أفاده في غاية البيان. وأطلق في وجوب وقيده الشارحون بأن يكون في حالة الغضب، أما في حالة الرضا فلا حد اتفاقاً وبهذا ترجح الحد . وقيد بقوله وعنى الصعود لأنه لو لم يعن الصعود يحد اتفاقاً.
His statement: (And if he said 'zanata fi al-jabal' and intended climbing, he is punished with hadd). This is according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said: He is not punished because the word with hamzah (zanata) literally means climbing. A woman of the Arabs said: 'Climb to good deeds, zana in the mountain.' And mentioning the mountain confirms that it is intended. For both of them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), it (zanata) is also used for immoral acts (zina) with hamzah, because some Arabs pronounce the geminate with hamzah just as they soften the hamzah, and the state of anger and insult indicates that the immoral act is intended, similar to if he said 'O zani' or said 'zanata'. And mentioning the mountain only confirms climbing as intended if it is accompanied by the word 'ala', since it (climbing) is used with that. He restricted by 'fi' because if he said 'zanata ala al-jabal', it is said he is not punished, and it is said he is punished due to the meaning we mentioned. In Ghayat al-Bayan: The madhhab according to me is that if this speech occurred in a context of insult, the hadd is obligatory because the situation indicates that; since climbing a mountain is not an insult; otherwise (if not in insult context), no hadd due to probability, and hadd is not established by probability. In Fath al-Qadir: The most correct is the obligation of hadd when in anger. And he restricted by his saying 'zanata' with hamzah, because if it were with ya (zana) then hadd is obligatory by agreement. And he restricted by the preposition phrase ('fi al-jabal'), because if he only said 'zanata' he would be punished by agreement, as stated in Ghayat al-Bayan. And he made it absolute in obligation, and the commentators restricted it to the state of anger; as for the state of contentment, no hadd by agreement, and by this the hadd is strengthened. And he restricted by his saying 'and intended climbing', because if he did not intend climbing, he is punished by agreement.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the phrase 'zanata fi al-jabal' (زنأت في الجبل). The word 'zanata' with hamzah can mean both 'committed zina' and 'climbed' in Arabic. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that saying this to someone in a context of anger constitutes qadhf, warranting the hadd punishment, because the situation indicates the insult meaning. Imam Muhammad disagrees, arguing that the primary meaning of the hamzated form is climbing, and the mention of the mountain reinforces that; thus it is ambiguous, and hadd cannot be imposed on probability. The text discusses conditions: if the word is with ya (zana) instead of hamzah, it is definitely zina and hadd applies by consensus. If the preposition 'fi' is used, it is ambiguous; if 'ala' is used, it may be climbing. The relied-upon position is that in a state of anger, the hadd is obligatory due to contextual indication, as stated by the commentators. The key is intention: if the speaker intended climbing, there is no hadd; but if he intended zina or it is in a clear insult context, hadd applies.

Key Terms
زنأ (zanata): The verb with hamzah, used both for climbing and for committing zina, depending on context.
مهموز (mahmuz): A word with a hamzah consonant; here referring to the verb 'zanata' with hamzah.
قذف (qadhf): False accusation of zina, punishable by hadd (80 lashes) if the accuser fails to produce four witnesses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) Hanafi position, when does saying 'zanata fi al-jabal' to someone incur hadd al-qadhf?
A) In all cases, because the word resembles 'zina'.
B) Only if the speaker intended the literal meaning of climbing.
C) If said in a state of anger with insulting intent.
D) Only if the target is not muhsan.

Key Takeaway
An ambiguous word with multiple meanings can still constitute qadhf if the context (especially anger) clearly indicates the insult meaning; the mufta bihi view requires anger for the hadd to apply.

Mutual Qadhf: Both Punished with Hadd
Ruling when two persons accuse each other of zina

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two persons accuse each other of zina and explain why the hadd is applied to both, not cancelled.

Core Idea
When two people slander each other, both receive the hadd because the right of Allah predominates over personal right.

This passage addresses a scenario where two individuals exchange accusations of zina, each calling the other a zani. The question arises whether the hadd punishment applies to both, or whether the mutual insults cancel each other out. The text clarifies that both are liable to hadd, and explains the reasoning: the right involved is predominantly Allah's right, not a mere personal injury. Therefore, mutual retaliation (qisas) does not apply, and even reconciliation cannot drop the punishment without a demand.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is mutual qadhf: one person says 'Ya zani' and the other responds 'La bal anta'. The ruling is that both are liable for hadd al-qadhf.
This applies regardless of who initiated.
2
Understand the linguistic implication of the response
The response 'La bal anta' means 'No, rather you are the zani'. The particle 'bal' corrects the preceding allegation, so the speaker is explicitly accusing the other.
Thus both utterances are clear qadhf.
3
Analyze the rationale: why mutual qisas does not apply
Hadd al-qadhf is predominantly a right of Allah (haqq Allah). If it were treated like qisas (personal retaliation), it would allow dropping the right of Allah, which is impermissible.
Hence each hadd is independent.
4
Note the procedural rule regarding pardon and enforcement
Even if both parties pardon after the hadd is established, the imam does not execute it without demand, but the pardon itself is invalid. If one demands later, the hadd is carried out.
This balances the individual's right to demand with Allah's right.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولو قال يا زاني وعكس حدا أي المبتدي والمجيب بقوله لا بل أنت لأن كلاً منهما قذف صاحبه ؛ أما الأول فظاهر، وكذا الثاني لأن معناه لا بل أنت زانٍ إذ هي كلمة عطف يستدرك به الغلط فيصير المذكور في الأول خبراً لما بعد «بل» وإنما لم يلتقيا قصاصاً حد القذف الغالب حق الله تعالى فلو جعل قصاصاً يلزم إسقاط حقه تعالى فلا يجوز ذلك ولذا لم يجز عفو المقذوف، فإذا طالب كل منهما الآخر وأثبته لزم الاستيفاء فلا يتمكن واحد منهما من إسقاطه فيحد كل منهما ؛ كذا في فتح القدير وظاهره أنه يقام عليهما ولو أسقطاه وتقدم عدم صحته وأنه غلط في الفهم، فإذا أسقطاه بعد الثبوت امتنع الإمام من إقامته لعدم الطلب لا لصحة الإسقاط ، فإذا عادا وطلبا أقامه عليهما.
His statement: (And if he said 'O zani' and the other responded similarly, they are both punished with hadd) i.e., the initiator and the respondent by saying 'No, rather you' because each of them has slandered his companion. As for the first, it is apparent, and likewise the second because its meaning is 'No, rather you are a zani', since 'bal' is a conjunctive particle used to correct an error, so the mentioned (zani) becomes a predicate after 'bal'. They do not inflict qisas (retaliation) upon each other because hadd al-qadhf predominantly is a right of Allah; if it were made qisas, it would necessitate dropping His right, so that is not permissible. Hence the pardon of the victim is not valid. So if each of them sues the other and proves the charge, execution (of the hadd) becomes binding; neither can drop it, so each is punished. This is from Fath al-Qadir. Its apparent meaning is that the hadd is carried out against them even if they pardon each other, and it has preceded that such pardon is not valid and is a misunderstanding. If they pardon after the hadd is established, the imam refrains from executing it due to lack of demand, not because the pardon is valid. So if they return and demand, he executes it on them.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling when two people accuse each other of zina (e.g., A says 'Ya zani' and B responds 'La bal anta' meaning 'No, rather you are the zani'). Each is guilty of qadhf against the other. The hadd punishment applies to both because the right involved is predominately Allah's right, not purely a personal right. Therefore, the victim (the accused party) cannot pardon the hadd unilaterally. If both parties bring their case to the qadi and prove the accusation, the hadd must be executed on both. Even if they later try to reconcile and drop the charges, the hadd is not invalidated; however, the imam will not execute it unless one of them demands it again, because the right of the individual (to demand execution) is a condition for enforcement. Once they demand, the hadd is carried out. This emphasizes that qadhf is treated as a public offense against Allah's command, not a mere personal injury that can be settled between the parties.

Key Terms
قصاص (qisas): Retaliation; here, the idea of making the two hadd punishments cancel each other out as if in qisas, which is rejected.
عفو (afw): Pardon by the victim; in qadhf, pardon does not nullify the hadd because it is predominantly Allah's right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If two individuals exchange the words 'Ya zani' and 'La bal anta', what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) Neither is punished because they insulted each other equally.
B) Only the first speaker is punished.
C) Both are punished with hadd al-qadhf.
D) They are given ta'zir instead of hadd.

Key Takeaway
In mutual qadhf, both parties are liable for hadd because the right of Allah predominates; the punishment is not cancelled by mutual insult or subsequent pardon.

Averting Hadd and Li'an Due to Doubt in 'Zanaytu Bika'
Ruling on ambiguous zina accusation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that doubt (shak) averts hadd to ambiguous statements of zina, and explain why both hadd and li'an are nullified when a woman says 'zanaytu bika'.

Core Idea
When a statement admits two interpretations, both hadd and li'an are suspended due to doubt.

A woman says to a man: 'I committed zina with you.' Does this statement trigger hadd for qadhf (false accusation) or li'an (special procedure between spouses)? The Hanafi school, as explained in this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, holds that neither applies. The reason is that the statement is ambiguous: it could refer to zina before marriage, which would not be an accusation against the man, or after marriage, which would be an accusation. This uncertainty (shak) is sufficient to avert both punishments, following the well-established juristic principle of avoiding hudud when doubts arise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous statement
The woman says 'zanaytu bika' (I committed zina with you). This is the key statement that creates the legal problem.
The Arabic verb 'zanaytu' is first-person feminine, so she confesses her own zina but also implicates the man via 'bika'.
2
Uncover the two possible interpretations
The statement could mean either: (1) she committed zina with him before they were married, or (2) she committed adultery with him after marriage. Each interpretation leads to a different legal outcome.
The passage explicitly states these two possibilities.
3
Trace the legal consequence of each interpretation
If she meant before marriage, then she is not accusing him of anything (she is admitting her own sin), so no qadhf from him; thus hadd is not due on him, but li'an might be if they are spouses? Actually the text says on that interpretation, hadd is due without li'an (or corrected to li'an without hadd). The exact corrected version is 'li'an without hadd' for that scenario. If she meant after marriage, then she is accusing him of adultery, which would require li'an if they are spouses, and he might have a hadd claim against her for qadhf? But the text says that would be hadd without li'an. So each interpretation triggers a different single punishment.
The text initially says 'hadd without li'an' for one, but the correct is 'li'an without hadd' as per al-Hidayah.
4
Apply the doubt principle
Since it is impossible to determine which interpretation is correct, uncertainty (shak) arises. According to Hanafi legal maxim, hudud are averted by doubts. Therefore, neither hadd nor li'an can be applied.
This is the core reasoning: because the intended meaning is unclear, both punishments are nullified.
5
Conclude with the operative ruling
The final ruling (mufta bihi) is that both hadd and li'an are nullified (batil). The authority for this is the principle of shak and the corrections in al-Hidayah and al-Fath.
The passage confirms that this is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو قالت زنيت بك بطلاً) أي الحد واللعان لوقوع الشك في كل واحد منهما لأنه يحتمل أنها أرادت الزنا قبل النكاح فيجب الحد دون اللعان لتصديقها إياه وانعدامه منه ، ويحتمل أنها أرادت زناي الذي كان معك بعد النكاح لأني ما مكنت أحداً غيرك وهو المراد في مثل هذه الحالة. وعلى هذا الاعتبار يجب الحد دون اللعان لوجود القذف منه وعدمه منها فجاء ما قلناه أطلقه فشمل ما إذا بدأت بقولها زنيت بك ثم قذفها أو قذفها مجلس الشرع فلم يكن ذلك محض حقهما حتى يتكافاً فيه قوله : ( وعلى هذا الاعتبار يجب الحد دون اللعان صوابه اللعان دون الحد كما في الهداية والفتح وغيرهما. وقوله فجاء ما قلنا» أي من بطلان الحد واللعان لوقوع الشك فإنه على تقدير يجب الحد دون اللعان وعلى تقدير يجب اللعان دون الحد والحكم بتعيين أحدهما متعذر فلا يجب واحد منهما كذا في الفتح. قوله: (أطلقه فشمل الخ) أي
His statement: (And if she said 'I committed zina with you', both (hadd and li'an) are nullified) due to doubt (shak) occurring in each of them. Because it is possible she meant zina before marriage, in which case hadd is obligatory without li'an (since she is confirming him and not accusing him), and it is possible she meant 'my zina that was with you after marriage' because I did not allow anyone else besides you, and that is the intended meaning in such a situation. According to this consideration, hadd is obligatory without li'an due to the existence of qadhf from him (the man) and its absence from her. So what we said (that both are nullified) came about. He made it absolute, so it includes cases where she started with 'zanaytu bika' then he slandered her, or he slandered her in a shari'ah council, so it was not purely their right to cancel each other. His statement: 'And according to this consideration, hadd is obligatory without li'an' – the correct version is 'li'an without hadd' as in al-Hidayah and al-Fath. And his statement 'so what we said came about' means that both hadd and li'an are nullified due to doubt; because on one assumption hadd is obligatory without li'an, and on another li'an is obligatory without hadd, and the determination of one is impossible, so neither becomes obligatory, as in al-Fath.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage discusses the case where a woman says to a man 'zanaytu bika' (I committed zina with you). The ruling is that both hadd (for qadhf) and li'an (the special procedure for spouses) are nullified due to ambiguity (shak). The ambiguity is about timing: if she means before marriage, then she is not accusing him of zina (since she admits her own), so he does not get hadd, but she may be guilty of something else? Actually the text explains: on one interpretation, if she means before marriage, then she is confirming his innocence (since she says she committed zina with him before marriage, but that does not accuse him of anything current), so hadd would apply to him? Wait, the logic is: if she means before marriage, then he is not guilty of zina with her (since it was before), so her statement is not a qadhf against him, but it is a confession of her own zina. However, the man might claim hadd against her? Actually the text says 'hadd without li'an' on that interpretation. Then another interpretation: if she means after marriage, then she is accusing him of zina (since she says 'I committed zina with you' meaning during marriage), so that would be qadhf from him? Actually careful: The text says 'يحتمل أنها أرادت الزنا قبل النكاح فيجب الحد دون اللعان لتصديقها إياه وانعدامه منه' meaning: if she meant before marriage, then hadd is obligatory without li'an because she is confirming him (i.e., he is not guilty of zina) and there is no qadhf from him? It's a bit confusing. The corrected version in the commentary says the correct is 'li'an without hadd' on one interpretation and 'hadd without li'an' on the other. The overall outcome due to doubt is that neither hadd nor li'an is applied. The passage emphasizes that when the meaning is ambiguous, the default is to avoid punishment. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that hudud are averted by doubts.

Key Terms
شك (shak): Uncertainty about the intended meaning of a statement, leading to aversion of punishment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The woman receives hadd for confession of zina.

Key Takeaway
When a woman's statement 'zanaytu bika' is ambiguous about timing, both hadd and li'an are nullified due to doubt.

Ruling on Wife's Response to Accusation of Zina
Commentary on wife's response to qadhf with conditional rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules of qadhf to a wife's response and distinguish between different phrasings that affect the hadd punishment.

Core Idea
A wife's response 'I committed zina with you' is not qadhf due to probability, but changing the phrasing or adding a time can shift the punishment.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the nuanced rules of qadhf when a wife replies to her husband's accusation. The Hanafi school uses the concept of ihtimal (probability) to differentiate between explicit and implicit accusations. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly applying the hadd punishment in hudud cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the baseline ruling
When the wife says 'zanaytu bik' (I committed zina with you) in response to the husband's accusation, neither is liable for hadd because of the probability that she meant before marriage or that he was a witness.
No difference between 'bi' and 'ma'a'.
2
Analyze the first restriction (adding time)
If the wife adds 'before I married you', she becomes liable for hadd because she accuses him of zina before marriage, which he did not accuse her of, so it becomes qadhf against him.
The husband's accusation becomes void because she affirmed it.
3
Analyze the second restriction (wife vs stranger)
If the woman is a stranger (not his wife), she is liable for hadd because there is no probability to protect her statement.
The probability only applies within marriage.
4
Analyze the third restriction (different wording)
If the wife says 'anta azna minni' (you are more sinful in zina than me), only the husband is liable for hadd because it is a clear accusation of zina against him.
This is considered an explicit qadhf.
5
Synthesize the role of ihtimal
The presence or absence of ihtimal (probability) is the key factor that determines whether a statement constitutes qadhf. The Hanafi school uses this to avoid punishing ambiguous statements.
Ihtimal prevents the hadd from being applied to the wife in the baseline scenario.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أجابت به للاحتمال المذكور، ولا فرق بين الباء وكلمة مع كزنيت معك للاحتمال السابق مع احتمال آخر وهو أني زنيت بحضورك وأنت تشهد فلا يكون قذفاً. وقيد بكونها اقتصرت على هذه المقالة لأنها لو زادت قبل أن أتزوجك تحد المرأة دون الرجل لأن كلاً منهما قذف صاحبه غير أنها صدقته فبطل موجب قذفه ولم يصدقها فوجب موجب قذفها. وقيد بكونها امرأته لأنه لو كان ذلك كله امرأة أجنبية حدت المرأة دون الرجل لما ذكرنا من تصديقها وعدم الاحتمال الذي ذكرناه مع الزوجة وقيد بقولها زنيت بك لأنها لو قالت في جوابه أنت أزنى مني حد الرجل وحده ؛ كذا في الخانية
Then she responded with it due to the mentioned probability, and there is no difference between the preposition 'ba' and the word 'with' like 'I committed zina with you' due to the previous probability and another probability: that I committed zina in your presence and you are a witness, so it does not constitute qadhf. And it is restricted that she confined herself to this statement because if she added 'before I married you', the woman is punished with hadd but not the man, because each of them accused the other of zina, except that she affirmed him, so the effect of his accusation became void, but he did not affirm her, so the effect of her accusation became obligatory. And it is restricted that she is his wife because if she were a foreign woman, the woman is punished with hadd but not the man, due to what we mentioned of her affirmation and the absence of the probability that we mentioned with the wife. And it is restricted to her saying 'I committed zina with you' because if she said in response 'you are more sinful in zina than me', only the man is punished with hadd; as in al-Khaniya.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling of qadhf when a wife responds to her husband's accusation. The commentator explains that if the wife says 'I committed zina with you' (زنى بك) in response to her husband's accusation, it does not constitute qadhf because there is probability: she might mean that she committed zina before marriage, or that he was a witness. The ruling distinguishes between different phrasings. If she confines her response to this statement, she is not subject to hadd, but if she adds 'before I married you', then she is subject to hadd because she is accusing him of zina with her before marriage, while he did not accuse her of that. However, she is not subject to hadd if she is his wife because she affirmed his accusation, but if she were a stranger, she would be subject to hadd. If she says 'you are more sinful in zina than me' (أنت أزنى مني), only the husband is punished with hadd because that is a clear qadhf of him.

Key Terms
قذف (qadhf): False accusation of zina against a muhsan person, punishable by hadd.
احتمال (ihtimal): Probability or possible interpretation that prevents the statement from being a clear qadhf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Only the wife is punished with hadd.
C) Only the husband is punished with hadd.
D) Neither is punished.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses ihtimal (probability) to exempt a wife's ambiguous response from qadhf, but precise wording and context can shift liability.

How Acknowledgment and Denial of Paternity Determine Li'an or Hadd
Rulings on paternity acknowledgment and denial in li'an

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a man first acknowledges then denies paternity, and when he first denies then acknowledges, and identify the child's lineage in both cases.

Core Idea
Acknowledgment of paternity is binding; subsequent denial triggers li'an, while admission after denial incurs hadd.

In Hanafi law, paternity is established by acknowledgment. If a man later denies it, he becomes an accuser of his wife and must perform li'an. Conversely, if he first denies and then acknowledges, he has contradicted himself—the li'an is void and he faces the hadd penalty for false accusation. This lesson walks through these two scenarios and their consequences.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two sequences
Identify the two orders: acknowledgment-then-denial and denial-then-acknowledgment. Note which action is first.
The first action (acknowledgment or denial) determines the legal consequence.
2
Apply the ruling to each sequence
If acknowledgment precedes denial, the ruling is li'an. If denial precedes acknowledgment, the ruling is hadd of qadhf.
Li'an is required because the man becomes an accuser; hadd applies because he contradicts himself.
3
Determine the child's lineage
In both cases, the child belongs to the father because his acknowledgment (whether early or late) establishes lineage.
Li'an does not sever lineage in this context; the child remains his.
4
Distinguish the phrase 'not my son nor your son'
When the husband says this to his wife, he denies birth rather than accusing her of zina, so neither li'an nor hadd applies.
The phrase 'nor your son' avoids accusation, so no penalty.
5
Analogous case
The same principle applies if someone denies a stranger's parentage to his parents: no penalty.
The absence of accusation of zina removes both hadd and li'an.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وإن أقر بولد ثم نفاه لاعن) لأن النسب لزمه بإقراره وبالنفي بعده صار قاذفاً فيلاعن قوله : (وإن عكس حد) أي إن نفى الولد ثم أقر به فإنه يحد حد القذف لأنه لما أكذب نفسه بطل اللعان لأنه حد ضروري صير إليه ضرورة التكاذب، والأصل فيه حد القذف فإذا بطل التكاذب يصار إلى الأصل قوله : والولد له فيهما) أي فيما إذا أقر به ثم نفاه أو نفاه ثم أقر به لإقراره به سابقاً أو لاحقاً واللعان يصح بدون قطع النسب كما يصح بدون الولد قوله : (ولو قال ليس بابني ولا بابنك بطلاً) أي الحد واللعان لأنه أنكر الولادة وبه لا يصير قاذفاً، وكذا لو قال لأجنبي لست بابن فلان ولا فلانة وهما أبواه لا يجب عليه شيء
His statement: 'If he acknowledges a child then denies it, he must perform li'an' because lineage is established by his acknowledgment, and by denial afterwards he becomes an accuser, so he performs li'an. His statement: 'And if the opposite, he is punished with hadd' meaning if he denies the child then acknowledges it, he is punished with hadd of qadhf because when he contradicted himself, the li'an becomes void because it is a necessary punishment resorted to due to mutual accusation, and the original ruling is hadd of qadhf, so when mutual accusation is void, one returns to the original. His statement: 'And the child belongs to him in both cases' meaning in the case where he acknowledged then denied, or denied then acknowledged, due to his acknowledgment earlier or later, and li'an is valid without severing lineage just as it is valid without a child. His statement: 'And if he says 'he is not my son nor your son', both are void' meaning the hadd and the li'an, because he denies the birth, and by that he does not become an accuser. Similarly, if he says to a stranger 'you are not the son of so-and-so and so-and-so' while they are his parents, nothing is required of him.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage explains the rulings on acknowledging and denying paternity. If a man first acknowledges a child as his own, then later denies it, he must perform li'an (mutual cursing) because the lineage is established by his acknowledgment, and the denial makes him an accuser of zina against the mother. If he first denies the child and then acknowledges it, he is punished with hadd of qadhf because he contradicted himself, so the li'an becomes void and the original penalty of qadhf applies. In both cases, the child belongs to him due to his acknowledgment either earlier or later. If he says 'he is not my son nor your son' (referring to the mother), both hadd and li'an are void because he is not accusing her of zina but simply denying birth. Similarly, if he says to a stranger 'you are not the son of your parents' while they are his parents, nothing is required.

Key Terms
لعان (li'an): Mutual cursing between spouses when husband accuses wife of zina without witnesses, used to dissolve marriage and deny paternity.
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment of paternity, which establishes lineage and prevents later denial.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man denies a child, then later acknowledges it, what is the consequence?
A) He must perform li'an.
B) He is punished with hadd of qadhf.
C) No penalty.
D) The child is not his.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the order of acknowledgment and denial determines whether li'an or hadd applies; but the child's lineage is always affirmed.

Six Cases Where Qadhf Does Not Incur Hadd
Commentary on six cases where qadhf hadd is not applied.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six categories of persons against whom qadhf does not incur hadd and explain why each lacks ihsan.

Core Idea
Qadhf hadd requires the accused to be muhsan; absence of ihsan removes the penalty.

The hadd penalty for qadhf (false accusation of zina) is not applied automatically. A key condition is that the accused must be muhsan (legally chaste). This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq lists six categories of persons who lack ihsan, thereby exempting the accuser from the hadd punishment. Understanding these exceptions is crucial for correctly applying the law of qadhf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The passage states that in six specific cases, the one who commits qadhf is not punished with hadd. These are exceptions to the general rule that qadhf of a muhsan incurs hadd.
The ruling is a 'negative' exception: no hadd.
2
Examine the condition of ihsan
Ihsan (legal chastity) is the key condition for hadd in qadhf. The six cases all involve situations where the accused's chastity is compromised or uncertain.
Ihsan requires freedom, Islam, adulthood, and chastity.
3
Analyze the six cases
List and understand each case: (1) woman with a child of unknown father, (2) woman who performed li'an with a child, (3) man who had intercourse in non-milk ownership (e.g., with a married woman), (4) a shared slave-girl, (5) Muslim who committed zina while a disbeliever, (6) mukatab who dies with enough assets to pay his contract.
Each case involves a sign of zina or lost chastity.
4
Review the proof (dalil)
The commentator discusses the hadith of Hilal and Amr ibn Shu'ayb which seem to impose hadd for accusing a mula'anah. The Hanafi school addresses this by noting the weakness of the hadith or distinguishing its application.
The Hanafi school relies on the principle that the sign of zina (child without father) removes chastity.
5
Note the inter-madhhab dispute
The three other Imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) hold that qadhf of a mula'anah with a child does incur hadd. This is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on different interpretations of the same hadith.
The practical consequence: in the Hanafi school, no hadd; in the others, hadd is applied.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ومن قذف امرأة لم يدر أبو ولدها أو لاعنت بولد أو رجلاً وطئ في غير ملكه أو أمه مشتركة أو مسلماً زنا في كفره أو مكاتباً مات عن وفاء لا يحد) بيان لست مسائل : أما الأوليان فلقيام أمارة الزنا منها وهو ولادة ولد لا أب له ففاتت العفة نظراً إليها وهي شرط . أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان الولد حياً عند القذف أو ميتاً، وقيد بكونها لاعنت بولد إذ لو قذف الملاعنة بغير ولد فعليه الحد لانعدام أمارة الزنا . وأشار بقوله لاعنت» إلى أنه لا بد من بقاء لو بطل بإكذابه نفسه ثم قذفها رجل حد لزوال التهمة بثبوت النسب منه، وكذا لو قامت البينة على الزوج أنه ادعاه وهو ينكر يثبت النسب منه ويحد ومن قذفها بعد ذلك يحد لأنها خرجت عن صورة الزواني ولو قذفها الزوج فرافعته وأقامت بينة أنه أكذب نفسه حد لأن الثابت بالبينة كالثابت بإقرار الخصم أو بمعاينة، ولا بد من أن يقطع القاضي نسب الولد حتى لو لاعنت بولد ولم يقطع القاضي النسب وجب الحد على قاذفها كما في غاية البيان . والمراد بعدم معرفة أبي ولدها عدمها في بلد القذف لا في كل البلاد ولذا قال في الجامع اللعان حتى في حيث لم يقل ولو قالت كذا في جوابه لكنه خلاف الظاهر كما في النهر. قوله : لأنها لو قالت . جوابه أنت أزنى مني حد الرجل وحده) هذا مبني على ما مر أوائل الباب عن الخانية مخالفاً للظهيرية من أنه لا يجب الحد بأنت أزنى مني، أما على ما في الظهيرية فإنها تحد بقولها ذلك وقدمنا هناك عن الصغير : امرأة قذفت في بعض البلاد ومعها أولاد لا يعرف لهم أب فقال لها رجل يا زانية بن الخ. وفي فتح القدير : واعلم أنه إن صح ما رواه الإمام أحمد وأبو داود في حديث هلال أمية من قوله وقضى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أن لا يدعى ولدها لأب ولا ولدها ومن رماها أو يرمى عن رمى ولدها فعليه الحد» وكذا ما رواه الإمام من حديث عمرو بن شعيب عن أبيه . جده قال : قضى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في ولد المتلاعنين أنه يرث أمه وترثه ومن رماها به جلد ثمانين. أشكل على المذهب والأئمة الثلاثة جعلوا قذف الملاعنة بولد كقذف الملاعنة بلا ولد إلى آخره
His statement: (Whoever accuses a woman whose child's father is unknown, or a woman who performed li'an with a child, or a man who had intercourse in non-milk ownership, or a shared slave-girl, or a Muslim who committed zina while a disbeliever, or a mukatab who dies with sufficient assets, he is not punished with hadd) Explanation of six issues: As for the first two, due to the presence of a sign of zina from her, which is giving birth to a child without a father, so chastity is lost in her view, and it is a condition. He made it absolute, so it includes whether the child is alive at the time of qadhf or dead. And it is restricted that she performed li'an with the child, because if he accuses a woman who performed li'an without a child, he incurs hadd due to the absence of a sign of zina. And his indication by 'li'an' is that it is necessary for it to remain; if it is voided by him contradicting himself, then if a man accuses her, he is subject to hadd because the suspicion is removed by the establishment of lineage from him. Similarly, if evidence is established against the husband that he claimed it while denying, lineage is established from him and he is subject to hadd. Whoever accuses her after that is subject to hadd because she leaves the appearance of adulteresses. And if the husband accuses her and she litigates and establishes evidence that he contradicted himself, he is subject to hadd because that which is established by evidence is like that established by the opponent's confession or by eyewitness. And it is necessary for the judge to sever the lineage of the child; even if she performed li'an with a child and the judge did not sever the lineage, hadd is obligatory on her accuser as in Ghayat al-Bayan. And the meaning of 'father of her child unknown' is that it is unknown in the city of qadhf, not in all cities, thus he said in al-Jami' 'li'an even in' where he did not say 'and if she said such in response' but it is contrary to the apparent as in al-Nahr. His statement: 'Because if she said in response 'you are more sinful in zina than me', only the man is punished with hadd.' This is based on what passed at the beginning of the chapter from al-Khaniyya, contrary to al-Zahiriyya, that hadd is not obligatory for 'you are more sinful in zina than me'; however, according to al-Zahiriyya, she is subject to hadd by saying that. And we have presented there from al-Saghir: a woman was accused in some city and she had children whose father is not known, so a man said to her 'O adulteress' etc. And in Fath al-Qadir: Know that if what Imam Ahmad and Abu Dawud narrated in the hadith of Hilal ibn Umayya is authentic, from his statement: 'And the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) judged that her child should not be attributed to a father, and whoever accuses her or her child, he incurs hadd' and also what Imam narrated from the hadith of Amr ibn Shu'ayb from his father from his grandfather: He said: 'The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) judged regarding the child of mutual cursing that he inherits from his mother and she inherits from him, and whoever accuses her of it receives eighty lashes.' It is problematic for the [Hanafi] school, and the three Imams made qadhf of a woman who performed li'an with a child like qadhf of a woman who performed li'an without a child, etc.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage explains six cases where qadhf does not incur the hadd penalty because the accused lacks ihsan (chastity). The first two cases involve women whose child's father is unknown (a sign of zina) or who have performed li'an with a child. The presence of a fatherless child is an indication of zina, so the woman's chastity is questionable, and accusing her does not result in hadd. If a woman has performed li'an, her chastity is also in doubt. The third and fourth cases involve a man who had intercourse in non-milk ownership (e.g., with a married woman forbidden to him) or an owned slave-girl shared between partners. The fifth and sixth cases involve a Muslim who committed zina while a disbeliever (so his previous zina taints his chastity) and a mukatab (slave under contract of manumission) who dies with sufficient assets to pay his contract. The commentator then discusses the requirement that the child's father is unknown in the specific city, not globally, and provides evidence from hadith. The hadith of Hilal and Amr ibn Shu'ayb indicate that accusing a woman who has performed li'an with a child incurs hadd, which is problematic for the Hanafi school because they hold that hadd does not apply. The three other Imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) consider qadhf of a mula'anah as if she had not performed li'an, thus requiring hadd.

Key Terms
أمارة الزنا (amarat al-zina): An indication or sign of zina, such as a woman giving birth to a child without known father.
ملاعنة (mula'anah): A woman who has performed li'an (mutual cursing) with her husband, often resulting in dissolution of marriage and denial of paternity.
إحصان (ihsan): Legal chastity and integrity of a person, being free, adult, Muslim, and chaste. Required for the accused in qadhf to incur hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does qadhf of a woman whose child's father is unknown not incur hadd according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because the child is illegitimate.
B) Because the woman's chastity is in doubt due to the sign of zina.
C) Because the woman is a slave.
D) Because the accuser is the husband.

Key Takeaway
The hadd penalty for qadhf is not applied when the accused lacks ihsan, as in the six listed categories where a sign of zina or prior immoral act taints chastity.

Distinguishing Haram li-Aynihi and li-Ghayrihi in Qadhf
Analysis of qadhf rules concerning different types of unlawful intercourse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between intercourse that is haram li-aynihi and haram li-ghayrihi, and apply this distinction to determine whether qadhf incurs hadd.

Core Idea
Only intercourse forbidden in itself removes chastity; temporary prohibitions preserve it, making qadhf punishable by hadd.

In the previous lesson, we learned the basic cases of qadhf. Now we delve into a critical nuance: not all unlawful intercourse strips a person of ihsan (chastity). The Hanafi school distinguishes between intercourse that is inherently forbidden (haram li-aynihi), like zina, and intercourse that is forbidden due to a temporary external factor (haram li-ghayrihi), like during haidh or ihram. This distinction directly affects whether an accusation of zina incurs the hadd punishment. The passage also explores intra-Hanafi disputes on how permanent prohibitions are established and how ownership may interact with them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage discusses qadhf of a man who has committed unlawful intercourse. Determine whether the accuser incurs hadd or not, based on the nature of the intercourse.
The ruling depends on whether the intercourse is classified as zina (haram li-aynihi) or not.
2
Distinguish haram li-aynihi from haram li-ghayrihi
Define each term. Haram li-aynihi: forbidden in itself, e.g., intercourse outside any valid ownership or claim. Haram li-ghayrihi: forbidden due to a temporary cause, e.g., haidh, ihram, purchasing a shared slave-girl.
Only haram li-aynihi is considered zina; thus it removes ihsan.
3
Apply Abu Hanifa's condition on permanent prohibition
For a permanent prohibition (e.g., by blood or fosterage) to remove chastity, Abu Hanifa requires it to be established by ijma (consensus) or a mashhur hadith (well-known tradition).
This ensures the prohibition is certain.
4
Note intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Identify the dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding the statement 'you are more sinful in zina', and between al-Karkhi and the correct (sahih) view on whether a foster sister who is owned removes chastity.
These nuances affect the mufta bihi position.
5
Determine the mufta bihi ruling
The relied-upon view: qadhf of one who committed haram li-aynihi: no hadd; qadhf of one who committed haram li-ghayrihi: hadd. The sahih view prevails on the foster sister case: no hadd due to contradiction of ownership and prohibition.

As for the third and fourth, i.e., if he accuses a man who has had intercourse with a woman not in his ownership, or a shared slave-girl, then due to the loss of chastity, which is a condition of ihsan, because the accuser is truthful. And the principle is that whoever commits intercourse that is forbidden in itself (haram li-aynihi), hadd is not obligatory for accusing him, because zina is intercourse forbidden in itself. And if it is forbidden due to something else (haram li-ghayrihi), he is subject to hadd because it is not zina. And intercourse outside of ownership in every way or in some way is forbidden in itself. Likewise, intercourse in ownership with permanent prohibition. And if the prohibition is temporary, then the prohibition is due to something else. Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage discusses the third and fourth cases from the previous lesson: qadhf of a man who has had intercourse in non-milk ownership (e.g., with a married woman) or with a shared slave-girl. The principle is that if the intercourse is forbidden in itself (haram li-aynihi), such as zina (intercourse without ownership or any valid claim), then the person is not chaste, so accusing him of zina does not incur hadd because the accuser is truthful. If the intercourse is forbidden due to an external factor (haram li-ghayrihi), like temporary prohibition (haidh, ihram, etc.), then it is not zina, so the accused remains chaste, and the accuser incurs hadd. Abu Hanifa adds a condition: for a permanent prohibition (e.g., by blood) to remove chastity, it must be established by consensus or a well-known hadith. The commentator then lists various scenarios: intercourse with one's Magian slave-girl, menstruating wife, or foster sister who is owned – these incur hadd for the accuser because the prohibition is temporary or the permanent prohibition is overridden by ownership. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: al-Karkhi holds that the accuser is subject to hadd when the prohibition is permanent but the woman is owned, but the correct view (sahih) is that no hadd is incurred due to the contradiction of ownership and prohibition. The passage also mentions a difference between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding the obligation of hadd in some cases narrated by al-Hasan. Finally, a discussion on whether the statement 'you are more sinful in zina than me' is an explicit qadhf, with the apparent opinion that it is, based on al-Zahiriyya. Examples of 'well-known hadith' include the hadith 'No marriage except with witnesses' and the hadith on breastfeeding prohibitions.

Key Terms
حرام لعينه (haram li-aynihi): Forbidden in itself, like zina (intercourse without any valid claim). Such a person is not chaste, so qadhf incurs no hadd.
حرام لغيره (haram li-ghayrihi): Forbidden due to an external factor (e.g., haidh, ihram). Such intercourse is not zina, so the person remains muhsan.
حرمة مؤبدة (hurma mu'abbada): Permanent prohibition (e.g., by blood or fosterage). Abu Hanifa requires it to be established by ijma or mashhur hadith.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The prohibition must be from the Quran.
B) The prohibition must be established by consensus or a well-known hadith.
C) The prohibition must be by lineage only.
D) The prohibition must be by marriage.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between haram li-aynihi and haram li-ghayrihi is decisive in qadhf: only the former removes chastity and exempts the accuser from hadd.

Tadakhul in Hudud: One Punishment for Repeated Offenses
Expansion of the rule that one hadd suffices for repeated acts of the same type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of tadakhul to determine whether multiple hadd punishments are required for repeated qadhf, zina, or shurb, and distinguish the effect of partial execution.

Core Idea
One hadd suffices for multiple acts of the same type if no execution has begun; partial execution changes the outcome.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the principle that a single hadd punishment is sufficient when a person commits multiple offenses of the same category (qadhf, zina, shurb). The author details various scenarios, including the role of partial punishment and the presence of victims, illustrating how the rule of tadakhul (interference) operates in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core rule
State the general Hanafi principle: one hadd for repeated acts of the same type before any execution begins.
This is the default when no lashes have been struck.
2
Distinguish partial execution
If a person has received some lashes (e.g., 79 of 80) and then commits another offense of the same type, the remainder is completed and no new punishment is added.
This is still tadakhul but with partial completion.
3
Apply to multiple victims
For qadhf against multiple victims (free or slave), the rule applies differently if a slave victim is later emancipated, affecting the total number of lashes.
Free hadd is 80, slave is 40; the 40 given for the slave count toward the free hadd if the second victim is free.
4
Handle escape and new offense
If a person escapes after partial punishment and commits a new offense, the outcome depends on the type of offense: for shurb and zina, a new hadd is given; for qadhf, it depends on which victims appear.
This distinction is based on the nature of the claim (right of Allah vs. right of individuals).
5
Reconcile conflicting statements
The author resolves an apparent inconsistency: the example of one lash remaining applies when both victims are present; the statement that one hadd suffices for a group applies before any lashes are struck.
This shows the importance of context in understanding the rulings.

بشرعه. وأطلق في قوله قذف مراراً فشمل ما إذا كان المقذوف واحداً أو جماعة فقذفهم بكلمة واحدة أو بكلمات، وشمل ما إذا كان في يوم أو أيام وما إذا طلبوا الحد كلهم أو بعضهم، وما إذا حضروا أو حضر أحدهم كما في الخانية وغيرها، وما إذا جلد للقذف إلا سوطاً ثم قذف آخر في المجلس فإنه يتم الأول ولا يثنى عليه للثاني للتداخل وما إذا قذف عبداً فأعتق ثم قذف آخر فأخذه الأول فضرب أربعين ثم أخذه الثاني :قالوا فإنه يتم له ثمانين لأن الأربعين وقع لهما فيبقى للباقي أربعين، ولو قذف الآخر قبل أن يأتي به فالثمانون تكون لهما جميعاً ولا يضرب ثمانين مستأنفاً لأن ما بقي تمامه حد الأحرار فجاز أن يدخل فيه الأحرار. وفي المحيط : رجل شرب الخمر فضرب بعض الحد ثم هرب ثم شرب ثانياً ضرب حداً مستقبلاً، وكذا لو ضرب الزاني بعض الحد ثم هرب وزنى بأخرى، ولو ضرب القاذف بعض الحد فهرب ثم قذف آخر ثم قدم إلى القاضي ينظر ; إن حضر المقذوف الثاني والأول جميعاً يكمل الأول ويسقط الثاني لأنه يتداخل، وإن حضر الثاني دون الأول يضرب جلداً مستقبلاً للثاني ويبطل الأول لأنه أمكن إقامة الحد للثاني لوجود دعواه ولا يمكن الإقامة للأول لعدم دعواه .اهـ. فتعين حمل ما تقدم من أنه لو جلد للقذف إلا سوطاً إلى آخره على ما إذا حضراً جميعاً، ومن أنه لو قذف جماعة يكتفى بحد واحد على ما إذا كان القذف لهم قبل أن يضرب البعض كما لا يخفى .
By his law (?) And he made absolute in his saying 'qadhf repeatedly' so it includes whether the accused is one person or a group, whether he accused them with one word or multiple words, and includes whether it was in one day or several days, and whether they all requested the hadd or some of them, and whether they all were present or one of them was present, as in al-Khaniyya and others. And includes if he was lashed for qadhf except one lash, then he accused another in the same session, then the first is completed and he is not punished again for the second due to interference (tadakhul). And includes if he accused a slave, then the slave was emancipated, then he accused another, and the first took him and struck forty [lashes], then the second took him. They said: he completes eighty for him because the forty fell for both, so forty remain for the remaining. And if he accused the other before bringing him, then the eighty are for both together, and he is not struck eighty anew, because what remains is the completion of the hadd of free persons, so it is permissible for free persons to enter into it. In al-Muhit: A man drank khamr and was struck with some of the hadd, then he escaped, then he drank a second time – he is struck with a new hadd. Likewise, if the adulterer was struck with some of the hadd, then he escaped and committed zina with another woman. And if the qadif was struck with some of the hadd, then he escaped and accused another, then he is brought to the judge: if the second and first accusers are both present, he completes the first and the second is dropped because they interfere; if the second is present without the first, he is struck with a new hadd for the second and the first is invalidated, because it is possible to establish the hadd for the second due to his claim, but not possible for the first due to lack of his claim. End. So it is determined that what was mentioned earlier – that if he was lashed for qadhf except one lash etc. – is to be interpreted as when both are present, and that if he accused a group, one hadd suffices, as when the accusation for them is before he strikes some, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the rule that if a person repeatedly commits qadhf (false accusation of zina), zina, or drinking khamr, a single hadd suffices for all acts of the same type. The author explains the scope of the rule: it applies whether the victim (in qadhf) is one person or multiple persons, whether the accusation is made in one word or many, whether done in one day or over days, and whether all victims demand the hadd or only some. It also covers the situation where a person has been partially punished for qadhf (e.g., 79 of 80 lashes have been given) and then commits another qadhf – in that case, the remaining lash is given to complete the first punishment, and the second offense is not punished separately because it interferes (tadakhul). The text also discusses a scenario where a slave was accused of zina but then emancipated before the hadd is carried out: if the same person later accuses someone else (who is free), the punishment for the second is combined with the first such that if only 40 lashes have been given for the first (since the victim was a slave, hadd is 40), and then the second victim (free) comes, the total becomes 80, but the 40 already given count for both. The source al-Muhit provides further elaboration: a person who drinks khamr and receives only part of the hadd before escaping, then drinks again, is given a new complete hadd for the second offense. Similarly for zina. For qadhf, if the culprit escapes after partial punishment and then accuses another, the outcome depends on who appears in court: if both victims appear, the first hadd is completed and the second is dropped; if only the second appears, a new hadd is given for the second and the first is invalidated. The passage concludes by reconciling earlier statements: the earlier example of having one lash remaining applies when both victims are present; the statement that one hadd suffices for a group applies when the accusation was made before he had struck any lashes.

Key Terms
تداخل (tadakhul): Interference of penalties; when multiple offenses of the same type are committed before any punishment is executed, only one hadd is given.
حد الأحرار (hadd al-ahrar): The amount of hadd prescribed for free persons; for qadhf of a free victim, it is 80 lashes; for a slave victim, 40 lashes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person accuses a slave of zina and is given 40 lashes, and then accuses a free person, what is the ruling?
A) He is given another 40 lashes to make 80 total.
B) He is given a new 80 lashes for the second offense.
C) He is not given any additional lashes.
D) He is given 40 lashes for the second offense only.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle of tadakhul ensures that repeated offenses of the same type before execution are punished with a single hadd, with special considerations for partial execution and victim status.

Multiple Slanders: Merging of Hadd and the Ibn Abi Layla Incident
Ruling with proof via rational argument and precedent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on merging multiple slanders into one hadd and identify the five errors made by Ibn Abi Layla in applying the hadd.

Core Idea
Multiple slanders before punishment merge into one hadd; after partial hadd, merging depends on claimants' presence.

Slandering multiple people or the same person repeatedly raises questions about punishment: does each slander require a separate hadd, or do they merge? This lesson explores the Hanafi position on tadakhul (merging) in qadhf, including nuanced scenarios like partial punishment and repetition. We also examine a famous incident where judge Ibn Abi Layla erred, and Abu Hanifa's five-point critique, which highlights essential conditions for implementing the hadd.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule for multiple slanders
From the commentary: if a person slanders multiple individuals (with one or multiple statements) before any hadd is executed, only one hadd is imposed. This is the principle of tadakhul.
The rule applies whether the slandered are one or many, present or absent, as long as no punishment has been carried out.
2
Distinguish scenarios involving partial hadd
If the slanderer has already received part of the hadd (e.g., 79 lashes out of 80), then after additional slanders, the original hadd is completed (if the claimants are present) or a fresh hadd is given (if only the new claimant appears). The key factor is whether the first hadd can be completed.
The source uses examples like 'except one whip' and the freed slave case to illustrate partial completion.
3
Analyze the Ibn Abi Layla incident
Ibn Abi Layla gave two hadd punishments for one statement 'O son of two fornicators' without proper procedure. Identify his five errors: (1) no demand from the slandered, (2) one hadd suffices, (3) if two were needed, delay between them, (4) punishment in the mosque, (5) not checking parents' status.
Abu Hanifa's critique teaches that procedural and ethical conditions are as important as the substantive rule.
4
Apply the exception for repeating the same slander
If a person slanders the same individual again after being punished for it, no new hadd is imposed because the purpose (clearing the accuser's lie) is already achieved. This is based on the case of Abu Bakra.
This exception does not apply to other crimes like theft or fornication, where repetition after punishment incurs a new hadd.
5
Handle special cases: non-Muslims and slaves
If the slandered person was a disbeliever or slave at the time of the alleged zina, the slanderer is not liable for hadd because the accusation does not carry the same defamatory effect. The commentator clarifies that only when the slandered's status as free Muslim at the time of zina is established does hadd apply.
This shows that hukm depends on the status at the time of the act, not at the time of slander.

Source Text
