المنكر لأن النهي عما مضى نهي لا يتصور فيتمحض تعزيراً وذلك إلى الإمام ا.هـ. وذكر قبله من عليه التعزير إذا قال لرجل أقم على التعزير ففعل ثم رفع إلى القاضي فإن القاضي يحتسب بذلك التعزير الذي أقامه بنفسه ا هـ . وفي المجتبى : فأما إقامة التعزير فقيل لصاحب الحق كالقصاص، وقيل للإمام لأن صاحب الحق قد يسرف فيه غلظاً بخلاف القصاص لأنه مقدر بخلاف التعزير الواجب حقاً الله تعالى حيث يتولى إقامته كل أحد بحكم النيابة عن الله تعالى اهـ. وفي القنية : ضرب غيره بغير حق وضربه المضروب أيضاً أنهما يعزران بإقامة التعزير بالبادي منهما لأنه أظلم والوجوب عليه أسبق اهـ. الهندواني ويجوز يقال نكر المرأة دلالة على أنه لا فرق بين الزوجة والأجنبية، وقد أفصح عن ذلك في الخانية حيث قال : رأى رجلاً يزني بامرأته أو بامرأة رجل آخر وهو محصن فصاح به ولم يهرب ولم يمتنع عن الزنا حل لهذا الرجل قتله، وإن قتله فلا قصاص عليه. وذكر مثله في السرقة حيث قال : رأى رجلاً يسرق ماله فصاح به أو ينقب حائطه أو حائط غيره وهو معروف بالسرقة فصاح به ولم يهرب حل قتله ولا قصاص عليه .اهـ. وغاية الأمر أن ما في منية المفتي وعليه جرى الخبازي في مختصر المحيط مطلقاً لكن يجب حمله على التقييد توفيقاً بين كلامهم. ومن هنا جزم ابن وهبان في نظمه بالشرط المذكور مطلقاً وهو الحق واعلم أنه في الخانية شرط في جواز قتل الزاني أن يكون محصناً وفي السارق أن يكون معروفاً بالسرقة وبالأول جزم الطرسوسي، ورده ابن وهبان بأنه ليس الحد بل من الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر وهو حسن فإن هذا المنكر حيث تعين القتل طريقاً في إزالته فلا معنى لاشتراط الإحصان فيه ولذا أطلقه البزازي. قوله : (وذكر قبله الخ) قال في النهر : هذا محمول في حق العبد على أنهما حكماه ففي فتح القدير الذي يجب حقاً للعبد لتوفقه على الدعوى لا يقيمه إلا الحاكم إلا أن يحكما فيه قوله : ( وفي القنية ضرب غيره بغير حق الخ) قال الرملي : قدم أنهما إذا تشاتما تكافأ إذا لم يكن بين يدي القاضي فراجعه في شرح قوله «ولو قال يا زاني محصناً»
Book of Hudud / Chapter of Qadhf: Regarding the unrelated woman, the wife, and the mahram: for the unrelated woman, killing is not permissible except with the mentioned condition of not being deterred by shouting and hitting; for others, it is permissible absolutely. In al-Mujtaba, the principle is that for any person who sees someone committing zina, it is permissible for him to kill him; he only refrains out of fear that he might be killed and not believed that he committed zina. By this analogy, the one who oppresses with injustice, highway robbers, the tax collector, and all oppressors for the least thing of value, and all major sins, helpers, oppressors, and agents are included; killing all of them is permitted and their killer is rewarded. End. The author did not mention who administers it; they said: every Muslim administers it at the time of direct commission of sin; but after completion, that is not for other than the ruler. He said in al-Qunyah: He saw another committing an indecency that necessitates ta'zir, and he gave ta'zir without the permission of the muhtasib; then the muhtasib may give ta'zir to the one who gave ta'zir if he gave ta'zir after completion. The author said: His statement 'if he gave ta'zir after completion' indicates that if he gave ta'zir while he was engaged in the indecency, he has that right, and it is good, because forbidding evil is commanded of everyone; after completion, it is not forbidding evil because forbidding what has passed is not conceivable, so it is purely ta'zir, and that is for the imam. End. And before that, he mentioned: The one upon whom ta'zir is due, if he said to a man 'Administer ta'zir on me' and he did it, then he raised it to the judge, the judge counts that ta'zir that he administered himself. End. And in al-Mujtaba: As for administering ta'zir, it was said it is for the owner of the right like qisas, and it was said it is for the imam because the owner of the right might be excessive in severity, unlike qisas because it is fixed; unlike ta'zir which is obligatory as a right of Allah, where everyone administers it by deputyship from Allah. End. And in al-Qunyah: One struck another without right, and the struck one also struck him; both are given ta'zir, starting with the initiator because he is more unjust and the obligation on him is prior. End. al-Hindawani: It is permissible; the indefinite form of 'woman' indicates that there is no difference between the wife and an unrelated woman. And this is clarified in al-Khaniyya where he said: He saw a man committing zina with his wife or with another man's wife, and he is muhsin; he shouted at him and he did not flee nor desist from zina; it is permissible for this man to kill him, and if he kills him, there is no qisas upon him. And he mentioned similarly in theft: He saw a man stealing his property, he shouted at him, or he was breaking into his wall or another's wall while being known for theft; he shouted at him and he did not flee; it is permissible to kill him and there is no qisas upon him. End. The upshot is that what is in Munyat al-Mufti and what al-Khabbazi followed in Mukhtasar al-Muhit is absolute, but it must be restricted to reconcile their statements. Hence Ibn Wahban in his versification decisively stated the mentioned condition absolutely, and that is correct. Know that in al-Khaniyya, the condition for the permissibility of killing the zani is that he be muhsin, and for the thief that he be known for theft; al-Tarsusi decisively stated the first, but Ibn Wahban refuted him, saying it is not a hadd but from commanding good and forbidding evil, which is good; and since this evil is such that killing is determined as a means to remove it, there is no meaning to stipulating ihsan in it; thus al-Bazzazi made it absolute. His statement: (And before that he mentioned) said in al-Nahr: This is interpreted concerning the slave that they both judged; in Fath al-Qadir, that which is obligatory as a right of the slave based on his claim is not administered except by the ruler, unless they both judge in it. His statement: (And in al-Qunyah: one struck another without right...) al-Ramli said: He stated that if they insult each other, they are equal if not before the judge; so refer to his commentary on his statement: 'If he said, O zani, muhsin.'
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion on the chapter of qadhf, specifically addressing when killing a person caught in the act of zina or theft is permissible. The author distinguishes between an unrelated woman (ajnabiyya) and one's wife or mahram: for the former, killing is only allowed if the person does not desist after shouting and hitting; for the latter, it is allowed absolutely. The text then cites al-Mujtaba to establish the general principle that anyone who sees another committing zina may kill him, but he refrains for fear of not being believed. This principle is extended by qiyas (analogy) to oppressors, highway robbers, tax collectors, and all tyrants. A key point is the timing of ta'zir: during the commission of the sin, any Muslim may administer ta'zir (since it is part of forbidding evil), but after the act, only the ruler (imam) may do so. The passage also discusses who should administer ta'zir—whether the victim (like qisas) or the imam—and notes that striking without right leads to ta'zir for both parties, with the initiator being punished first. The text then delves into conditions for killing: some scholars require the adulterer to be muhsin (married) and the thief to be known for theft, while others (like Ibn Wahban) reject these conditions, arguing that this is not a hadd but rather a means of forbidding evil. The passage concludes with a note on the interpretation of rulings concerning slaves and mutual insults.

Key Terms
تعزير (ta'zīr): Discretionary punishment for offences without fixed hadd.
حال مباشرة المعصية (ḥāl mubāsharat al-maʿṣiya): During the very act of committing a sin.
بعد الفراغ منها (baʿd al-farāgh minhā): After the act has been completed.
البادي (al-bādī): The one who initiates the aggression.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when may a Muslim administer ta'zir without the imam's permission?
A) At any time, for any sin
B) Only during the actual commission of the sin
C) Only after obtaining permission from the muhtasib
D) Only for sins that require hadd

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of administering ta'zir depends on timing: during the sin it is forbidding evil, after the sin it is punishment reserved for the imam.

The Muhsin Condition in Killing for Zina and the Thief Condition
Discusses the condition of ihsan for zani and notoriety for thief

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute over requiring the zani to be muhsin and the thief to be known for theft, and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
The majority Hanafi position rejects the muhsin condition because killing here is forbidding evil, not a hadd punishment.

This lesson delves into a specific intra-Hanafi disagreement. In al-Khaniyya, it is stipulated that the person caught in zina must be muhsin (married) for killing to be permissible, and the thief must be known for theft. However, later scholars like Ibn Wahban and al-Bazzazi argue that this killing is not a hadd but an act of forbidding evil, so these conditions are unnecessary. Understanding this dispute clarifies the legal reasoning behind the final operative ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the positions in the dispute
Al-Khaniyya and al-Tarsusi condition killing of the zani on him being muhsin, and for the thief on being known for theft. Ibn Wahban and al-Bazzazi reject these conditions.
The dispute is recorded in the passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.
2
Analyze the basis for requiring ihsan
Those who require ihsan may view the killing as a substitute for the hadd of stoning (rajm) which only applies to the muhsin. Thus, they analogize to the hadd.
Al-Tarsusi decisively stated the first (ihsan condition).
3
Analyze the basis for rejecting the condition
Ibn Wahban argues that this killing is not a hadd but part of commanding good and forbidding evil. Since the evil of zina is the same regardless of marital status, there is no rationale for requiring ihsan. Al-Bazzazi made it absolute.
The passage says 'ليس الحد بل من الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر'.
4
Understand the implication for the thief
Similarly, for theft, the condition that the thief be known for theft is also rejected by the majority. The persistence in evil is the key, not reputation.
Al-Bazzazi's absolute view applies to both.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The later Hanafi authorities (Ibn Wahban, al-Bazzazi) are followed. The passage says 'وهو الحق' (and that is correct) referring to Ibn Wahban's view. The mufta bihi is that no muhsin or notoriety condition is required.
The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq seems to endorse this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحدود/ باب حد القذف
الأجنبية والزوجة والمحرم ففي الأجنبية لا يحل القتل إلا بالشرط المذكور من عدم الانزجار بالصياح والضرب، وفي غيرها يحل مطلقاً. وفي المجتبى الأصل في كل شخص إذا رأى يزني أن يحل له قتله وإنما يمتنع خوفاً أن يقتله ولا يصدق في أنه زنى. وعلى هذا القياس المكابرة بالظلم وقطاع الطريق وصاحب المكس وجميع الظلمة بأدنى شيء له قيمة وجميع الكبائر والأعونة والظلمة والسعاة فيباح قتل الكل ويثاب قاتلهم اهـ. ولم يذكر المصنف من يقيمه قالوا لكل مسلم إقامته حال مباشرة المعصية، وأما بعد الفراغ منها فليس ذلك لغير الحاكم. قال في القنية : رأى غيره على فاحشة موجبة للتعزير فعزره بغير إذن المحتسب فللمحتسب أن يعزر المعزز إن عزره بعد الفراغ منها . قال رضي الله عنه : قوله «إن عزره بعد الفراغ منها فيه إشارة إلى أنه لو عزره حال كونه مشغولاً بالفاحشة فله ذلك وأنه حسن لأن عن المنكر وكل واحد مأمور به وبعد الفراغ ليس بنهي عن المنكر لأن النهي عما مضى نهي لا يتصور فيتمحض تعزيراً وذلك إلى الإمام ا.هـ. وذكر قبله من عليه التعزير إذا قال لرجل أقم على التعزير ففعل ثم رفع إلى القاضي فإن القاضي يحتسب بذلك التعزير الذي أقامه بنفسه ا هـ . وفي المجتبى : فأما إقامة التعزير فقيل لصاحب الحق كالقصاص، وقيل للإمام لأن صاحب الحق قد يسرف فيه غلظاً بخلاف القصاص لأنه مقدر بخلاف التعزير الواجب حقاً الله تعالى حيث يتولى إقامته كل أحد بحكم النيابة عن الله تعالى اهـ. وفي القنية : ضرب غيره بغير حق وضربه المضروب أيضاً أنهما يعزران بإقامة التعزير بالبادي منهما لأنه أظلم والوجوب عليه أسبق اهـ. الهندواني ويجوز يقال نكر المرأة دلالة على أنه لا فرق بين الزوجة والأجنبية، وقد أفصح عن ذلك في الخانية حيث قال : رأى رجلاً يزني بامرأته أو بامرأة رجل آخر وهو محصن فصاح به ولم يهرب ولم يمتنع عن الزنا حل لهذا الرجل قتله، وإن قتله فلا قصاص عليه. وذكر مثله في السرقة حيث قال : رأى رجلاً يسرق ماله فصاح به أو ينقب حائطه أو حائط غيره وهو معروف بالسرقة فصاح به ولم يهرب حل قتله ولا قصاص عليه .اهـ. وغاية الأمر أن ما في منية المفتي وعليه جرى الخبازي في مختصر المحيط مطلقاً لكن يجب حمله على التقييد توفيقاً بين كلامهم. ومن هنا جزم ابن وهبان في نظمه بالشرط المذكور مطلقاً وهو الحق واعلم أنه في الخانية شرط في جواز قتل الزاني أن يكون محصناً وفي السارق أن يكون معروفاً بالسرقة وبالأول جزم الطرسوسي، ورده ابن وهبان بأنه ليس الحد بل من الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر وهو حسن فإن هذا المنكر حيث تعين القتل طريقاً في إزالته فلا معنى لاشتراط الإحصان فيه ولذا أطلقه البزازي. قوله : (وذكر قبله الخ) قال في النهر : هذا محمول في حق العبد على أنهما حكماه ففي فتح القدير الذي يجب حقاً للعبد لتوفقه على الدعوى لا يقيمه إلا الحاكم إلا أن يحكما فيه قوله : ( وفي القنية ضرب غيره بغير حق الخ) قال الرملي : قدم أنهما إذا تشاتما تكافأ إذا لم يكن بين يدي القاضي فراجعه في شرح قوله «ولو قال يا زاني محصناً»

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion on the chapter of qadhf, specifically addressing when killing a person caught in the act of zina or theft is permissible. The author distinguishes between an unrelated woman (ajnabiyya) and one's wife or mahram: for the former, killing is only allowed if the person does not desist after shouting and hitting; for the latter, it is allowed absolutely. The passage also discusses who should administer ta'zir—whether the victim (like qisas) or the imam—and notes that striking without right leads to ta'zir for both parties, with the initiator being punished first. The text then delves into conditions for killing: some scholars require the adulterer to be muhsin (married) and the thief to be known for theft, while others (like Ibn Wahban) reject these conditions, arguing that this is not a hadd but rather a means of forbidding evil. Key Terms
محصن (muḥṣin): A free, adult, Muslim who has had lawful sexual intercourse in a valid marriage.
معروف بالسرقة (maʿrūf bil-sariqa): Known for theft; a habitual or notorious thief.
الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر (al-amr bil-maʿrūf wal-nahy ʿan al-munkar): Commanding good and forbidding evil.

Checkpoint
What is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position regarding the condition of ihsan for killing a person caught in zina?
A) The person must be muhsin.
B) The person must be known for zina.
C) No condition of ihsan is required.
D) The killing requires permission from the imam.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school rejects the conditions of ihsan for zani and notoriety for thief, because the killing is an act of forbidding evil, not a hadd punishment.

Ta'zīr for Insults and Its Proofs from Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma'
Lists insults incurring ta'zir and proves ta'zir's obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of ta'zir for specific verbal insults and identify the proofs from Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma' that establish its obligation.

Core Idea
Ta'zir is wajib for any sin without a fixed hadd or for harming a Muslim, proven by Book, Sunnah, and consensus.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author lists numerous insults that incur ta'zir and then provides the foundational proofs. Understanding these proofs is essential for grasping the Hanafi position on discretionary punishment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is using specific insulting words (e.g., 'O fasiq', 'O kafir') against a Muslim, slave, or disbeliever. The ruling is that ta'zir is wajib (obligatory).
For the first two cases (slave or disbeliever accused of zina), qadhf hadd is prevented due to lack of ihsan, so ta'zir applies. For other insults, they cause harm without a fixed hadd.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites three sources: Quran (4:34: 'and strike them'), Sunnah (Prophet gave ta'zir for 'ya mukhanath' and imprisoned on suspicion), and Ijma' (consensus of the Ummah on obligation for major sins without fixed hadd).
The Quranic verse is about striking wives, but it is used as evidence for discipline permissibility.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative 'waḍribūhunna' indicates permission to strike for discipline, which is extended analogically to ta'zir for other offenses. The Sunnah provides direct application. Ijma' confirms the principle.
The hadith is a specific precedent.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The passage presents ta'zir as agreed upon; all schools accept its obligation in principle. No significant dispute is mentioned.
Differences may exist in details like maximum lashes, but not in principle.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The author relies on these proofs, and the mufta bihi is that ta'zir is wajib. The authority is from zahir al-riwayah.
The judge has discretion over the type and extent of punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاجر يا منافق يا لوطي يا من يلعب بالصبيان يا آكل الربا يا شارب الخمر يا ديوث يا محنث يا خائن يابن القحبة يا زنديق يا قرطبان يا مأوى الزواني أو اللصوص يا حرام قوله : (ومن قذف مملوكاً أو كافراً بالزنا أو مسلماً بيا فاسق يا کافر یا خبیث یا لص يا فاجر يا منافق يا لوطي يا من يلعب بالصبيان يا آكل الربا يا شارب الخمر يا ديوث يا مخنث يا خائن يابن القحبة يا زنديق يا قرطبان يا مأوى الزواني أو اللصوص يا حرام زاده عزر) لأنه جناية قذف في المسألتين الأوليين وقد امتنع وجوب الحد لفقد الإحصان فوجب التعزير وفيما عداهما قد أذاه وألحق الشين به ولا مدخل للقياس في الحدود فوجب التعزير وهو ثابت بالكتاب والسنة وإجماع الأمة. أما الكتاب فقوله تعالى واهجروهن في المضاجع واضربوهن [النساء : ٣٤] وأما السنة فكثيرة منها تعزيره عليه السلام رجلاً قال لغيره يا مخنث، وحبس عليه السلام رجلاً بالتهمة. وأجمعت الأمة على وجوبه في كبيرة لا توجب الحد أو جناية لا توجب الحد ؛ كذا في التبيين فصار الحاصل أن كل من ارتكب معصية ليس فيها حد مقدر وثبت عليه عند الحاكم فإنه يجب التعزير من نظر محرم ومس محرم وخلوة محرمة وأكل ربا ظاهر، ومن ذلك ما في القنية مسكينة أخذت كسرة خبز من خباز فضربها حتى صرعها ليس له ذلك ويعزر اهـ. ويؤخذ منه أن أخذ مال أحد ليس له ضربه حيث أمكنه من رفعه إلى الحاكم إلا أن يقال إنه لقلة قيمتها ولكونها مسكينة ومن ذلك الاستخفاف بالمسلم كما في القنية : ومنه المسلم إذا باع الخمر فإنه يضرب ضرباً وجيعاً بخلاف الذمي حتى يتقدم إليه، فإن باع في المصر بعد التقديم ثم أسلم لم يسقط الضرب ؛ كذا في القنية. وفي فتاوى القاضي: من يتهم بالقتل والسرقة وضرب الناس يحبس ويخلد في السجن إلى أن يظهر التوبة. وقد ذكروا في كتاب الكفالة أن التهمة تثبت بشهادة مستورين أو واحد عدل، فظاهره أنه لو شهد عند الحاكم واحد مستور وفاسق بفساد شخص ليس للحاكم حبسه بخلاف ما إذا كان عدلاً أو مستورين فإنه له حبسه . وقال المصنف فيها ولا يحبس في الحدود والقصاص حتى يشهد شاهدان أو واحد عدل .اهـ. وتقدير مدة الحبس راجعة إلى الحاكم كما لا يخفى . وفي فتح القدير : ويعزر من شهد شرب الشاربين والمجتمعون على شبه الشرب وإن لم يشربوا ومن معه ركوة خمر والمفطر في نهار رمضان يعزر ويحبس والمسلم يأكل الربا يعزر ويحبس، وكذا المغني والمخنث والنائحة يعزرون ويحبسون حتى يحدثوا توبة وكذا من قبل أجنبية أو عانقها أو لمسها بشهوة .اهـ. وفي شرح الطحاوي والأصل في وجوب التعزير أن كل من ارتكب منكراً أو أذى مسلماً بغير حق بقوله أو بفعله وجب عليه التعزير إلا إذا كان الكذب وعكس حداً فاعلمه اهـ . قلت : محمل ما. على ما إذا قال له يا خبيث مثلاً فرد عليه به فيحصل مر التكافؤ كما أشار إليه المؤلف هناك، أما الضرب فلا تكافؤ فيه لتفاوته وهو ظاهر. قوله: (ويخلد في الحبس إلى أن يظهر التوبة) أي أماراتها إذ لا وقوف لنا على حقيقتها ولا ينبغي القول بحبسه ستة
Fajir, munafiq, lutiy, one who plays with boys, usurer, wine drinker, dayyuth, effeminate, traitor, son of a prostitute, zindiq, qurtaban, shelterer of adulteresses or thieves, haram. His statement: (And whoever accuses a slave or a disbeliever of zina, or a Muslim with expressions like 'O fasiq, O kafir, O khabith, O thief, O fajir, O munafiq, O lutiy, O one who plays with boys, O usurer, O wine drinker, O dayyuth, O effeminate, O traitor, O son of a prostitute, O zindiq, O qurtaban, O shelterer of adulteresses or thieves, O haram' — he increased ta'zir) because it is a crime of qadhf in the first two cases, and the obligation of hadd is prevented due to lack of ihsan, so ta'zir is obligatory. In the other cases, he has harmed him and attached shame to him, and there is no entry for qiyas in hudud, so ta'zir is obligatory. This is established by the Book, the Sunnah, and the consensus of the Ummah. As for the Book, His statement: 'And abandon them in the beds and strike them' [An-Nisa 4:34]. As for the Sunnah, there are many, including that the Prophet (peace be upon him) gave ta'zir to a man who said to another 'O effeminate,' and he imprisoned a man on suspicion. The Ummah has agreed on its obligation for a major sin that does not necessitate hadd, or a crime that does not necessitate hadd; thus in al-Tabyin. So the conclusion is that whoever commits a sin that has no fixed hadd, and it is proven before the judge, then ta'zir is obligatory — such as forbidden looking, forbidden touching, forbidden seclusion, and apparent usury. Among that is what is in al-Qunyah: a poor woman took a crust of bread from a baker, and he struck her until she fell; he has no right to do that and he is given ta'zir. End. It is derived from this that taking someone's property does not permit striking him when it is possible to raise it to the judge, unless it is said that it is because of its low value and because she was poor. Among that is disparaging a Muslim, as in al-Qunyah: If a Muslim sells wine, he is beaten severely, unlike a dhimmi until he is warned; if he sells in the city after the warning then becomes Muslim, the beating is not dropped; thus in al-Qunyah. In Fatawa al-Qadi: Whoever is accused of murder, theft, and striking people is imprisoned and kept in prison until he shows repentance. They mentioned in Kitab al-Kafalah that accusation is established by the testimony of two masturs or one just person; so apparently if a single mastur and a fasiq testify before the judge about the corruption of a person, the judge may not imprison him, unlike if it is a just person or two masturs, then he may imprison him. And the author said in it: And he is not imprisoned in hudud and qisas until two witnesses or one just witness testify. End. And the estimation of the duration of imprisonment is referred to the judge, as is not hidden. In Fath al-Qadir: And he is given ta'zir: whoever witnesses the drinking of drinkers, and those gathered in a semblance of drinking even if they did not drink, and whoever has a small water-skin of wine with him, and the one who breaks fast in the day of Ramadan, he is given ta'zir and imprisoned; and the Muslim who consumes usury is given ta'zir and imprisoned; likewise the singer, the effeminate, and the wailing woman are given ta'zir and imprisoned until they show repentance; likewise whoever kisses an unrelated woman or embraces her or touches her with desire. End. In Sharh al-Tahawi: The principle in the obligation of ta'zir is that whoever commits an evil or harms a Muslim without right, by word or deed, ta'zir is obligatory upon him, except if it involves lying or is opposite to a fixed hadd; so know that. End. I say: It is interpreted as when he said to him 'O khabith' and he replied with the same; then mutual equality occurs as the author indicated there. As for hitting, there is no equality because of disparity; it is apparent. His statement: (And he is kept in prison until he shows repentance) i.e. its signs, for we have no knowledge of its reality, and one should not say he is imprisoned for six.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq focuses on ta'zir for verbal insults (qadhf and other offensive expressions). The author first lists numerous insults—such as 'fajir', 'munafiq', 'lutiy', 'usurer', 'wine drinker', etc.—and states that anyone who uses such words against a slave or disbeliever accusing them of zina, or against a Muslim with these epithets, incurs ta'zir. The reason: in the first two cases (slave or disbeliever), the hadd of qadhf is not applicable because the victim is not muhsin (i.e., free, Muslim, chaste), so ta'zir substitutes. For the other insults, they cause harm and disgrace, and since qiyas does not apply in hudud, ta'zir is required. The proof for ta'zir is then detailed: from the Quran (4:34: 'and strike them,' though originally about wives, it is used as evidence for ta'zir), from the Sunnah (the Prophet gave ta'zir to a man who called another 'effeminate' and imprisoned a man on suspicion), and from Ijma' (consensus that ta'zir is obligatory for major sins without fixed hadd or crimes without fixed penalty). The passage then gives examples: taking a loaf of bread from a baker and striking the poor woman is not allowed; selling wine by a Muslim entails beating; a person accused of serious crimes may be imprisoned until repentance; and various other acts like drinking, breaking fast in Ramadan, usury, singing, effeminacy, wailing, and kissing or touching an unrelated woman with desire all incur ta'zir and imprisonment until repentance. The text also notes that the duration of imprisonment is at the judge's discretion. Finally, it addresses mutual insults: if one says 'O khabith' and the other replies similarly, they are considered equal and both may be ta'zir, but striking does not have equality due to its severity.

Key Terms
تعزیر (ta'zīr): A discretionary punishment for offences that do not have a fixed hadd penalty
قذف (qadhf): False accusation of zina against a chaste person

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary textual proof from the Quran mentioned for the obligation of ta'zir?
A) Surah An-Nur verse 2
B) Surah An-Nisa verse 34
C) Surah Al-Ma'idah verse 38
D) Surah Al-Hujurat verse 6

Key Takeaway
Ta'zir is an obligatory discretionary punishment established by Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma' for any sin without a fixed hadd or for harming a Muslim.

The Linguistic and Legal Meaning of Qadhf
Definition of qadhf with linguistic and metaphorical scope

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the linguistic and legal meanings of qadhf and explain why the author uses it metaphorically for insults.

Core Idea
Qadhf literally means throwing stones; legally it accuses of zina, but here it is a legal metaphor for insult.

In this passage, the author of al-Bahr al-Raiq explains that the term qadhf is used in Islamic law as a metaphor for insulting someone, especially when the insult implicitly accuses a chaste person of unchastity. Linguistically, qadhf means throwing stones, as confirmed by Quranic verses. The author notes that he focuses on insults because they are common in his time, even though the strict hadd penalty for qadhf applies only to specific accusations of zina.

Guided Path
1
Identify the literal meaning
Read the passage to find the linguistic definition of qadhf. The text states it is 'throwing stones and the like' (الرمي بالحجارة ونحوها). Note the Quranic verse cited (37:8) that uses this literal sense.
The Quran uses qadhf to describe pelting devils with meteors.
2
Identify the legal metaphor
The author says he uses qadhf as a 'legal metaphor' (مجاز شرعي) for insulting (شتم). Understand that a legal metaphor is a term used in a non-literal sense due to legal convention.
Here, qadhf is extended from its literal meaning to cover general insults that may not meet the strict hadd conditions.
3
Explain the author's choice
The passage says the author limited himself to issues of insult because of their frequent occurrence, especially in his time. Recognize that the practical need to address common insults leads to a broader usage of the term qadhf.
This expansion does not remove the original hadd meaning; it adds a ta'zir dimension.
4
Connect the Quranic evidence
Examine the two Quranic verses cited. The first (37:8) confirms the literal 'throwing', and the second (34:53) shows qadhf used for 'casting at the unseen' (speculation). These support the linguistic foundation.
The passage uses these proofs to show that the term has a clear literal origin before its legal metaphorical use.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المحصنات ظاهراً كقوله يا كلب .اهـ. والمصنف رحمه الله اقتصر على مسائل الشتم لكثرة وقوعها خصوصاً في زماننا. وأطلق عليه قذفاً مجازاً شرعياً وهو حقيقة لغوية لأن القذف اللغة في الرمي بالحجارة ونحوها قال تعالى ويقذفون من كل جانب دحورا [الصافات : ۸] وقذف رميهن بالفجور والقذف بالغيب الرجم بالظن قال تعالى ويقذفون بالغيب [سبأ: ٥٣] وقذف قذفاً كذا في ضياء الحلوم
Chaste women apparently, like his saying 'O dog.' The author (may Allah have mercy on him) limited himself to issues of insult because of their frequent occurrence, especially in our time. And he called it qadhf as a legal metaphor, while it is a linguistic reality because qadhf in language is throwing stones and the like. Allah said: 'They are pelted from every side—rejected' (37:8). And qadhf of them (women) is accusing them of immorality. And qadhf of the unseen is casting by conjecture. Allah said: 'And they cast at the unseen' (34:53). And qadhf qadhfan—as in Diya' al-Hulum.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the term qadhf. The author explains that the term is used metaphorically in Islamic law to refer to insulting someone, especially by accusing them of unchastity. Originally, qadhf means throwing stones or something similar, as used in the Quran. The author notes that he focuses on insults because they are common in his time. The phrase 'chaste women apparently' refers to the fact that the hadd penalty for qadhf applies specifically when one accuses a chaste person of zina, but here the author extends the term to general insults. The Quranic verses cited confirm the literal meaning: in Surah al-Saffat, it refers to devils being pelted with meteors, and in Surah Saba', it refers to casting at the unseen (i.e., speculation).

Key Terms
قذف (qadhf): Linguistically: throwing stones; legally: accusing a chaste person of illicit sexual relations, but used metaphorically here for insulting.
مجاز شرعي (majāz sharʿī): A term used in a non-literal sense due to legal convention.
شتم (shatm): Insulting or reviling someone.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) Because the Quran commands all insults to be punished as qadhf.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi jurist expands the term qadhf metaphorically to cover insults, allowing ta'zir for common speech while preserving the strict hadd for proven slander against chaste persons.

Ta'zir for Insults and the Condition of Truth
Explaining when ta'zir is required for insulting someone and when it is not due to truthfulness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ta'zir for insulting someone and apply the condition of truthfulness to determine when ta'zir is not required.

Core Idea
Ta'zir for insult is not required when the speaker speaks the truth — the shame was already attached.

In Hanafi law, ta'zir is a discretionary punishment for sinful acts without fixed penalties. This passage clarifies that the general obligation of ta'zir for insults like calling someone 'fasiq' is conditional: the speaker must be unable to prove the attribute. If the person indeed possesses that attribute, the speaker is truthful and no ta'zir is imposed, as the shame already belonged to the person. Let us examine the ruling, its condition, and the rational proof behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the general ruling
The act is insulting someone with terms like 'fasiq', 'fajir', or 'thief'. The general ruling is that ta'zir is wajib for such insults, but this is not absolute.
The obligation is default, but subject to a condition.
2
Locate the condition that restricts the ruling
The author states the obligation is restricted (muqayyad) by the speaker's inability to prove the attribute. This means if the accusation is true and provable, ta'zir is not imposed.
The condition is from the author's expression 'wa huwa muqayyad bi-an ya'jiza...'.
3
Understand the rational proof (dalil aqli)
Al-Hasan ibn Ziyad in al-Mujarrad argues that if the addressee actually has the attribute, the speaker is truthful (sadiq) and no shame is newly attached; the shame preexisted. Thus, ta'zir is not deserved.
The proof is based on the principle that ta'zir is for deterrence, not for stating facts.
4
Note the source authorities and their weight
The ruling is supported by Al-Muhit and Fath al-Qadir, and the narration of Al-Hasan in al-Mujarrad. These are fatwa sources (fatawa mutakhkhirin), not necessarily Zahir al-Riwayah, but relied upon.
The authority weight is fatawa mutakhkhirin.
5
Apply the condition to a scenario
If a person is known to be a thief and someone calls him a thief, no ta'zir. If the attribute is unknown or the speaker cannot prove it, ta'zir applies.
The key is the knowledge of the attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأطلق في وجوب التعزير بالشتم المذكور وهو مقيد بأن يعجز القائل عن إثبات ما قاله قال في المحيط : ولو قال له يا فاسق یا فاجر یا مخنث يا لص والمقول له فاسق أو فاجر أو لص لا يعزر؛ ذكره الحسن في المجرد لأنه صادق في إخباره فلا يكون فيه إلحاق الشين به بل الشين كان ملحقاً به. وفي فتح القدير : إنما يجب التعزير فيمن لم يعلم اتصافه به، أما من علم اتصافه فإن الشين قد ألحقه هو بنفسه قبل قول القائل اهـ .
And he (the author) said absolutely regarding the obligation of ta'zir for the mentioned insult, but it is restricted by the condition that the speaker is unable to prove what he said. Al-Muhit states: If someone says to another 'O fasiq, O fajir, O effeminate, O thief' and the addressee is actually fasiq, fajir, or thief, he is not given ta'zir. Al-Hasan mentioned this in al-Mujarrad because he is truthful in his report, so there is no attaching of shame to him; rather, the shame was already attached to him. And in Fath al-Qadir: Ta'zir is only obligatory for one who is not known to possess that attribute; as for one who is known to possess it, then the shame was already attached to him by himself before the speaker's statement.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a statement of a ruling with its proof. It clarifies that the general obligation of ta'zir for insulting someone (like calling them 'fasiq' etc.) is conditioned on the speaker being unable to prove the attribute. If the person is actually fasiq, the speaker is truthful and therefore does not deserve ta'zir. The proof is rational: the harm (shame) already existed; the speaker merely stated a fact. Al-Hasan ibn Ziyad's narration in al-Mujarrad supports this. The text from Fath al-Qadir adds that if the attribute is known, the person himself brought the shame. This is a Hanafi position: ta'zir is not prescribed for true statements unless they cause harm in a prohibited way. The underlying principle is that ta'zir is for deterrence, and truthfulness does not require deterrence.

Key Terms
تعزير (taʿzīr): Discretionary punishment for sins that do not have a fixed hadd or kaffarah.
صادق (ṣādiq): Someone who tells the truth; here it means the speaker's statement matches reality.
شرط الصدق (shart al-ṣidq): Condition of truthfulness; the ruling that ta'zir is dropped if the insult is true and the attribute is known.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When the speaker is a close friend
B) When the addressee is indeed a fasiq
C) When the addressee is a minor
D) When the speaker apologizes

Key Takeaway
Truthfulness is a complete defense against ta'zir for insult when the attribute is known, because the shame already belonged to the person.

Proving One's Insult to Avoid Ta'zir
Ruling on evidence for insult to avoid ta'zir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when evidence of an insult is accepted to avoid ta'zir and distinguish between general and specific allegations.

Core Idea
General fisq is not proven by testimony; specific sinful acts are.

This lesson examines a ruling from al-Qunyah on ta'zir for insult. The key is that evidence must be specific about the sin committed, not just a general charge of fisq. Understanding this distinction is crucial for judges and muftis dealing with slander cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is insulting someone as 'fasiq'. Usually, this incurs ta'zir unless the insulter proves the basis of his insult. Identify that ta'zir is wajib for unproven insults.
The ruling is about the speaker's liability, not the insulted person's.
2
Locate the exception
The text explains that if the insult was 'zani' and four witnesses prove zina, the evidence is accepted because it relates to a hadd. Similarly, if the insulter specifies a sinful act like kissing a non-mahram, and brings two witnesses, the evidence is accepted.
The exception hinges on specificity and the nature of the right involved.
3
Understand the rationale
The rationale is that general jarh (impugnment) is not accepted because it is vague. But specific acts entail a right of Allah (hadd or ta'zir) that must be established.
This distinguishes between mere general accusation and a claim that triggers a fixed penalty.
4
Apply to similar cases
The principle extends to jarh of witnesses: if one says a witness is corrupt, he must prove a specific corrupt act (e.g., bribery) for the jarh to be accepted.
The judge should ask the insulter for the basis and require evidence if legally valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي القنية : قال له يا فاسق ثم أراد أن يثبت بالبينة فسقه ليدفع التعزير عن نفسه لا تسمع بينته لأن الشهادة على مجرد الجرح والفسق لا تقبل بخلاف ما إذا قال يا زاني ثم أثبت زناه بالبينة تقبل لأنه متعلق الحد، ولو أراد إثبات فسقه ضمناً لما تصح فيه الخصومة كجرح الشهود إذا قال رشوته بكذا فعليه رده تقبل البينة ؛ كذا هذه .اهـ. وهذا إذا شهدوا على فسقه ولم يبينوه، وأما إذا بينوه بما يتضمن إثبات حق الله تعالى أو العبد فإنها تقبل كما إذا قال له يا فاسق فلما رفع إلى القاضي ادعى أنه رآه قبل أجنبية أو عانقها أو خلا بها ونحو ذلك ثم أقام رجلين شهدا أنهما رأياه فعل ذلك فلا شك في قبولها وسقوط التعزير عن القائل لأنها تضمنت إثبات حق الله تعالى وهو التعزير على الفاعل لأن الحق الله تعالى لا يختص بالحد بل أعم منه ومن التعزير، وكذلك يجري هذا في جرح الشاهد بمثله وإقامة البينة عليه، وينبغي على هذا للقاضي أن يسأل الشاتم عن سبب فسقه فإن بين سبباً شرعياً طلب منه إقامة البينة عليه
And in al-Qunyah: If someone says to him 'O fasiq' and then wants to prove his fisq by evidence (bayyinah) to repel ta'zir from himself, his evidence is not heard because testimony on mere defect (jarh) and fisq is not accepted. But if he says 'O zani' and then proves his zina by evidence, it is accepted because it is related to the hadd. And if he wants to prove his fisq implicitly in a matter where litigation is valid, like jarh of witnesses if he says 'I bribed him with such-and-such, so he must return it,' the evidence is accepted. That is this. This is when they testify to his fisq without specifying it. But if they specify it with something that entails establishing a right of Allah or of a servant, then it is accepted. For example, if someone says 'O fasiq' and when the case is brought to the judge, he claims that he saw him kissing a foreign woman, or embracing her, or being alone with her, and so on, then he produces two men who testify that they saw him do that—there is no doubt about its acceptance and the ta'zir falls from the speaker because it entails establishing a right of Allah, which is ta'zir upon the doer. Because the right of Allah is not specific to hadd but is more general, including ta'zir. Similarly, this applies to jarh of a witness by a similar matter and establishing evidence against him. Accordingly, the judge should ask the insulter about the cause of his fisq; if he gives a legal cause, he should ask him to produce evidence upon it.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, discussing the procedure for a person who has insulted someone to prove the truth of his statement to avoid ta'zir. The key is that general testimony about someone's fisq is not accepted because it is vague. However, if the insult was specifically 'zani', and the person can prove the zina with four witnesses, the evidence is accepted because it relates to a fixed hadd. Similarly, if the insult was 'fasiq' and the speaker can point to a specific sinful act (like kissing a non-mahram), then testimony of that specific act is accepted because it establishes a right of Allah (ta'zir for that act). The passage also notes that in matters like impugning a witness (jarh), evidence of specific wrongdoing (like bribery) is accepted. The judge should investigate the basis of the insult and require proof if the cause is legally valid.

Key Terms
جرح (jarḥ): Impugning the character of a witness; in this context, general accusation of fisq without specification.
حق الله (ḥaqq Allāh): A right that belongs to Allah, such as hadd punishments and ta'zir.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Any testimony from two witnesses
D) He cannot prove it at all

Key Takeaway
Evidence of general fisq is not accepted; only specific sinful acts proven by witnesses can exonerate the insulter from ta'zir.

Why False Theft Claims Avoid Ta'zir: The Hanafi Intention-Based Distinction
Explains the rationale for no ta'zir on false theft claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school exempts a failed theft claimant from ta'zir and contrast it with the rule for zina claims.

Core Idea
A theft claim aims at property recovery, not accusation; thus failure to prove does not imply slander.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq tackles a subtle point in Hanafi criminal law: why someone who falsely accuses another of theft escapes punishment, while a false accusation of zina brings ta'zir. The key lies in the claimant's intention, deduced from the nature of the claim itself. Understanding this distinction illuminates the Hanafi method of deriving rulings from intent (qasd) and public interest (maslaha).

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed ruling
What is the difference? Theft claim fails → no ta'zir. Zina claim fails → ta'zir (or hadd).
2
Locate the basis for the distinction
The intention of the claimant: theft claim is about property, zina claim inherently attributes sin.
3
Analyze the proof (talil)
For property, one can prove ownership without attributing theft; for zina, one cannot prove the act without accusing the person.
4
Consider the counterargument
What if the claimant seeks hisba? The answer: even then, the only way to prove zina is by accusing, so the intention is unavoidable.
5
Apply the rule
In practice, a judge must assess the claimant's explicit words and circumstances to determine if the claim is truly about property or accusation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا إذا لم يخرج مخرج الدعوى قال في القنية: ولو ادعى رجل عند القاضي سرقة وعجز عن إثباتها لا يعزر بخلاف دعوى الزنا لأن القصد من دعوى السرقة إثبات المال لا نسبته إلى السرقة بخلاف دعوى الزنا وإن قصد إقامة الحسبة لكن لا يمكنه إثباتها إلا بالنسبة إلى الزنا فكان قاصداً نسبته إلى الزنا وفي المال يمكنه إثباته بدون نسبته إلى السرقة فلم يكن قاصداً نسبته إلى السرقة .اهـ.
And this is when it does not take the form of a lawsuit. Al-Qunyah states: If a man claims theft before a judge and is unable to prove it, he is not given ta'zir, unlike the claim of zina. Because the purpose of claiming theft is to establish a property right, not to attribute theft to him. Unlike the claim of zina, even if his intention is to establish a hisba (public good), he cannot prove it except by attributing zina to him, so he intends to attribute zina. Regarding property, he can prove it without attributing theft, so he did not intend to attribute theft.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, explaining a distinction in Hanafi law. If someone accuses another of theft and fails to prove it, he does not get ta'zir for slander, because the primary goal of a theft claim is to recover property, not to label the person as a thief. The accuser is seen as seeking his right, not spreading false accusations. In contrast, a claim of zina inherently involves accusing someone of a sin; it cannot be separated from the accusation. Therefore, if the claimant fails to prove zina, he is liable for hadd of qadhf (if the accused is muhsan) or ta'zir. This ruling is based on the intention (qasd) of the claimant, which is discerned from the nature of the claim. The principle is that ta'zir for false accusations is tied to the harm of reputation, and property claims do not necessarily harm reputation.

Key Terms
دعوى (daʿwā): A lawsuit or claim in court; when it takes the form of a judicial claim, different rules apply.
حسبة (ḥisba): Acting for the public good, e.g., reporting a crime to uphold moral order.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because theft is not a sin
B) Because the claimant can still prove it later
C) Because the purpose is to recover property, not to accuse
D) Because the judge always dismisses theft claims

Key Takeaway
A failed theft claim does not warrant ta'zir because its aim is property recovery, not defamation.

Ruling on Insulting a Dhimmi
Stating the ruling that a Muslim is punished for insulting a dhimmi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a Muslim insulting a dhimmi and identify the condition that triggers ta'zir.

Core Idea
Insulting a dhimmi is sinful and punishable by ta'zir, even if the words are factually true.

We have already seen that ta'zir applies for insults between Muslims. This lesson extends that ruling to interactions with non-Muslim subjects (dhimmis). The Hanafi school holds that a Muslim who insults a dhimmi incurs sin and must be punished, because the dhimma contract guarantees protection from harm. Even truthful insults like 'O kafir' can lead to ta'zir if they cause pain.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Locate the act being discussed: a Muslim insulting a dhimmi. The ruling: it is a sin (ithm) and warrants ta'zir (discretionary punishment).
The act is prohibited (haram), not murder or apostasy.
2
Understand the legal basis
The basis is the prohibition of harming others (the dhimmi). Even though calling a Jew 'kafir' is factually correct, the dhimmi's rights under the dhimma contract prevent such harm.
Hanafis prioritize protecting the dhimmi's feelings; truth does not license harm.
3
Recognize the limits
The insulter does not become a kafir by uttering these words. The punishment is only ta'zir, not hadd. Also, ta'zir is conditional on the dhimmi feeling pain (i.e., being hurt by the insult).
If the dhimmi is not pained, some texts suggest no ta'zir, but the sin remains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتقييد بالمسلم في قوله أو مسلماً في مسائل الشتم اتفاقاً إذ لو شتم مسلم ذمياً فإنه لأنه ارتكب معصية؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وفي القنية من باب الاستخلال ورد المظالم : يعزر لو قال ليهودي أو مجوسي يا كافر يأثم إن شق عليه .اهـ. ومقتضاه أن يعزر لارتكابه ما أوجب الإثم، وقد جعل ألفاظ المصنف من الشتم يا كافر يا منافق . وفي المحيط : جعل منه يا يهودي وظاهره أن الشاتم لا يكفر به. وصرح في الخلاصة
And the restriction to a Muslim in his statement 'or a Muslim' in the issues of insult is agreed upon, for if a Muslim insults a dhimmi, then he has committed a sin. As in Fath al-Qadir. And in al-Qunyah, from the chapter of Istikhlaal and Returning Oppressions: He is given ta'zir if he says to a Jew or Magian 'O unbeliever'; he sins if it pains him (the dhimmi). Its implication is that he is given ta'zir for committing that which necessitates sin. And the author's words from insults include 'O unbeliever', 'O hypocrite'. And in al-Muhit, he includes 'O Jew'. And its apparent meaning is that the insulter does not become an unbeliever by it. And it is stated in al-Khulasa...
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a statement of a ruling. It establishes that the previous discussion of ta'zir for insults applies only to Muslims insulting Muslims. If a Muslim insults a non-Muslim (dhimmi), he also receives ta'zir because it is a sin to cause pain. The texts from Fath al-Qadir and al-Qunyah confirm that even though calling a Jew 'kafir' is factually true, the Muslim is punished if it hurts the dhimmi. The words like 'ya kafir', 'ya munafiq', 'ya yahudi' are included. Importantly, the insulter does not become a kafir by saying such words. This ruling is based on the prohibition of harming others and the rights of dhimmis under Islamic protection.

Key Terms
ذمي (dhimmī): A non-Muslim subject living under Muslim rule with a contract of protection (dhimma).
تعزير (taʿzīr): Discretionary punishment for offenses that are not subject to hadd or qisas.
إثم (ithm): Sin; moral wrong that entails divine punishment if not repented.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Always, because the word is an insult.
B) Only if the dhimmi is pained by it.
C) Never, because it is true.
D) Only if the Muslim repeats it publicly.

Key Takeaway
Insulting a dhimmi is a sin and merits ta'zir, even if the statement is factually true, because the dhimmi's right to protection from harm takes precedence.

Explicit vs Implicit Slander: Words that Incur Hadd and Ta'zir
Defining explicit vs. implicit slander.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between sarih (explicit) and kinaya (implicit) slander words in Hanafi fiqh and identify which words incur hadd and which incur ta'zir.

Core Idea
Implicit slander words like 'qahba' incur ta'zir, not hadd, because they are kinaya.

In this passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq, the author clarifies the boundary between explicit and implicit accusations of zina. Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining the appropriate punishment in Hanafi fiqh. The term 'qahba' is analyzed as a metaphor, and a list of other insulting words that trigger ta'zir is provided.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms
Point out the terms sarih (explicit) and kinaya (implicit) as used in the passage. Understand that the difference determines whether hadd or ta'zir applies.
2
Examine the etymology of 'qahba'
Analyze how 'qahba' became a metaphor for adulteress due to the coughing habit of Arab prostitutes. This illustrates why it is considered kinaya.
Etymology shows indirect reference.
3
List the words that obligate ta'zir
Identify the words mentioned in the passage that require ta'zir: 'ya ibn al-aswad', 'ya ibn al-hajjam', 'ya kha'in', 'ya safih'.
These are not explicit accusations of zina.
4
Apply the distinction
Given an insult, determine if it is sarih (directly accuses zina) or kinaya (implies but does not directly state). This decides the punishment.
Practice with the terms from the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحدود/ باب حد القذف. وكأن الفرق بينهما أن روسبي صريح في القذف بالزنا بخلاف القحبة فإنه كناية عن الزانية . قال في الظهيرية : والقحبة الزانية مأخوذ من القحاب وهو السعال وكانت الزانية في العرب إذا مر بها رجل سعلت ليقضي منها وطره فسميت الزانية قحبة لهذا .اهـ. ومن الألفاظ الموجبة للتعزير بارستاقي يا ابن الأسود ويا ابن الحجام وهو ليس كذلك ؛ كذا في التبيين. ومنها يا خائن كما في الظهيرية، ومنها يا سفيه كما في المحيط.
Book of Hudud, Chapter of Hadd al-Qadhf. And it is as if the difference between them is that 'Rusbi' is explicit in slander of zina unlike 'qahba' for it is a metaphor (kinaya) for the adulteress. He said in al-Zahiriyya: 'al-qahba is the adulteress, derived from al-quhab which is coughing, and the adulteress among the Arabs, when a man passed by her, she coughed in order to fulfill her desire from him, so the adulteress was named qahba for this.' End. And among the words that obligate ta'zir: 'بارستاقي يا ابن الأسود ويا ابن الحجام' and he is not like that; thus in al-Tabyin. And among them: 'يا خائن' as in al-Zahiriyya, and among them: 'يا سفيه' as in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage discusses the distinction between explicit and implicit slander. The term 'qahba' (prostitute) is considered kinaya (allusion) and not explicit, hence it does not incur the hadd penalty for qadhf but rather ta'zir. The passage then lists various insulting phrases that obligate ta'zir, such as 'you son of a black woman' etc. The author draws from al-Zahiriyya and al-Tabyin. The list includes 'ya kha'in' (O traitor) and 'ya safih' (O foolish), which require ta'zir according to Hanafi sources.

Key Terms
قحبة (qahba): Literally the adulteress/prostitute; derived from coughing (qahhab) because Arab prostitutes would cough to attract men.
صريح (sarih): Explicit wording that directly accuses someone of zina, such as 'you committed zina'.
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment not fixed by Shari'a, for offenses that do not have a prescribed hadd.
روسپی (rusbi): Persian word for prostitute; considered explicit (sarih) in the passage.
بارستاقي (barstaki): Unknown term from the passage; appears in the list of ta'zir words.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It incurs the hadd penalty for qadhf (slander).
B) It incurs the ta'zir (discretionary punishment).
C) It is not punishable at all.
D) It requires the judge to issue a warning only.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, slander (qadhf) must be explicit to incur hadd; implicit words like 'qahba' fall under ta'zir.

Recommended Response to Insults Requiring Ta'zir
Advising the insulted person to not respond or to seek remedy through judge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi recommendation for responding to insults that necessitate ta'zir and distinguish between the preferred and permissible actions.

Core Idea
When insulted with a word requiring ta'zir, the best is silence; but if you must respond, let it be mild.

Imagine someone insults you with a word that could earn them a discretionary punishment (ta'zir). How should you, as a Muslim, react? The Hanafi school, drawing on the guidance of the Qur'an and Sunnah, advises restraint. This lesson explores the preferred course and permissible alternatives from the classic work Fath al-Qadir.

Guided Path
1
Identify the trigger
Recognize that the insult is of a type that obligates ta'zir (e.g., a serious defamation or foul name). The passage refers to 'ما يوجب التعزير'.
Not all insults trigger ta'zir; only those that are considered major offenses.
2
State the preferred response
The text says 'الأولى' (the best) is not to respond at all. This is a recommendation, not an obligation.
Al-awla indicates a strong preference but allows other options.
3
Consider the judicial option
If you choose not to ignore, you may take the matter to the judge (qadi) for discipline of the offender. This is permissible.
The judge's role is to enforce ta'zir; this avoids personal retaliation.
4
Evaluate a mild response
If you do respond, the passage gives an example of a mild reply: 'بل أنت لا بأس' (Rather, you are fine). Such a retort is allowed and likely does not incur ta'zir.
A harsh counter-insult would lead to mutual ta'zir, as noted in the broader commentary.
5
Apply the framework
Now practice: given an insult requiring ta'zir, decide stepwise – first prefer silence, then consider judicial recourse, and if response is inevitable, keep it mild.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي فتح القدير : الأولى للإنسان فيما إذا قيل له ما يوجب التعزير أن لا يجيبه قالوا لو قال له يا خبيث الأحسن أن يكف عنه، ولو رفع إلى القاضي ليؤدبه يجوز ، ولو أجاب مع هذا فقال بل أنت لا بأس اهـ.
And in Fath al-Qadir: The best for a person when something that obligates ta'zir is said to him is that he does not respond. They said: If someone says to him 'O wicked one,' the best is that he refrains from replying, and if he raises it to the judge to discipline him, it is permissible, and if he responds nevertheless and says 'Rather you are alright,' end.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage from Fath al-Qadir provides practical advice for someone who is insulted with a word that necessitates ta'zir. The preferred course of action (al-awla) is not to retaliate verbally. However, if the victim seeks the judge's intervention for disciplining the offender, that is permissible. Note that if the victim responds with a counter-insult, it may lead to mutual ta'zir, as discussed later in the text. The phrase 'بل أنت لا بأس' (rather you are alright) is given as an example of a mild response that might not escalate.

Key Terms
الأولى (al-awla): The better or preferred option, not obligatory.
التعزير (al-ta'zir): Discretionary punishment as defined previously.
يؤدبه (yu'adhibuhu): To discipline him (by the judge).

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Take the matter to the judge.
C) Do not respond at all.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school recommends complete restraint when insulted with a ta'zir-triggering word, but permits recourse to a judge or a mild retort if necessary.

Ruling on Seeking Forgiveness (Istihlal) for Slander
Intra-Hanafi dispute on method of istihlal for slander

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi opinions on the method of istihlal for slander and explain the author's critique of the view that sending a messenger suffices.

Core Idea
Istihlal requires the victim's actual pardon; mere apology without absolution may not remove the sin.

When a person slanders another, the Hanafi school holds that istihlal (seeking forgiveness) is obligatory. But how must it be done? Must the offender go in person, or can he send a messenger? This lesson examines an intra-madhhab dispute on this very question, along with a critical analysis from the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
List the two opinions: (1) from al-Qunyah stating istihlal is obligatory upon both parties (implying personal meeting?), and (2) from the 'great sheikh' holding that personal presence is not required; sending a messenger suffices to acquit oneself.
Passage does not explicitly state al-Qunyah's view on method, but it contrasts with the sheikh's concession.
2
Analyze the author's critique
The author finds the sheikh's view problematic because it implies sin leaves by merely going or sending, regardless of whether the victim actually pardons. The sin should remain until ibra' (absolution) occurs.
This critique highlights the principle that the victim's pardon is necessary to remove the sin.
3
Evaluate the proposed concession
The author suggests a counter-argument: since absolution is not in the offender's power, he does what he can (i.e., seeking reconciliation), and that might lift the sin. This is presented as a possible relaxation but not a settled ruling.
This demonstrates the Hanafi usul principle of 'taklif ma la yutaq' (burdening beyond capacity is not required).
4
Determine the mufta bihi
Based on the author's critique, the stricter view (personal presence required or at least actual pardon) seems preferred, but the text leaves the matter open with 'unless it is said...'. Therefore, the operative ruling is ambiguous; one should follow the position that ensures sin is removed (i.e., securing actual ibra').
Many later Hanafi authorities required personal presence unless impossible, but the passage does not settle it definitively.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي القنية : تشاتما يجب الاستحلال عليهما. وعن الشيخ الجليل : المتكلم إن من شتم غيره أو ضربه فالذهاب إليه في الاستحلال لا يجب عليه ويخرج عن العهدة بالإرسال إليه اهـ. وهو مشكل لأنه يقتضي أنه يزول عنه المأثم بمجرد الذهاب أو الإرسال، سواء حالله أو أبرأه أو لا، وينبغي أن يبقى الإثم إلى أن يوجد الإبراء إلا أن يقال: إن الإبراء ليس في قدرته وإنما في قدرته طلب المحاللة والإبراء وقد أتى بما في وسعه.
And in al-Qunyah: When two exchange insults, istihlal (seeking forgiveness) is obligatory upon them. And from the great sheikh: The speaker, if he insulted or hit another, going to him for istihlal is not obligatory upon him, and he acquits himself by sending [a messenger] to him. End. And it is problematic because it implies that sin leaves him merely by going or sending, whether the other pardons him or absolves him or not, and it should be that sin remains until absolution occurs, unless it is said: Absolution is not in his power; rather, seeking reconciliation and absolution is in his power, and he has done what is in his capacity.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute within the Hanafi school regarding the method of seeking forgiveness after slander. Al-Qunyah states that istihlal is obligatory upon both parties. However, a great sheikh (referred to as 'al-shaykh al-jalil') holds that the offender is not required to go in person; sending a messenger suffices. The author of the commentary (likely the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq) finds this problematic because the sin should not be removed unless the victim actually pardons. He suggests a concession: since the offender cannot compel forgiveness, he does what he can, and the sin might be lifted due to his effort? The text leaves it as 'except that it may be said...' indicating a relaxation but not a settled ruling.

Key Terms
استحلال (istihlal): Seeking forgiveness or absolution from the person who was wronged.
العهدة (al-'uhda): Moral responsibility or liability for the sin.
الإبراء (al-ibra'): Absolution/discharge from the claim of the victim.
القنية (al-Qunyah): A Hanafi fiqh work by al-Faqih al-Samarqandi.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, why is the view that sending a messenger suffices for istihlal considered problematic?
A) Because the messenger might not convey the apology sincerely.
B) Because it implies the sin is removed even without the victim's actual pardon.
C) Because the slanderer must also ask forgiveness from the judge.
D) Because it contradicts the Quranic command to seek forgiveness directly.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, istihlal for slander is wajib, but whether personal presence is required remains debated; the safer view is that sin is not removed until the victim actually pardons.

Ta'zir: Personal Right, Divine Right, or Both?
Dispute: ta'zir as personal vs. divine right, with reconciliation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two positions within the Hanafi school on the nature of ta'zir (Fath al-Qadir vs. al-Khaniyyah) and explain how the commentary reconciles them, with practical implications for pardon and enforcement.

Core Idea
Ta'zir combines both rights, but the personal right predominates.

The discretionary punishment (ta'zir) raises a fundamental question: is it a personal right of the victim that can be pardoned, or a divine right that the imam must enforce? The Hanafi school contains two views. This lesson unpacks the debate between Fath al-Qadir and al-Khaniyyah, how the commentator resolves it, and what that means for a judge or victim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
First, extract the view of al-Khaniyyah (ta'zir is purely a servant's right) and the view of Fath al-Qadir (ta'zir is divided into rights of Allah and rights of the servant). Note the key phrases.
Al-Khaniyyah's phrase: 'التعزير حق العبد كسائر حقوقه'
2
Analyze the reasoning behind each view
Al-Khaniyyah focuses on the victim's ability to pardon and use procedural devices. Fath al-Qadir argues that the offense (e.g., insult) is a sin, thus involving Allah's right. Understand the dalil: the sinfulness of the act.
Fath al-Qadir uses the logic: every sin is a violation of Allah's right.
3
Examine the reconciliation offered by the commentator
The commentator from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq suggests that 'right of the servant' can be interpreted as a circumstantial qualifier or substitute, and that most ta'zir cases combine both rights. He also notes that al-Khaniyyah's statement can be interpreted as referring only to cases where the offense is not a sin (e.g., ta'zir of a child).
The reconciliation preserves both views; the mufta bihi is that both rights are present unless the act is not sinful.
4
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
Consider a scenario: X insults Y, and Y forgives X. According to al-Khaniyyah, the case ends. According to the reconciled view, the imam may still impose a minor ta'zir or admonition because the sin (insult) violated Allah's right, unless the imam is sure deterrence is achieved.
The practical difference is whether the imam can override the pardon when the right of Allah is involved.

Source Text

وفي الخانية: التعزير حق العبد كسائر حقوقه يجوز فيه الإبراء والعفو والشهادة على الشهادة ويجري فيه اليمين يعني إذا أنكر أنه سبه يحلف ويقضي بالنكول . قال في فتح القدير : ولا يخفى على أحد أنه ينقسم إلى ما هو حق العبد وحق الله تعالى فحق العبد لا شك أنه يجري فيه ما ذكر، وأما ما وجب حقاً الله تعالى فقد قدمنا أنه يجب على الإمام إقامته ولا يحل له تركه إلا فيما علم أنه انزجر منه وقدم أنه بغیر صريح يعزر في معرض العرف وقال في الشرنبلالية نقل التصريح بوجوب الحد بقوله يابن القحبة في منح الغفار من المضمرات .اهـ. وهذا يدل على ذلك إذ لا فرق يظهر بين القحبة وابن القحبة تأمل. قوله : (وفي القنية تشائما يجب الاستحلال عليهما) انظر هذا ما مع عند قوله ولو قال يا زاني و عكس حداً حيث قال : لو قال له يا خبيث فقال له الآخر بل أنت تكافاً ولا يعزر كل منهما الآخر إلا أن يحمل ما هنا على ما إذا تخالفت ألفاظهما بأن أجابه بيا فاسق مثلاً تأمل. قوله : (قال في فتح القدير ولا يخفى الخ) اعتراض على عبارة الخانية حيث حصرت التعزير بحق العبد، ويمكن الجواب عنها بأن حق العبد منصوب على الحالية أو مرفوع على البدلية من التعزير وقوله «كسائر حقوقه» خبر المبتدأ وهو التعزير . قلت : وما ذكره في الفتح من أنه ينقسم إلى ما هو حق الله تعالى وحق العبد يدخل فيه قسم ثالث وهو ما اجتمع فيه الحقان بل الظاهر أن كل ما هو حق العبد يكون فيه حق الله تعالى لأن جنايته على العبد بالشتم أو الضرب معصية ولذا قال في الدرر : وهو أي التعزير له حق العبد غالب فيه نعم قد يكون غير معصية كتعزير الصبي . قوله : (فإن قلت في فتاوى قاضيخان الخ) وارد على قوله وأما ما كان منه حقاً الله تعالى يجب على الإمام إقامته كما أوضحه بقوله «وهذا يجب أن يكون الخ. وحاصل الجواب أن حمل كلام الخانية على أن المراد به ما كان من حقوق الله تعالى ممكن كما ذكره السائل ولا يناقض ما مر لأن جره إلى باب القاضي والدعوى تعزير له لكونه ذا مروءة، وكذا يمكن حمله على أن المراد به ما كان حق آدمي لما قلنا قوله : (ولا) مناقضة الخ) أقول : يمكن دفع المناقضة أوجه أخر من أن وهو من كان ذا مروءة أي ذا ديانة وصلاح كما يأتي لا يصدر منه موجب التعزير غالباً إلا على وجه السهو أو الغفلة نادراً ولذا لو عاد يعزر، وإذا كان المقصود من التعزير الانزجار فهو حاصل من ذي المروءة فلذا قالوا: إنه لا يعزر في أول مرة بل يوعظ فلعله لا يعلم ذلك، وقد مر استثناء ما إذا علم الإمام انزجار الفاعل قوله : (لا يحلفه بالله ما قلت الخ) أي لاحتمال صدقه فيما نسبه إليه ولا يمكنه إثباته قوله : (فهذا كله يدل على أن العفو للإمام جائز) قد يقال عليه إن المقصد من شرعية التعزير هو الانزجار فعفو الإمام عنه تضييع للمقصود فلا يجوز.
And in al-Khaniyyah: Ta'zir is a right of the servant like all his rights; it is permissible therein to absolve, pardon, and bear witness upon witness, and the oath applies—meaning if he denies that he insulted him, he swears and judgment is given by refusal (nukul). He said in Fath al-Qadir: It is not hidden from anyone that it is divided into what is a right of the servant and a right of Allah. As for the right of the servant, undoubtedly what was mentioned applies. As for what is obligatory as a right of Allah, we have stated that it is obligatory for the imam to enforce it and it is not permissible for him to leave it except where he knows that the doer has desisted. And it was stated that with other than explicit (sarih), he is punished by ta'zir in the context of custom. And he said in al-Shurnabulaliyya: The explicit expression of obligation of hadd by saying 'ya ibn al-qahba' is transmitted from Manh al-Ghaffar from al-Mudmarat. End. And that indicates this, for no difference appears between 'qahba' and 'ibn al-qahba'. Reflect. His statement: (And in al-Qunyah: 'When they exchange insults, istihlal is obligatory upon them') Look at this, what is the meaning of his statement: 'And if he said 'O zani' and reversed hadd' where he said: 'If he said to him 'O khabith' and the other said 'Rather you', then it is a retaliation (takaful) and neither disciplines the other unless it is taken that here it refers to when their words differ, such as replying with 'O fasiq' for example. Reflect.' His statement: (He said in Fath al-Qadir: 'It is not hidden...') is an objection to the statement of al-Khaniyyah where it restricted ta'zir to the right of the servant. And it is possible to reply that 'right of the servant' is in the accusative case as a circumstantial qualifier (hal) or nominative as a substitute (badal) for ta'zir, and his phrase 'like all his rights' is the predicate of the subject which is ta'zir. I say: And what is mentioned in al-Fath that it is divided into what is a right of Allah and a right of the servant includes a third category: that in which both rights are combined. Rather, it is apparent that every ta'zir that is a right of the servant contains a right of Allah because his transgression against the servant by insult or beating is a sin. Hence it is said in al-Durar: It—meaning ta'zir—has predominance of the right of the servant in it. Yes, it may not be a sin, like the ta'zir of a child. His statement: (If you say in Fatawa Qadikhan...) is an attack on his statement 'As for that which is a right of Allah, it is obligatory for the imam to enforce it' as he clarified by his saying 'This must be...'. The summary of the reply is that interpreting the statement of al-Khaniyyah as referring to what is from the rights of Allah is possible as the questioner mentioned, and it does not contradict what has passed, because dragging him to the court and the claim is a ta'zir for him due to his being a man of integrity (muru'a). Likewise, it can be interpreted as referring to what is a right of a human being due to what we said. His statement: (There is no contradiction...) I say: The contradiction can be repelled in other ways: that he who is a man of integrity—i.e., religious and righteous as will come—does not generally commit what necessitates ta'zir except rarely out of absentmindedness or negligence; therefore, if he repeats, he is punished by ta'zir. And if the purpose of ta'zir is deterrence, it is achieved in the case of a man of integrity. Hence they said: He is not punished in the first time but is advised, for perhaps he was unaware. And the exception has passed when the imam knows that the doer has desisted. His statement: (He does not make him swear by Allah 'I did not say this'...) i.e., due to the possibility of his truth in what he attributed to him, and he cannot prove it. His statement: (All this indicates that pardon for the imam is permissible.) It might be said against it that the purpose of legislating ta'zir is deterrence; the imam's pardon would be a waste of that purpose, so it is not permissible.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This extensive passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses the nature of ta'zir. Al-Khaniyyah considers ta'zir solely a right of the servant (i.e., the victim), allowing forgiveness, pardon, and testimony on testimony. Fath al-Qadir objects, stating ta'zir is divided into rights of Allah and rights of the servant. The commentator then discusses the interaction: most ta'zir cases involve both rights, as the offense is a sin (right of Allah) and a violation against a person (right of servant). The discussion further includes the case of a man of honor (muru'a) who should be advised first, the issue of pardoning by the imam, and a reference to rulings from Qadi Khan and others. The debate concludes with the permissibility of pardon by the imam, though a final remark questions it as defeating the purpose of deterrence.

Key Terms
التعزير (al-ta'zir): Discretionary punishment; in this passage, it is discussed as either a right of the servant or a right of Allah.
حق العبد (haqq al-'abd): The personal right of the victim to demand punishment or pardon the offender.
حق الله (haqq Allah): The divine right that requires enforcement of punishment for the sake of public order and deterrence.
مروءة (muru'a): Honor, integrity, and religious propriety; a person of muru'a is not expected to commit ta'zir-worthy acts repeatedly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the implication of ta'zir involving both the right of Allah and the right of the servant?
A) The victim can always pardon and the judge cannot intervene.
B) The judge must enforce the punishment and cannot accept pardon from the victim.
C) The judge must enforce the punishment but the victim's pardon is still effective for the personal aspect, and the judge may consider deterrence.
D) The punishment must be carried out regardless of the victim's wishes.

Key Takeaway
The prevalent Hanafi view is that ta'zir combines both a personal right and a divine right; the victim's pardon does not automatically prevent the imam from imposing a deterrent punishment.

Ta'zir for Insulting Words: Three Hanafi Positions and the Imam's Pardon
Discussion of which insults incur ta'zir and whether the imam may pardon

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on ta'zir for obviously false insults (e.g., 'ya kalb') and explain the Hanafi rule on the imam's authority to pardon ta'zir.

Core Idea
Obvious falsehoods like 'O dog' do not incur ta'zir, but custom and victim status create Hanafi disagreements.

This lesson examines the Hanafi legal treatment of insulting words that are clearly false. While the apparent position (zahir al-riwaya) holds that such words do not incur ta'zir because the lie is self-evident, other Hanafi authorities argue that the customary insult and the victim's dignity necessitate punishment. We also explore the imam's discretionary power to pardon ta'zir, which is tied to the purpose of deterrence.

Guided Path
1
State the central issue
Identify the legal question: Do obviously false insults (e.g., calling someone 'O dog') incur ta'zir?
The issue arises because the truth of the insult is impossible, yet custom treats it as insulting.
2
Identify the Zahir al-Riwaya position
State the well-known Hanafi view: no ta'zir for such insults because the falsehood is certain, so no shame attaches to the victim.
Dalil: certain negation of the attribute precludes shame (al-Hidayah).
3
Analyze the opposing position of al-Hindwani and the three imams
Explain why they hold ta'zir is due: these words are used in custom as insults, so they cause shame and require punishment.
Opposing dalil: custom (urf) overrides reality; the social effect matters.
4
Examine the detailed position of al-Hidayah
Describe the middle path: ta'zir is applied via istihsan if the victim is of noble status (dhu muru'a), otherwise not.
This is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position.
5
Apply the rule on the imam's pardon
Determine when the imam may pardon ta'zir: for rights of Allah, if the imam knows the offender is already deterred; for rights of servants, pardon rests with the victim.
Deterrence is the purpose; if achieved, pardon is permitted.

كتاب الحدود باب حد القذف الفاعل قبل ذلك . ثم يجب أن يتفرع عليه أنه يجوز إثباته بمدع شهد به فيكون مدعياً شاهداً إذا كان معه آخر. فإن قلت : في فتاوى قاضيخان وغيره إن كان المدعى عليه ذا مروءة وكان أول ما إذا علم أنه انزجر الفاعل قبل ذلك. ويدل عليه أيضاً من أنه إذا كان الشاتم ذا مروءة وعظ، وقد علمت أن ذلك لحصول الانزجار من ذي المروءة فهذا في الشتم الذي هو حق عبد واكتفى فيه بالوعظ فكيف في حق الله تعالى؟
