لأنه زاجر مشروع لحق الله تعالى فوجب كالحد وما علم أنه انزجر بدونه لا يجب. قوله : (ثلاثة مذاهب الأول ظاهر الرواية والثاني مختار الهندواي، والثالث ما يأتي عن صاحب الهداية من التفصيل. قوله : (كأنه لعدم ظهور الكذب الخ قال في النهر : ما ذكره من الفرق مدفوع بأن الحكم بتعزيره غير مقيد بموت أبيه .اهـ. قلت والظاهر في وجه الفرق أن قوله يا یابن الحجام فيه نسبة إلى غير أبيه فكان القياس لزوم الحد فيه لكنه في العرف يراد به الخسة والدناءة
Book of Hudud, Chapter of Slander (Hadd al-Qadhf). The doer before that. Then it must branch from it that it is permissible to establish it by a claimant who testified, so he becomes a claimant-witness if he has another. If you say: In Fatawa Qadikhan and others: If the defendant is a person of muru'a (dignity) and it is his first action, he is admonished as an istihsan and not punished by ta'zir; if he returns and repeats, it is narrated from Abu Hanifa that he is struck. And this must be in the rights of Allah, for the rights of servants the judge cannot drop ta'zir. I say: It is possible that its interpretation is from the rights of Allah, and there is no contradiction because if he is a person of muru'a, his ta'zir has been achieved by being dragged to the judge's court and the lawsuit, so it does not drop the right of Allah in ta'zir. And his saying 'and he is not punished by ta'zir' means by striking the first time; if he returns, then he is punished by ta'zir then by striking. And it is possible that its interpretation is the right of a human from insult, and he is one whose ta'zir is as we mentioned. And it is narrated from Muhammad concerning a man who insults people: if he is a person of muru'a, he is admonished; if he is less than that, he is imprisoned; if he is a habitual insulter, he is struck and imprisoned, meaning the one less than that. And muru'a in my view is in religion and righteousness. End of what is in Fath al-Qadir. And in al-Khulasa: If one claims against him that he said to him 'O immoral, O heretic, O disbeliever, O hypocrite, O wicked' or what necessitates ta'zir, he does not make him swear by Allah that you did not say this, but he makes him swear by Allah that you have no right against him that he claims, mentioned in the manner of seeking release. And in al-Qunyah: Ta'zir does not fall by repentance. And in Mushkil al-Athar: The establishment of ta'zir is up to the imam according to Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and al-Shafi'i, and pardon is also up to him. Al-Tahawi said: In my view, pardon is fixed for the one who was harmed, not for the imam. He (the author) said, may Allah be pleased with him: Perhaps what they said that pardon is up to the imam is that in ta'zir that is obligatory as a right of Allah, because he committed a wrong not having a prescribed hadd without harming a person, and what al-Tahawi said is in the case where he harmed a person. End of what is in al-Qunyah. So all this indicates that pardon for the imam is permissible, and it contradicts what is in Fath al-Qadir. From Allah because his transgression against the servant by insult or beating is disobedience, hence it is said in al-Durar: And it (ta'zir) is mostly a right of the servant; indeed it may be not disobedience like ta'zir of a child. His saying: (If you say in Fatawa Qadikhan etc.) comes upon his saying: And as for what is a right of Allah, it is obligatory for the imam to establish it, as he clarified with his saying: 'And this must be...' and the summary of the answer is that the interpretation of the words of al-Khaniyya is that what is meant by it is that which is from the rights of Allah is possible as the questioner mentioned, and it does not contradict what passed because his being dragged to the judge's court and the lawsuit is a ta'zir for him due to his being of muru'a. Likewise it is possible to interpret it as meaning that which is a human right because of what we said. His saying: (None) contradiction etc.) I say: It is possible to refute the contradiction in other ways: that he who is of muru'a, i.e., of piety and righteousness as will come, usually does not commit an act requiring ta'zir except rarely by way of oversight or heedlessness, so if he returns, he is punished. And if the purpose of ta'zir is deterrence, that is achieved from the person of muru'a, so they said: He is not punished by ta'zir the first time but admonished; perhaps he does not know that. And there has passed the exception of the case where the imam knows the doer is deterred. His saying: (He does not make him swear by Allah that you did not say etc.) means because of the possibility of his truthfulness in what he attributed to him and he cannot prove it. His saying: (So all this indicates that pardon for the imam is permissible) it may be objected that the purpose of legislating ta'zir is deterrence, so the imam's pardon for it is a waste of the purpose, so it is not permissible absolutely. Because he increased ta'zir? And saying 'O dog, O billy-goat, O donkey, O pig, O cow, O snake, O cupper, O effeminate, O prostitute, O illegitimate child, O rascal, O conceited, O abased, O mockery, O laughter, O lazy, O dullard, O possessed'? His saying: (And saying O dog, O billy-goat, O donkey, O pig, O cow, O snake, O cupper, O effeminate, O prostitute, O illegitimate child, O rascal, O conceited, O abased, O mockery, O laughter, O lazy, O dullard, O possessed, no) means no ta'zir is incurred by these expressions. As for the lack of ta'zir in 'O dog, O donkey, O pig, O cow, O snake, O billy-goat, O wolf, O monkey', it is because of the obviousness of his lie. He said in al-Hawi al-Qudsi: The principle is that every shameful word that returns to the insulter does not incur ta'zir; if the shame returns to the insulted, it incurs ta'zir. And he explained it in al-Hidayah: because he did not attach shame to him due to certainty of its negation. And concerning these expressions there are three schools: The apparent position (zahir al-riwaya) is that he is not punished (as we mentioned). Al-Hindwani chose that he is punished by ta'zir, and it is the saying of the three imams because these expressions are used for insult in our custom. In Fatawa Qadikhan regarding 'O dog' it says: And from al-Faqih Abu Ja'far that he is punished because he is a liar? He said: And the correct is that it is not an insult conclusively. End. And in al-Mabsut: Indeed the Arabs because he is a liar? they consider it an insult, hence they name (people) 'dog' and 'wolf'. And Qadikhan mentioned from the Amaali of Abu Yusuf concerning 'O pig, O donkey' that he is punished, then he said: And in a narration from Muhammad he is not punished, and it is the correct one. And the author of al-Hidayah considered istihsan for ta'zir if the addressed is from the nobles, and he was followed in al-Tabyin. And in Fath al-Qadir he equated between saying 'O cupper' and saying 'O son of a cupper' in not being so in lack of ta'zir? And he differentiated between them in al-Tabyin and obligated ta'zir in 'O son of a cupper' but not in 'O cupper', as if because of the lack of obviousness of the lie in saying 'O son of a cupper' due to his father's death, so the listeners do not know his lie, so shame attaches to him, contrary to saying 'O cupper' because they observe his craft. And as for 'bigha' - with a single baa and emphatic ghain - it is 'ma'boon' in Persian, and it is said 'bagha', and as if it is derived from al-bigha' (prostitution); thus in al-Mughrib. And it is appropriate that ta'zir be obligatory in it by agreement because it attaches shame to him due to the lack of obviousness of the lie in it outwardly because it is hidden, and it means 'O ma'fooj', which is the one who is penetrated in the anus. And it has been said: The meaning is that he has pardon if he sees that deterrence is achieved without it, hence he said in al-Fath: Except if he knows that the doer was deterred before that. And it is also indicated by the fact that if the insulter is of muru'a, he is admonished. And you have known that that is for the achievement of deterrence from the person of muru'a. So this is in insult that is a right of a servant, and they sufficed with admonition, so how about in the right of Allah? And it was mentioned in al-Fath at the beginning of the chapter that what is expressly stated of ta'zir, such as having intercourse with his wife's slave-girl or a jointly owned slave-girl, must be obeyed; and what is not expressly stated, if the imam sees the maslaha after avoiding his own desires, or he knows that he will not be deterred except by it, then it becomes obligatory because it is a legislated deterrent for the right of Allah, so it becomes obligatory like the hadd; and what he knows that he is deterred without it, it is not obligatory. His saying: (Three schools: first, the apparent position; second, the choice of al-Hindwani; third, what comes from the author of al-Hidayah of detail.) His saying: (As if because of the lack of obviousness of the lie etc.) He said in al-Nahr: What he mentioned of the distinction is refuted because the ruling of his ta'zir is not conditioned by the death of his father. End. I say: And the apparent in the face of the distinction is that his saying 'O son of a cupper' contains attribution to someone other than his father, so analogically the hadd would be necessary in it, but in custom it is intended for baseness and lowliness.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq deals with ta'zir (discretionary punishment) for insulting speech, specifically which words incur ta'zir and which do not, and the scope of the imam's authority to pardon. The discussion is part of the chapter on slander (had al-qadhf), but it primarily addresses ta'zir for insults that do not reach the level of hadd. The author first addresses a scenario from Fatawa Qadikhan: if the defendant is a person of dignity (dhu muru'a) and it is his first offense, he is only admonished; if he repeats, he is struck according to Abu Hanifa. This applies to rights of Allah, as rights of servants cannot be dropped by the judge. The commentary reconciles this by noting that the very act of being brought to court is itself a ta'zir for a person of dignity. There is also a narration from Imam Muhammad that differentiates based on the person's status. Then the text discusses oaths in such cases. It also addresses the question of whether ta'zir is dropped by repentance; it is not. There is a disagreement between the earlier Hanafi authorities and al-Tahawi regarding who has the right to pardon: the imam or the victim. The preferred view is that the imam may pardon if the purpose of deterrence is achieved. The passage then lists many insulting terms like 'dog', 'donkey', etc., and states that according to the apparent position (zahir al-riwaya), they do not incur ta'zir because the falsity is obvious. However, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: al-Hindwani and the three imams say they do incur ta'zir because in custom they are insults. The author of al-Hidayah differentiates: if the victim is noble, ta'zir is applied; otherwise not. There is also a distinction between 'O cupper' and 'O son of a cupper'—the latter incurs ta'zir because the lie is not apparent. The term 'bigha' (effeminate) always incurs ta'zir by agreement as it is a hidden defect. The passage concludes with the principle that the imam's duty is to apply ta'zir when necessary for deterrence, and he may pardon if deterrence is already achieved.

Key Terms
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment for offenses not covered by hadd or qisas, determined by the imam
مروءة (muru'a): Personal dignity, piety, and social standing that prevents a person from committing offenses
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwaya): The apparent or well-known position transmitted from Abu Hanifa through his students in the six authoritative books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Zahir al-Riwaya, what is the ruling for saying 'يا كلب' (O dog) to someone?
A) It incurs ta'zir because it is an insult
B) It does not incur ta'zir because the falsity is obvious
C) It incurs hadd al-qadhf
D) It is entirely permissible

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has three positions on ta'zir for obviously false insults, with the relied-upon view being no ta'zir unless the victim is of high status.

Ta'zir for Insulting Words: The Three Hanafi Positions
Explains three Hanafi views on ta'zir for insulting words.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the three Hanafi positions on ta'zir for common insults and apply the principle of shin (shame) to determine whether ta'zir is due.

Core Idea
Ta'zir for an insult depends on shame: if shame sticks to the victim, ta'zir is wajib; if it rebounces to the speaker, no ta'zir.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq examines which insulting words require ta'zir when they do not meet the conditions for hadd al-qadhf. The Hanafi school has three distinct positions, rooted in the principle of whether the shame attaches to the insulted person or returns to the insulter. Understanding this principle is key to applying ta'zir correctly in cases of verbal abuse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the governing principle
From al-Hawi al-Qudsi: if the shame of the word returns to the speaker (i.e., the word is obviously false), no ta'zir; if the shame attaches to the victim, ta'zir is due. This is based on certainty of falsehood.
The principle of shin (shame) is central to Hanafi reasoning here.
2
State the Zahir al-Riwayah position
No ta'zir for common animal insults (like 'ya kalb', 'ya himar') because falsehood is apparent—everyone knows the speaker is lying, so shame returns to the speaker.
This is the most authoritative transmission from Imam Muhammad.
3
State the position of al-Hindwani and the three Imams
Ta'zir is given because in custom these words are used for insult, so shame attaches to the victim, regardless of apparent falsehood.
Al-Hindwani and Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad (the three Imams) hold this view.
4
Explain the tafsil position from al-Hidayah
Ta'zir is given only if the victim is from the nobility (ashraf), because the shame is more acute; otherwise no ta'zir.
This is a middle view brought by al-Marghinani.
5
Apply the principle to specific words
Work through examples like 'ya bagha' (unanimous ta'zir), 'ya walad al-haram' (ta'zir because it implies adultery but not explicit), 'ya 'ayyar' (no ta'zir because condition is apparent).
Each word is evaluated for whether the shame attaches to the victim.

Source Text

كتاب الحدود باب حد القذف مطلقاً لما زاده عزر و بیا کلب یا تیس یا حمار یا خنزير يا بقر يا حية يا حجام یا بغا یا مؤاجر یا ولد الحرام یا عیار یا ناکس یا منکوس يا سخرة يا ضحكة يا كشحان يا أبله يا موسوس لا أي لا يعزر بهذه الألفاظ أما عدم التعزير في يا كلب يا حمار يا خنزير يا بقر يا حية يا تيس یا ذئب یا قرد فلظهور كذبه. قال في الحاوي القدسي : الأصل أن كل عاد شينه إلى الساب فإنه لا يعزر، فإن عاد الشين فيه إلى المسبوب عزر. وعلله في الهداية بأنه ما ألحق الشين به للتيقن بنفيه. وفي هذه الألفاظ ثلاثة مذاهب: ظاهر الرواية أنه لا يعزر ذكرنا واختار الهندواني أنه يعزر به وهو قول الأئمة الثلاثة لأن هذه الألفاظ تذكر للشتيمة في عرفنا. وفي فتاوى قاضيخان في يا كلب لا قال : وعن الفقيه أبي جعفر أنه يعزر لأنه يعزر قال : والصحيح أنه لا شتيمة ثم قطعاً اهـ. وفي المبسوط : فإن العرب لا لأنه كاذب يعزر تعده شتيمة ولهذا يسمون بكلب وذئب . وذكر قاضيخان عن أمالي أبي يوسف في يا خنزير يا حمار يعزر ثم قال: وفي رواية محمد لا يعزر وهو الصحيح، وصاحب الهداية استحسن التعزير إذا كان المخاطب من الأشراف وتبعه في التبيين، وسوى في فتح القدير بين قوله يا حجام وبين قوله يابن الحجام حيث لم يكن كذلك في عدم التعزير وفرق بينهما في التبيين فأوجب التعزير في يابن الحجام دون يا حجام كأنه لعدم ظهور الكذب في قوله يابن الحجام لموت أبيه فالسامعون لا يعلمون كذبه فلحقه الشين بخلاف قوله له يا حجام لأنهم يشاهدون صنعته . وأما بغا - بالباء الموحدة والغين المعجمة المشددة - فهو المأبون بالفارسية ويقال باغا وكأنه انتزع من البغاء ؛ كذا في المغرب. وينبغي أن يجب التعزير فيه اتفاقاً لأنه ألحق الشين به لعدم ظهور الكذب فيه ظاهراً لأنه مما يخفى وهو بمعنى يا معفوج وهو المأتي في الدبر. وقد فالمراد أن له العفو إذا رأى حصول الانزجار بدونه فلذا قال في الفتح إلا إذا علم أنه انزجر الفاعل قبل ذلك. ويدل عليه أيضاً من أنه إذا كان الشاتم ذا مروءة وعظ، وقد علمت أن ذلك لحصول الانزجار من ذي المروءة فهذا في الشتم الذي هو حق عبد واكتفى فيه بالوعظ فكيف في حق الله تعالى؟ وذكر في الفتح أول الباب أن ما نص عليه من التعزير كما في وطء جارية امرأته أو جارية مشتركة يجب امتثال الأمر فيه وما لم ينص عليه إذا رأى الإمام المصلحة بعد مجانبة هوى نفسه أو علم أنه لا ينزجر إلا به وجب لأنه زاجر مشروع لحق الله تعالى فوجب كالحد وما علم أنه انزجر بدونه لا يجب. قوله : (ثلاثة مذاهب الأول ظاهر الرواية والثاني مختار الهندواي، والثالث ما يأتي عن صاحب الهداية من التفصيل. قوله : (كأنه لعدم ظهور الكذب الخ قال في النهر : ما ذكره من الفرق مدفوع بأن الحكم بتعزيره غير مقيد بموت أبيه .اهـ. قلت والظاهر في وجه الفرق أن قوله يا یابن الحجام فيه نسبة إلى غير أبيه فكان القياس لزوم الحد فيه لكنه في العرف يراد به الخسة والدناءة صرح في الظهيرية بوجوب التعزير فيه معللاً بأنه ألحق الشين به بل هو أقوى إيذاء لأن الابنة في العرف عيب شديد إذ لا يقدر على ترك أن يؤتى في دبره بسبب دودة ونحوها، وأما المؤاجر فإن كان بكسر الجيم فهو بمعنى المؤجر للشيء ولا عيب فيه إلا أن هذا اللفظ لهذا المعنى في اللغة خطأ ،وقبيح، وإن كان بفتح الجيم بمعنى المؤجر بالفتح يقال آجره المملوك فاسم المفعول مؤجر ومؤاجر كذا في المغرب - فقد نسبه إلى أن غيره قد استأجره ولا عيب فيه، سواء كان صادقاً أو كاذباً، لأنها عقد شرعي. وأما ولد الحرام فينبغي التعزير به لأنه في العرف بمعنى يا ولد الزنا ولم يجب القذف لأنه ليس بصريح وقد ألحق الشين به. وقد أبدله في فتح القدير بيا ولد الحمار وهذا هو الظاهر. وأما العيار - بالعين المهملة المفتوحة والياء المثناة التحتية المشددة - فهو كثير المجيء والذهاب. عن ابن دريد وعن ابن الأنباري : العيار من الرجال الذي يخلي نفسه وهواها لا يردعها ولا يزجرها. وفي أجناس الناطفي : الذي يتردد بلا عمل وهو مأخوذ من قولهم فرس عائر وعيار ؛ كذا في المغرب. وكأنه لما كان أمر الإنسان ظاهراً من التردد أو كثرة المجيء والذهاب لم يلحق الشين به فلذا لم يعزر. وأما قوله یا ناکس يا منكوس ففي ضياء الحلوم من باب فعل - بكسر العين - النكس الرجل الضعيف، ومن باب فَعَلَ بالفتح يفعل بالضم النكس قلب الشيء على رأسه قال الله تعالى ثم نكسوا على رؤوسهم ﴾ [الأنبياء : ٦٥] .اهـ. فكأنه دعا على المخاطب فلا تعزير فيه لعدم إلحاق الشين به . وأما السخرة بضم السين ففي المغرب : السخري من السخرة وهو ما يتسخر أي يستعمل بغير أجر اهـ. فلا شين فيه بل هو مدح وأما الضحكة - بضم الضاد - فهو الشيء يضحك منه؛ في ضياء الحلوم . ولا يخفى أن المقول له إذا لم يكن كذلك فقد استخف به ومن استخف بغيره عزر فينبغي التعزير به ولذا قال في الولوالجية : لو قال له يا ساحر يا ضحكة يا مقامر كذا فإذا سقط الحد يبقى التعزير كما لو قال لعربي يا نبطي أو لهاشمي لست بهاشمي تأمل. ثم إن الذي رأيته في التبيين هكذا : الألفاظ ومن التي لا توجب التعزير قوله يا رستاقي ويابن الأسود ويابن الحجام وهو ليس كذلك اهـ. فقوله وهو ليس كذلك جملة حالية أي والحال أنه ليس برستاقي ولا ابن الأسود ولا ابن الحجام، وكان المؤلف ظن أن قوله وهو ليس كذلك رد لقوله «ومن الألفاظ التي لا توجب التعزير قوله : (يا معفوج الخ) اسم مفعول من عفج بالعين المهملة والفاء والجيم. قال في التاتارخانية : وهو المضروب في الدبر وهو بمعنى ما فسره به المؤلف. وفي القاموس : عفج يعفج ضرب وجاريته جامعها قوله : (وقد صرح في الظهيرية بوجوب التعزير فيه) أي في قوله يا معفوج وقوله بل هو أقوى إيذاء أي لفظ بغا بمعنى المأبون . قلت : وقد رأيت في التتارخانية صرح يعزر به حيث قال : وفي تجنيس الناصري قال السيد الإمام الأجل : لو قال يا بغا يا مؤاجر يا جيفة في عرفنا فيه التعزير قوله : (وأما قوله يا ناكس الخ قال الباقاني في شرح الملتقى: ناكس ومنكوس على وزن فاعل ومفعول لفظ عجمي والنون في أوله للنفي والكاف منه مفتوح ولفظ كس بأنه
Chapter of Hudud, Section of Qadhf (Defamation). Absolutely, whatever additional (insult) he utters, he is given ta'zir. And (words like) 'O dog', 'O goat', 'O donkey', 'O pig', 'O cow', 'O snake', 'O cupper', 'O catamite', 'O hireling', 'O child of fornication', 'O wanderer', 'O base', 'O inverted', 'O laughingstock', 'O dull', 'O foolish' – no, i.e., he is not given ta'zir for these words. As for the absence of ta'zir in 'O dog', 'O donkey', 'O pig', 'O cow', 'O snake', 'O goat', 'O wolf', 'O monkey' – because his falsehood is apparent. Al-Hawi al-Qudsi said: The principle is that every word whose shame returns to the insulter does not require ta'zir; if the shame attaches to the insulted person, he is given ta'zir. And in al-Hidayah, he explained it that he did not attach shame to him due to certainty of its falsehood. Regarding these words there are three madhhabs: According to Zahir al-Riwayah, he is not given ta'zir as we mentioned; al-Hindwani chose that he is given ta'zir, and that is the saying of the three Imams, because these words are used for insult in our custom. In Fatawa Qadikhan on 'O dog': No, he said: And from al-Faqih Abu Ja'far, that he is given ta'zir because... he said: The correct is that it is not an insult then decisively. In al-Mabsut: The Arabs do not consider it an insult because he is a liar; for this reason they name (people) 'dog' and 'wolf'. Qadikhan mentioned from Amali Abu Yusuf on 'O pig', 'O donkey' that he is given ta'zir, then he said: In the narration of Muhammad, he is not given ta'zir, and that is the correct (position). The author of al-Hidayah considered it commendable (istihsan) to give ta'zir if the addressee is from the nobility, and al-Tabyin followed him. In Fath al-Qadir, he equated saying 'O cupper' and 'O son of the cupper' when not such (nobility) in not giving ta'zir, but al-Tabyin differentiated between them: he obligated ta'zir for 'O son of the cupper' but not for 'O cupper', as if due to lack of apparent falsehood in saying 'O son of the cupper' because his father died, so listeners don't know his falsehood, thus shame attaches to him, unlike saying 'O cupper' because they see his profession. As for 'bugha' – with a single ba and a shadda ghain – it means catamite in Persian, also said 'bagha', as if derived from 'bagha' (fornication); thus in al-Mughrib. It is appropriate that ta'zir is obligatory for it unanimously, because it attaches shame to him due to lack of apparent falsehood, as it is hidden, and it means 'O effeminate one', the one penetrated in the anus. And the meaning is that he has the pardon if he sees deterrence achieved without it; thus he said in al-Fath: 'Except if he knows that the doer was deterred before.' And it also indicates from (the case) that if the insulter is a man of chivalry, he is admonished, and you know that that is due to deterrence from a man of chivalry. This is in insult which is a right of a slave, and admonition suffices in it, so how about in the right of Allah? And in al-Fath at the beginning of the chapter: That which is stipulated regarding ta'zir, as in intercourse with his wife's slave-girl or a jointly owned slave-girl, the command must be obeyed; and that which is not stipulated, if the Imam sees the maslaha after avoiding his own desire, or knows that he will not be deterred except by it, it becomes obligatory because it is a lawful deterrent for the right of Allah, so it becomes obligatory like the hadd; and if he knows that he is deterred without it, it is not obligatory. His saying: (Three madhhabs: first Zahir al-Riwayah, second the choice of al-Hindwani, third what comes from the author of al-Hidayah of the detail.) His saying: (as if due to lack of apparent falsehood, etc.) He said in al-Nahr: What he mentioned of the difference is refuted because the ruling on his ta'zir is not restricted to the death of his father. I say: The apparent in the face of the difference is that his saying 'O son of the cupper' attributes him to someone other than his father, so by analogy the hadd would be required for it, but in custom it is meant as lowliness and meanness. Al-Dhahiriyya explicitly stated the obligation of ta'zir in it, reasoning that it attaches shame to him, rather it is stronger hurt because buggery in custom is a severe defect since he cannot avoid being penetrated due to a worm or similar. As for 'al-mu'ajir' (the hireling): if with kasr of jim, it means the one who hires out a thing, and there is no defect in it, except this word for this meaning in language is incorrect and ugly. If with fatha of jim, meaning the one hired, one says 'ajartuhu al-mamluk' so the object participle is 'mu'jar' and 'mu'ajir'; thus in al-Mughrib – so he has attributed him to that someone else has hired him, and there is no defect in it, whether true or false, because it is a valid contract. As for 'walad al-haram' (child of fornication), ta'zir should be given for it because in custom it means 'O child of adultery', and the hadd is not obligatory because it is not explicit (sarih), and it attaches shame to him. In Fath al-Qadir, he replaced it with 'O child of a donkey', and that is apparent. As for 'al-'ayyar' (wanderer) – with open 'ayn and shadda ya – it means one who comes and goes much. From Ibn Duraid and Ibn al-Anbari: al-'ayyar is a man who follows his desire, not restraining it. In Ajnas al-Natafi: one who roams without work, derived from their saying 'faras 'air' and 'ayyar'; thus in al-Mughrib. It seems that since his matter is apparent from roaming or much coming and going, shame does not attach to him, therefore he is not given ta'zir. As for his saying 'O nakis' and 'O mankus' – in Diya' al-Hulum: from the pattern of fa'ala (with kasr of 'ayn), an-nakis is the weak man; from fa'ala (with fatha) yaf'ulu (with damm), an-naks is turning something on its head, Allah said: 'Then they turned back upon their heads' (Anbiya 65). So it is as if he prayed against the addressee, so no ta'zir for it due to lack of attaching shame. As for 'as-sukhrah' (drudge) with damm of sin – in al-Mughrib: as-sukhri is from sukhrah, which is that which is employed without wage. So there is no shame in it, rather it is praise. As for 'ad-dahkah' (laughingstock) – with damm of dad – it is something laughed at; in Diya' al-Hulum. It is not hidden that if the one addressed is not such, then he has belittled him, and whoever belittles another is given ta'zir; therefore it is appropriate to give ta'zir for it. Thus in al-Wilwaljiyya: if he said to him 'O sorcerer', 'O laughingstock', 'O gambler' – then if the hadd falls, ta'zir remains, as if he said to an Arab 'O Nabati' or to a Hashimi 'You are not Hashimi' – ponder. Then what I saw in al-Tabyin is like this: from the words that do not necessitate ta'zir are his saying 'O rustic', 'O son of a black man', and 'O son of a cupper' when he is not such. So his saying 'when he is not such' is a circumstantial clause meaning while he is not a rustic nor son of a black nor son of a cupper. The author thought that his saying 'when he is not such' is a refutation of his saying 'From the words that do not necessitate ta'zir'. His saying: ('O ma'fuj, etc.') is the object participle from 'afaja with 'ayn and fa and jim. In al-Tatarikhaniyya: it is the one struck in the anus, and it is what the author interpreted. In al-Qamus: 'afaja ya'fiju means he struck, and 'jariyyatahu jama'aha' (he had intercourse with his slave-girl). His saying: (And he explicitly stated in al-Dhahiriyya the obligation of ta'zir in it) i.e., in saying 'O ma'fuj', and his saying 'rather it is stronger hurt' means the word 'bugha' meaning catamite. I say: I have seen in al-Tatarikhaniyya explicit that he is given ta'zir for it, where he said: In Tajnis al-Nasiri, al-Sayyid al-Imam al-Ajal said: If he says 'O bugha', 'O mu'ajir', 'O corpse' in our custom, there is ta'zir. His saying: (As for his saying 'O nakis, etc.') al-Baqqani said in Sharh al-Multaqa: 'nakis' and 'mankus' are Persian words on the pattern of fa'il and maf'ul, the nun at the beginning is for negation, and the kaf is with fatha, and the word is 'kas' meaning...
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses ta'zir (discretionary punishment) for various insulting words when the hadd (fixed punishment) for qadhf (defamation) does not apply. The author explains that some words clearly carry shame (shin) and thus require ta'zir, while others are obviously false and the shame returns to the insulter, so no ta'zir is given. The key principle is from al-Hawi al-Qudsi: if the shame of the word rebounds to the speaker (i.e., the word is so obviously false that it makes the speaker look bad), no ta'zir; if the shame attaches to the person being insulted, ta'zir is required. This is based on the certainty of falsehood: when the insult is patently untrue (like calling someone 'dog' when he is not), no shame sticks. The passage then outlines three Hanafi positions: (1) Zahir al-Riwayah (the dominant position of Imam Muhammad) holds that no ta'zir is needed because the falsehood is apparent. (2) al-Hindwani and the three other Imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad?) actually al-Hindwani chose ta'zir because in custom these are insults. The text says 'the three Imams' meaning the Hanafi founding imams? Actually careful: 'wa ikhtara al-Hindwani annahu yu'zaru bihi wa huwa qawl al-a'immah al-thalathah' – al-Hindwani and the three Imams' view is that ta'zir is given because these words are used for abuse. (3) The author of al-Hidayah (and al-Tabyin) distinguishes based on the status of the addressee: if the person is noble, ta'zir is given because the shame attaches; if not, no ta'zir. The passage then goes through many specific words, applying the principle. For each word, the author notes why shame attaches or not. For example, 'ya bagha' (catamite) unanimously requires ta'zir because the defect is hidden and shame attaches. 'Ya mu'ajir' (hireling) depends on meaning: if it means employer, no defect; if hired worker, no defect because it's a valid contract. 'Ya walad al-haram' (child of fornication) deserves ta'zir because it implies adultery, though not explicit enough for hadd. 'Ya 'ayyar' (wanderer) does not require ta'zir because the condition is apparent (he is known to roam). 'Ya nakis/manqus' (weak/inverted) is a prayer, not an insult. 'Ya sukhrah' (drudge) is praise. 'Ya dahkah' (laughingstock) does attach shame if the person is not actually a laughingstock. The author also notes that if a person given to chivalry insults, mere admonition suffices (since he will be deterred), but if not, ta'zir may be obligatory if necessary for deterrence. This is a detailed expansion of the ruling on ta'zir for verbal abuse, showing the nuanced application of the principle of shin (shame).

Key Terms
التعزير (al-ta'zir): Discretionary punishment for offenses that do not have a fixed hadd or kaffarah, determined by the judge.
الشين (al-shin): Shame or defect that attaches to the insulted person from the abusive word.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The transmitted rulings of Imam Muhammad in his six books, considered the most authoritative within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Zahir al-Riwayah, is ta'zir required for saying 'O donkey' to someone?
A) Yes, because it is an insult.
B) No, because the falsehood is apparent.
C) Yes, if the person is noble.
D) No, because it is not a sin.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's approach to ta'zir for insults is nuanced: the key is whether the shame attaches to the victim, and there are three authoritative positions within the school.

Ta'zir for Insulting Terms: Ma'fuj, Mu'ajir, and Walad al-Haram
States the obligation of ta'zir for certain insulting terms with reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ta'zir for the terms 'ma'fuj', 'mu'ajir', and 'walad al-haram', and distinguish between cases that require ta'zir vs. hadd al-qadhf based on the explicitness of the insult.

Core Idea
Ta'zir is applied to insults that attach disgrace but lack explicit qadhf wording.

In Hanafi fiqh, not all insults incur the fixed hadd punishment for slander (qadhf). Some terms, though insulting, are not explicit accusations of fornication and thus fall under ta'zir—a discretionary punishment. This lesson examines the rulings for three such terms as discussed in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, focusing on the reasoning (ta'lil) behind each ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terms and their rulings
The passage discusses three terms: 'ya ma'fuj' (O sodomized one), 'ya mu'ajir' (O hired one), and 'ya walad al-haram' (O bastard). For each, determine whether ta'zir is wajib, recommended, or not required. Note the ruling for 'mu'ajir' depends on vowelization.
The term 'mu'ajir' with kasr (mu'ajir) is incorrect language and insulting; with fatha (mu'ajar) it is not an insult.
2
Analyze the reasoning (ta'lil) for each ruling
For 'ma'fuj', ta'zir is wajib because it attaches severe disgrace (alhaqa al-shayn). For 'mu'ajir' (with kasr), it is wajib because the incorrect language is considered ugly and insulting. For 'walad al-haram', ta'zir is recommended (yanbaghi) because it implies fornication in custom but is not explicit qadhf.
The text uses 'wajib' for the first two and 'yanbaghi' for the third, indicating a difference in legal strength.
3
Distinguish between ta'zir and hadd al-qadhf
Hadd al-qadhf requires explicit (sarih) accusation of fornication. Terms like 'walad al-haram' are kinayah (implicit) and thus do not trigger hadd, only ta'zir. Understand why the Hanafi school makes this distinction based on the need for certainty in criminal punishment.
This reflects the Hanafi principle that hudud are avoided by doubts (shubuhat).
4
Apply the role of custom (urf) in determining insult
The rulings rely on 'urf (custom) to determine what counts as an insult. For example, 'ma'fuj' is considered a severe defect in custom. 'Walad al-haram' is understood in custom as accusing someone of fornication. This shows how custom influences legal evaluation.
Custom is a source of law in Hanafi fiqh, especially in matters of ta'zir and insult.
5
Summarize the Hanafi position and note ikhtilaf
The Hanafi position is that ta'zir is applied for these terms. However, other schools might require more explicit wording for ta'zir or hadd. Recognize that this is an area of legitimate ikhtilaf.
The cross-madhhab status is mukhtalaf fih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صرح في الظهيرية بوجوب التعزير فيه معللاً بأنه ألحق الشين به بل هو أقوى إيذاء لأن الابنة في العرف عيب شديد إذ لا يقدر على ترك أن يؤتى في دبره بسبب دودة ونحوها، وأما المؤاجر فإن كان بكسر الجيم فهو بمعنى المؤجر للشيء ولا عيب فيه إلا أن هذا اللفظ لهذا المعنى في اللغة خطأ، وقبيح، وإن كان بفتح الجيم بمعنى المؤجر بالفتح يقال آجره المملوك فاسم المفعول مؤجر ومؤاجر كذا في المغرب - فقد نسبه إلى أن غيره قد استأجره ولا عيب فيه، سواء كان صادقاً أو كاذباً، لأنها عقد شرعي. وأما ولد الحرام فينبغي التعزير به لأنه في العرف بمعنى يا ولد الزنا ولم يجب القذف لأنه ليس بصريح وقد ألحق الشين به. وقد أبدله في فتح القدير بيا ولد الحمار وهذا هو الظاهر.
He stated in al-Zahiriyya the obligation of ta'zir in it, reasoning that it attaches disgrace to him; rather it is stronger injury because 'الابنة' (sodomy) in custom is a severe defect since he cannot prevent being penetrated in his anus due to a worm or the like. As for 'المؤاجر': if with kasr of the jim, it means the one who hires out something, and there is no defect in that, except this word for this meaning in language is incorrect and ugly; and if with fatha of the jim, meaning the one who is hired out, as in 'آجره المملوك' so the passive participle is مؤجر and مؤاجر, as in al-Mughrib - then it attributes to him that someone else has hired him, and no defect in that, whether truthful or false, because it is a legitimate contract. As for 'ولد الحرام' (child of unlawful union): ta'zir should be applied because it means in custom 'O son of fornication', but hadd al-qadhf is not obligatory because it is not explicit, and it has attached disgrace to him. He replaced it in Fath al-Qadir with 'ya ibn al-himar' (O son of donkey), and this is apparent.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement on the obligation of ta'zir for certain insulting terms. The commentator discusses three terms: 'يا معفوج' (O sodomized one), 'يا مؤاجر' (O hired one), and 'يا ولد الحرام' (O bastard). For the first term, ta'zir is wajib because it attaches disgrace and is a severe insult. For 'مؤاجر', the ruling depends on pronunciation: if with kasr (مُؤَاجِر) it is incorrect language and thus insulting, but if with fatha (مُؤَاجَر) it simply means someone who is hired, which is not a defect. For 'ولد الحرام', ta'zir is recommended (ينبغي) because it implies fornication and attaches disgrace, but since it is not an explicit accusation of fornication, hadd is not required. The Hanafi position is ta'zir for these terms.

Key Terms
معفوج (ma'fuj): Passive participle from 'afaja: one who is struck in the anus, i.e., sodomized.
مؤاجر (mu'ajir/mu'ajar): With kasr: one who hires out; with fatha: one who is hired. The latter is not an insult.
ولد الحرام (walad al-haram): Child of an unlawful union; i.e., bastard. In custom, it means 'son of fornication'.
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment for offenses that do not have a fixed hadd.
حد القذف (hadd al-qadhf): The fixed punishment for slanderous accusation of fornication.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on saying 'يا معفوج' (O sodomized one)?
A) Hadd al-qadhf is obligatory because it is a clear accusation.
B) Ta'zir is wajib (obligatory) because it attaches disgrace.
C) No punishment because it is not an insult in custom.
D) Ta'zir is recommended but not obligatory.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school applies ta'zir for insults that attach disgrace but are not explicit enough to trigger hadd al-qadhf, using custom to determine severity.

No Ta'zir for Non-Disgraceful Terms: 'Ayyar', 'Nakis', 'Mankus'
Ruling on ta'zir for non-disgraceful terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ta'zir for the terms 'ayyar, nakis, and mankus, and explain why custom ('urf) determines whether ta'zir is required.

Core Idea
Custom determines disgrace; terms that do not attach disgrace in custom do not incur ta'zir.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains that not every harsh-sounding word triggers ta'zir. The commentator analyzes two categories: the term 'ayyar (wanderer) and the pair nakis/mankus (weak/inverted), showing that they do not imply a specific accusation of immorality in common usage. The key is whether the term attaches disgrace (shayn) in custom ('urf). If it does not, no ta'zir is required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terms and their lexical meanings
The passage defines 'ayyar as someone who roams without work, and nakis/mankus as weak or inverted. These meanings are not inherently disgraceful.
Note the difference between nakis (weak man) and mankus (inverted) from the same root with different vowels.
2
Assess disgrace in custom
The author argues that since a person's condition is apparent from roaming or frequent movement, such a description does not attach disgrace (shayn) in customary usage. Similarly, nakis and mankus are like supplications, not accusations.
Custom ('urf) is the standard for determining whether a term is defamatory.
3
Derive the ruling
Because no disgrace is attached, ta'zir (discretionary punishment) is not applicable. The act of calling someone these terms does not incur legal liability.
The ruling is based on talil (causal reasoning) that the absence of disgrace negates ta'zir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العيار - بالعين المهملة المفتوحة والياء المثناة التحتية المشددة - فهو كثير المجيء والذهاب. عن ابن دريد وعن ابن الأنباري : العيار من الرجال الذي يخلي نفسه وهواها لا يردعها ولا يزجرها. وفي أجناس الناطفي : الذي يتردد بلا عمل وهو مأخوذ من قولهم فرس عائر وعيار ؛ كذا في المغرب. وكأنه لما كان أمر الإنسان ظاهراً من التردد أو كثرة المجيء والذهاب لم يلحق الشين به فلذا لم يعزر. وأما قوله يا ناكس يا منكوس ففي ضياء الحلوم من باب فعل - بكسر العين - النكس الرجل الضعيف، ومن باب فَعَلَ بالفتح يفعل بالضم النكس قلب الشيء على رأسه قال الله تعالى ﴿ثم نكسوا على رؤوسهم﴾ [الأنبياء : ٦٥]. اهـ. فكأنه دعا على المخاطب فلا تعزير فيه لعدم إلحاق الشين به .
As for 'العيار' (with open 'ayn and shadda on ya), it is one who comes and goes frequently. From Ibn Duraid and Ibn al-Anbari: al-'ayyar is a man who lets himself and his desires go, not restraining them. In Ajnas al-Natifi: the one who roams without work, derived from their saying 'faras 'a'ir' and 'ayyar'; thus in al-Mughrib. Since a person's condition is apparent from roaming or frequent coming and going, it does not attach disgrace to him, so he is not ta'zir. As for saying 'يا ناكس' (O weak) or 'يا منكوس' (O inverted): in Diya' al-Hulum, from the form fa'ala (with kasr of 'ayn), al-naks means a weak man; and from the form fa'ala (with fatha), al-naks means turning something upside down, as Allah said: 'Then they turned back upon their heads' [21:65]. So it is like a supplication against the addressee, so no ta'zir because no disgrace is attached.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement on terms that do not incur ta'zir. The commentator discusses two terms: 'عيار' (one who roams idly) and 'نَاكِس/مَنْكُوس' (weak/inverted). For 'عيار', the meaning is not disgraceful in custom; it merely describes someone who moves about a lot. For 'نَاكِس' and 'مَنْكُوس', they are like supplications or descriptions of weakness, not specific accusations of immorality. Hence, no ta'zir is required. The Hanafi position is that ta'zir is not wajib for these terms.

Key Terms
عيار ('ayyar): One who roams without work, derived from 'ayyar (wild horse).
نَاكِس (nakis): Weak man, from نكس (kasr 'ayn).
مَنْكُوس (mankus): Inverted, from نكس (fath 'ayn) meaning to turn upside down.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, ta'zir for verbal insults depends on whether the term attaches disgrace according to custom; terms that do not attach disgrace are not punishable.

Ruling on Ta'zir for Terms 'Sukhrah' and 'Dahkah'
Distinguishes between praise and insult for two terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between terms that require ta'zir and those that do not, based on Hanafi criteria of belittlement and custom.

Core Idea
Calling someone a 'laughingstock' may require ta'zir, but calling them 'exploited' is not an insult—custom determines the ruling.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies two terms: 'sukhrah' (forced labor) and 'dahkah' (laughingstock). The author explains that while 'sukhrah' is considered praise, 'dahkah' can be an insult depending on the context. You need to understand the Hanafi principle that ta'zir is applied when a term belittles the addressee, unless the term is true or customary praise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms and their literal meanings
Read the passage to find the definitions of 'sukhrah' (from al-Mughrib: something used without hire) and 'dahkah' (something laughed at).
These lexical definitions are key to the ruling.
2
Understand the Hanafi classification
The author says 'sukhrah' has no disgrace; it is praise. 'Dahkah' is an insult if the addressee is not actually a laughingstock.
The classification depends on custom and truthfulness.
3
Recognize the condition for ta'zir
Ta'zir is required when the term belittles the addressee ('استخف به'). For 'dahkah', this occurs if the person is not really a laughingstock.
The condition is that the statement is false and belittling.
4
Apply the ruling to the two terms
For 'sukhrah': no ta'zir because it is praise. For 'dahkah': ta'zir is wajib when the addressee is not what the term implies.
The ruling is taken from al-Walwaljiyya.
5
Note the broader principle
When hadd (like qadhf) does not apply, ta'zir remains for belittling insults. Examples include calling an Arab 'Nabati' or a Hashimi 'not a Hashimi'.
This shows ta'zir fills gaps where hadd is not triggered.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السخرة بضم السين ففي المغرب : السخري من السخرة وهو ما يتسخر أي يستعمل بغير أجر اهـ. فلا شين فيه بل هو مدح وأما الضحكة - بضم الضاد - فهو الشيء يضحك منه؛ في ضياء الحلوم . ولا يخفى أن المقول له إذا لم يكن كذلك فقد استخف به ومن استخف بغيره عزر فينبغي التعزير به ولذا قال في الولوالجية : لو قال له يا ساحر يا ضحكة يا مقامر كذا فإذا سقط الحد يبقى التعزير كما لو قال لعربي يا نبطي أو لهاشمي لست بهاشمي تأمل.
As for 'السخرة' (with damm of sin): in al-Mughrib, al-sukhri is from al-sukhra, which means something used without hire. So there is no disgrace in it; rather it is praise. As for 'الضحكة' (with damm of dad): it is something laughed at; in Diya' al-Hulum. It is not hidden that if the addressee is not like that, then he has belittled him, and whoever belittles another is ta'zir, so ta'zir should be applied. Hence it is said in al-Walwaljiyya: if one says 'O magician, O laughingstock, O gambler' – so if the hadd falls, ta'zir remains, just as if one says to an Arab 'O Nabati' or to a Hashimi 'You are not a Hashimi' – reflect.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement on two terms: 'سخرة' (forced labor without pay) and 'ضحكة' (laughingstock). For 'سخرة', the meaning is not disgraceful; in fact it is praise because using someone without hire is not an insult. For 'ضحكة', if the person is not actually a laughingstock, the speaker has belittled him, and belittling requires ta'zir. Thus, ta'zir is recommended. The commentator also cites a general principle from al-Walwaljiyya: if hadd (like hadd al-qadhf) does not apply, ta'zir remains for such insults. Examples include calling an Arab 'Nabati' or a Hashimi 'not a Hashimi'.

Key Terms
سخرة (sukhrah): Compulsory labor without pay; used as a term for someone who is exploited.
ضحكة (dahkah): Someone who is laughed at; a laughingstock.

Key Takeaway

Definition of 'Ma'fuj' and Its Ta'zir Ruling
Defines the term 'ma'fuj' and confirms its ta'zir ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the term 'ma'fuj' and state the Hanafi ruling of ta'zir for uttering it.

Core Idea
Calling someone 'ma'fuj' is a severe insult that obligates ta'zir in Hanafi law.

Words matter in Islamic law, especially when they defame and degrade. In this passage, the Hanafi jurist clarifies the meaning of the term 'ma'fuj' and establishes that such an insult is not merely uncivil but carries a binding ta'zir penalty. By parsing the linguistic root and citing authoritative fatwa works, we see how the school defines and penalizes verbal abuse.

Guided Path
1
Isolate the term and its morphology
Identify 'ma'fuj' as a passive participle from the verb 'afaja (ع ف ج). Recognize that the form indicates a person upon whom the action is done.
Passive participle (ism maf'ul) often carries a connotation of being acted upon.
2
Unpack the lexical definition
Consult the dictionary meaning: 'ma'fuj' means 'one struck in the anus' (المضروب في الدبر). This is a literal and disgraceful insult.
al-Qamus defines 'afaja as 'to strike' or 'to have intercourse with a slave girl,' but here it refers specifically to anal strike.
3
Identify the legal classification
Understand that such a term is not a neutral description but a severe insult (a strong injury). Hanafi custom (urf) treats it as disgraceful.
The text compares it to 'bagha' and 'ma'bun', which are similarly insulting.
4
Confirm the ruling
Note that al-Zahiriyya and al-Tatar Khaniyya explicitly declare ta'zir wajib for saying 'ya ma'fuj'. The ruling also extends to similar terms like 'bagha' and 'jifa' in customary usage.
The commentator (Ibn Abidin) affirms this ruling and adds further examples from Tajnis al-Nasiri.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (يا معفوج الخ) اسم مفعول من عفج بالعين المهملة والفاء والجيم. قال في التاتارخانية : وهو المضروب في الدبر وهو بمعنى ما فسره به المؤلف. وفي القاموس : عفج يعفج ضرب وجاريته جامعها قوله : (وقد صرح في الظهيرية بوجوب التعزير فيه) أي في قوله يا معفوج وقوله بل هو أقوى إيذاء أي لفظ بغا بمعنى المأبون . قلت : وقد رأيت في التتارخانية صرح يعزر به حيث قال : وفي تجنيس الناصري قال السيد الإمام الأجل : لو قال يا بغا يا مؤاجر يا جيفة في عرفنا فيه التعزير
His saying: '(O ma'fuj, etc.)' is a passive participle from 'afaja with 'ayn, fa', and jim. He said in al-Tatar Khaniyya: It is the one struck in the anus, and it is as the author interpreted. In al-Qamus: 'afaja ya'fiju means to strike, and he had intercourse with his slave girl. His saying: 'And he stated in al-Zahiriyya the obligation of ta'zir in it' i.e., in saying 'O ma'fuj', and his saying 'rather it is stronger injury' meaning the word 'bagha' with the meaning of 'ma'bun' (effeminate). I (the commentator) say: And I saw in al-Tatar Khaniyya he stated that he is ta'zir with it, where he said: In Tajnis al-Nasiri, al-Sayyid al-Imam al-Ajal said: if one says 'O bagha, O mu'ajir, O jifa' in our custom, there is ta'zir in it.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the term 'معفوج' (ma'fuj) and a restatement of its ruling. The commentator explains that 'معفوج' is a passive participle from the verb 'عفج', meaning someone who is struck in the anus, i.e., sodomized. This is a severe insult. The commentator confirms that al-Zahiriyya declares ta'zir wajib for this word, and that it is even stronger injury than other insults, similar to the word 'بغا' which means 'مأبون' (effeminate). The commentator also adds that in al-Tatar Khaniyya, the ruling of ta'zir is confirmed for terms like 'بغا', 'مؤاجر', and 'جيفة' (corpse) in their custom.

Key Terms
معفوج (ma'fuj): Passive participle from 'afaja: one struck in the anus, i.e., sodomized.
بغا (bagha): A term meaning 'ma'bun' (effeminate, passive homosexual).
مأبون (ma'bun): Effeminate man, passive partner in sodomy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the term 'معفوج' (ma'fuj) mean according to the text?
A) One who is struck in the anus, i.e., sodomized.
B) One who is weak and unable to work.
C) One who roams idly.
D) One who hires himself out.

Key Takeaway
The term 'ma'fuj' is a severe insult that obligates ta'zir in Hanafi law.
